UNDER THE
GUMTREE
J BRUIN TON STEPHENS
drops his initial, and
• becomes plain Brunton
Stephens in the new col-
lection of his verses published by
Angus and Robertson. A good
elderly portrait is prefixed ; but
the printing and paper of the
new book are much inferior to
the printing and paper of the
Convict Once and Other Poems
issued by George Robertson in
1888: indeed, the book is in
these respects the least creditable
of all which its present publishers
have issued in their series of Aus-
tralian authors. The backbone
of the new Poetical Works is the
George Robertson collection of
1888 ; but two pieces are dropped
—“King Billy’s Skull” and
“The Antipodes”; and there
are added fifteen pieces, of which
the most notable ate “ A Coin of Trajan in Aus-
tralia,” from The Australasian; “The Chamber of
Faith,” from The Antipodean ; and the recent piece
“Fulfilment,” written for the Federation of Aus-
tralia. Certain of the pieces included, such as the
“Johnsonian Address” and “ A Historical Pro-
blem,” have a very doubtful title to the honour.
For the rest, “ Convict Once ” is relegated to the
end of the book, instead of standing in the fore-
front ; and we pass first through a series of the
author’s moral and’didactic verses, which contain
doubtless his best" work in serious vein. Both
versions of the “Australian Anthem” are given
—the old and the new—one including a verse for
‘ ‘ the Motherland ” and the other referring to Aus-
tralia exclusively ; so that each political opinion
is catered for. One piece we are glad to see is
the song from Stephens’s operetta Fayette —
The new edition of Poetical Works seems to be
regarded as definitive ; and of late, indeed, the
author has added little to his store. Eor lovers
of Australian verse he will always have interest;
and his claims to be considered an Australian
classic are so great that one would like to see his
work clothed in better dress for preservation and
re-reading.
This criticism from St. James’s Budget oi Jan.
24 represents the real attitude of the typical
English mind to things characteristically Aus-
tralian ; the other kind of attitude is mostly
publishing puff or literary pretence—
Those who love the Australian bush, the “ Never-
Never Country ” tales of buck-jumpers, tramps, jacka-
roos, the damper, the billy, and the swag, will discover
congenial fare in Joe Wilson and His Mates, by Mr.
Henry Lawson (Blackwood). Those, and they are many,
who find such things a weariness to the flesh will do well
to avoid the book. Joe Wilson’s fortunes are related in
the first half of the volume, and dry and monoton-
ous as they are, they are pervaded by a certain
thread of human interest. The true, or ap-
parently true, story of a real man’s life can
never be completely unattractive. The remainder of the
book consists of a series of ordinary camp-fire yarns of
the time-honoured bush flavour. Like all anecdotes
hailing from the dismal Australian solitudes, one seems
to have read them before.
No matter what variations you may admit for
individuals or classes, every analysis of the British
character must begin and end with the statement
of the Latin primer—“ Britannia est insula.” And
the insular attitude is summed in: “’Ere’s a
strange bloke : ’eave ’arf a brick at ’im J”
The vehemently vocal N.S.W. Works Minister
O’Sullivan (which anagrammatises, by the way, as
I Vain Soul—with an excessive L ! that can be
taken as the audience’s exclamatory comment)
has turned to patronising literature in the intervals
of building bridges and naming dredges. A sketch
of the history of “The ‘Rocks’” —that unduly
familiar Sydney suburb —is the result. Roderic
Quinn writes it; and Govt, prints it and pays for
it—out of loan-money ? The sketch is interesting
and well-written (though you hear now and anon
the shriek of mandrake-words dragged by the roots
from the soil of usage); and the loan-money or
the revenue-money has been often worse spent.
J*
Barbara Baynton, who left Sydney for England
the other day with a book of literary sketches
designed for London publication, has written
several remarkably strong Australian stories,
nearly all unpublished. As a writer, she is a kind
of female Henry Lawson, with no less intensity,
and even a greater fidelity to detail. Her work
is uneven; but it holds rare poignancy and
power.
J»
“ We called him Bamum Powell,” says Philip
Pienaar in his book With Steyn and De Wet, pub-
lished by Methuen. This is a Boer version of
some of the incidents of the war, and forms a use-
ful corrective to undiscriminating pro-British
accounts. Of the motives which inspire the Boers
this perhaps is the best exposition :
I say nearly every man still fighting is as fervent a
Progressive as the world could wish, and a 3 much opposed
to Paul Kruger’s policy as the British themselves. Then
what are they fighting for ? you ask. For independence.
. . . And they will continue. Not because they are sure
of success, but because it is their duty.
Aubrey de Vere himself usually bore the expense
of publication of his books. That explains why he
was able to say, when earnings in literature were
discussed : “ I call no writer poor who can—as I
can—double his income at any moment.”
“ How ? ” he was asked. “ By laying down my
pen.”
King Edward has been asked by a petition of
sobolars to incorporate “The British Academy
for the Promotion of Historical, Philosophical,
and Philological Studies.” There is certainly
need of the Studies.
A correspondent—“B.”—
The danger you point out is no doubt real enough. But
does it matter ? Are the Australians worth the trouble
of saving—even if you could save them? The whole push
went crazy over the Boer war—refused to investigate or
to think, and revelled in ignorance and brutality. Im-
pulsive they are, of course, and that mightn’t matter
much; but impulsiveness joined with shallowness and
immense conceit is mortal disease. Talk about “ blind
hysterics of the Celt!” The Celt's nowhere. The out-
cry against the Barton tariff is another example. Tire
tariff may be a blessing or an unmixed evil; but these
pawing, wild-eyed blatherskites don’t know, and mostly
are incapable of finding out for themselves, the
real nature of what they are denouncing. The
occasional wave of republican feeling is just
as significant as the present torrent of im-
perialism. We can brag, we can help to deal it out to
the Boer, we can yell and be spiteful if we feel a hand at
our pockets, we can get drunk and blow tin trumpets in
honour of a bogus British hero, we can feel our bosoms
swell when Barton or a hero of melodrama gives us
rhetorical commonplaces; but we can’t be steady, we
can’t think calmly and consistently. We have no poise.
We live, like blackfellows, for the moment, We know
when we are hungry or thirsty for bread or blood. Immi-
gration is the only thing that will keep us from bora cere-
monies and corroborees. If we like the pleasures of
Imperialism, let us.have them. You can’t stop us, any-
way.
Nevertheless, Matthew Arnold said the last word,
the fine word—
They out-talk’d thee, hissed thee, tore thee?
Braver men fared thus before thee ;
Fired their ringing shot and pass’d,
Hotly charged—and sank at last.
Charge once more, then, and be dumb 1
Let the Victors, when they come,
When the forts of folly fall,
Find thy body by the wall.
Wherefore—no mean compromises, and Nil Des-
perandum !
“Cockaigne” writes —
Novelist F. W. Robinson died on Dec. Bth last. Known
especially as a writei’ of Three Deckers, the 3 vol. novels
which used to be sold at £1 11s, 6d., but have been
knocked on the head. Hardly anyone bought them
except the Circulating Libraries, and yet each of these
novels stood Robinson in about £5OO, and he polished off
two each year. When prices dropped to 65., he found it
did n’t pay. He then got less than £lOO for a novel. He
wrote 55.
Sudermann’s new play JEs Lebe das Leben!
(“ Live your life ! ”) was produced at the Deutsches
Theater in Berlin on Ist February. The central
idea is that life is only worth living as long as it
provides enjoyment for the senses. “The play,”
says a critic, “ is modern in every sense, and it is
significant that barely half-a-dozen of the audience
expressed any objection to its exaltation of
adultery.”
£
The same night saw Stephen Phillips’s play
Ulysses produced at Her Majesty’s in London.
“Weak stuff,” “a failure,” “wishy-washy senti-
mentality”—“as different as possible from the
strong, purging winds of Homer’’—these are some
commentaries.
J*
Perhaps the most attractive reproduction in
Part I of The Nation's Pictures (Cassell and Co.)
is that of Gx-eiffenhagen’s very sensuous “Idyll”
—two lovers ruddy in a field of red poppies, all
aflare with sanguine sunset. The other pictures
are Edwin Abbey’s “Mistress Mine,” Alfred
List’s “Autumn,” and Orchardson’s “Napoleon
on the Belle-rophon ” —all for ninepence. The
examples show what seven-league strides the
three-colour screen process ha 3 made—aided by
lithography, surely ?
“ The Evolution of the Direct Man ” (says The
Freethinker) is a most interesting and suggestive
Iiapter in Dr. Jordan’s (Eng.) recent book, The
Toral Nerve. The average brain (he says) is in-
irect; a brain of indirect methods, hazy ideas,
id timid conclusions. The direct brain—critical,
>sting, inquiring, discriminative, and firm—is
radually coming into existence. And it leads to
dmness instead of feverish life. Health is better
ow than it used to be; life is longer, bodily
;ature is greater. Science, under the appearance
E complexity, tends to the simplification of exist-
ace. “The future,” as Dr. Jordan reads it,
gives ample promise of a simpler life, a life too—
lough much will depend on individual nerve-
rganisation—of slighter labour, longer repose,
nd quieter meditation.”

Nobody bothers about Zangwill now; but it is
interesting to note how the Jew’s business spirit
has-come out in him. He is heart and soul) pen
and speech, with the Zionist movement which
wants to send the world-scattered Jews back as a
nation to Judee. And indeed, although Marcus
Clarke is dead, somebody might be found to give
Zangwill a list of a few Jews whom Australia
could very well spare.

Contributor “ X” —
As to Alfred Harinsworth’s opinion of Furniss’s /vi/ra
Joka (25/1/2), surely Alfred must have been “kidding”
Furniss. Lika Joka’s failure was prophesied because
there wasn’t enough for the money (3d.)- Furniss
wished to run against Punch, also 3d.,' but the Punch
people had half-a-dozen star illustrators to Lika Jolca’s
one—Furniss. Logically, you would expect Harmsworth
to be one of the last to say that too much was given for
the money : he himself goes on the principle of giving
good, bouncing penn’orths, and finds it pays ; but none
of his magas. are one-man shows.
Why doth the dawning speak of her?
What kindred hath she with the morn ?
Why should my wakening thoughts transfer
To her the glow in Orient born ?
I only know that wheresoe’er
Awaketh Beauty, she is there. ,
Why is the noontide full of her?
What kindred hath she with the sun?
Why should the whole world’s minister
Be eloquent alone of one?
I only know that wheresoe’er
Exulteth Beauty, she is there. v
Why is the midnight fraught with her?
What kindred hath she with the night?
0 dreams that are of things that were,
Ye change my darkness into light!,
I know, I know that wheresoe’er
Reposeth Beauty, she is there.
Henry Lawson’s collection of sketches, Joe
Wilson and- His Mates , is praised by one of
Lond. Athenceum’s reviewers as the best work he
has yet produced ; but Lawson’s Australian ad-
mirers will find the book rather disappointing. It
is a pleasant, rambling mixture of observation and
reminiscence; but as a whole it is decidedly
flatter and less interesting than his earlier
books. When there are three or four pages
of really dramatic writing, as in “The Babies
in the Bush,” they are so smothered with unim-
portant detail that the effect is to a great extent
missed. Lawson appears to have been writing
with an eye to filling Eng. magazine space ; and
for Australian readers much of his “local colour”
is unnecessary. The.explanation of gold-washing
at p. 167, for example, is well-meant for the
English, who know nothing about gold-washing ;
but }n Australia we desire to take that for
granted, and get on with the story. The use of
English stereo-plates apparently accounts for
such eccentricities of spelling as “ Kendel ” and
“Gob” and Co. in the local edition. Two pieces
of verse included in the volume are not in
Lawson’s best style ; but the first, addressed “ to
the chaps who barracked for ME,” is eminently
■ characteristic.
SEC RET A SI LVARUM.
Frau Soldo ham. aus dem Berg hergor. —Shepherd’s
Song in ‘Tannhauser’,
Where as, when Holda leaves her hill
of winter, on the quest of JUne,
black oaks with emerald lamplets thrill
that flicker forth to her magic tune,
at dawn the forest shivers whist
and all its hidden glades awake
under the thinning milk-white mist,
and the sun-quickened branches make
along its edge a woven sound
of legends that allure and flit
on wound horns towards the enchanted ground
where, in the light moon-vapours lit
all night, while the black woods in mass,
serried, forbid with goblin fear,
fay-revels gleam o’er the pale grass
till shrill-throats chirp the matin near.
0 then, O then in the sweet of the year
adventurous in the witching green,
last feal of the errant spear,
to seek the eyes of lost Undine
clear blue above the blue cold stream
that lingers till her plaint be done,
01 and perchance from that sad dream
to woo her, laughing, to the sun
and that glad blue that seems to flow
far up, where dipping branches shift
sidelong their soft throng’d frondage slow
and slow the thin cloud-fleeeelets drift.
O there to drowse the summer thro’
deep in some odorous twilit lair,
swoon’d in delight o'f golden dew
within the sylvan witch’s hair:
the while on halt-shut lids to feel
the yellow sunshafts broken dim,
and seldom waftures moth-like steal
and settle on the bare-flung limb ;
or under royal autumn, pall’d
with smouldering magnificence,
to feel the olden heart enthrall’d
in wisdoms of forgotten sense,
and wild desire and pain, that fill’d
red August’s heart of throbbing bloom,
in one grave hour of knowledge still’d
where glory ponders o’er its doom :
and when the boughs are iron lace,
and silence chisels silver rime,
by some old hearth, with half-lit face,
to dream the vanished forest prime,
the spring tide’s sweet and June’s delight,
more precious, now that hard winds chill
the dews that made their mornings bright,
and Holda sleeps beneath her hill.
N.S.W.
C.B.

PUBLIC NOTICE!
WHEREAS it has been ascertained that certain unscrupulous persons, aware
of the unprecedented success which has resulted from the placing on the market the
now Celebrated “Can’t B Beat ” Shilling Pipes, Branded Thus :
are selling certain Pipes to the Public as such, but which are infinitely
inferior, and are NOT the Genuine C. 8.8. Pipes, as supplied to the Trade and to
the Public at the various Establishments of Mick Simmons.
Notice is hereby given that as ignorance cannot in the future be accepted as an
excuse, all persons infringing our Rights as Proprietors of the above Brand, which
is an abbreviation of our well-known Trade Mark, “Can’t B Beat,” will be
prosecuted under the Trade Marks Act, and be Proceeded Against in Equity for an
Injunction.
GBB
/cbK
Trade Mark registered in Australasia and
New Zealand.
The Public are requested to see that the above Brand is stamped upon every Pipe
purchased, and they are particularly Cautioned against deception in this respect.
The Public are further requested to at once make known to the undersigned any
attempt to pass off either unbranded Pipes or Pipes with any Brand other than the
above, for C B B.
MICK SIMMONS, Haymarket.

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE.
The health of women ie of supreme im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
BAFE CURE —a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation—
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.

About seven years ago I contracted a severe

“Youth Quickly Plies!”
Ay, and we stay not to consider how speedily
until it has forever fled. _ Youth is to man or
woman what springtime is to the year. Every-
thing is new, delightful, and full of promise.
There are no regrets for the past, for there is no
past—everything lies in the golden future. The
conscience is clear, the heart light, the spirit
joyous, the digestion sound, the step elastic. Happy
youth cares not a snap of the finger about the
increase in the income-tax, the rate of exchange,
the state of trade, the changes pending in the
political map, or any such dry-as-dust matters;
it is too busy being happy. Ah well! this is as
it always was, and doubtless ever will be. We
shall none of us ever be younger than we are
to-day. These considerations make it doubly
sad when youth is debarred by ill-health from
the pleasures which rightly belong to it. Let us
listen to what Miss Emma Lillian Fisher, of 373,
Bourke-street, Darlinghurst, N.S.W., has to tell
us. Writing on 26th June, 1901, Miss Fisher says:
“In February, 1896, when residing in Chapman-
street, Surry Hills, I had the misfortune to
accidentally fall from a window into a yard below,
a distance of 20ft. My mother rushed into
the yard expecting to find me very seriously
injured, but to her great satisfaction I had
escaped with no more serious injury than a few
contusions. Unfortunately, though no bones were
broken, the shock to the system was so severe
that a breakdown of my hitherto robust health
ensued. I lost my appetite, grew listless, weak,
pale and nervous, afraid to venture out alone
because of a dizziness which at times came over
me. I was attended by one of the leading doctors ;
but my case seemed to baffle his skill, for I
made no advance towards recovery. He said I
was suffering from anaemia and nervous pros-
tration, the result of shock ; but be that as it
may, he only succeeded in naming my complaints.
He could not relieve him. My brother Harry
had for a long time endeavoured to persuade
me to try a course of Seigel’s Syrup. At first
he measured out the doses and stood over me
while I swallowed them. But compulsion soon
became unnecessary. Each dose seemed to infuse
new health into me. My worst symptoms—
headache, heartburn, palpitations, and dizziness —
were speedily put to flight. My appetite revived,
and the cheerfulness natural to my disposition
returned. I continued to take Seigel’s Syrup
for several months, by which time my health was
thoroughly re-established.”
Miss Fisher is a vivacious young lady of twenty-
two, and her appearance has nothing which
suggests her painful experiences.
Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lonely Track.
They were firm old friends in the days gone past—
Kind hearts of the olden school;
But the will grows weak when the drought holds
fast,
And each of them played the fool.
’Twas a brutal word, and the word returned,
An oath, and an angry blow,
A fiery passion that fiercely burned,
And
“Connors went out three days ago—
Went out on the Lonely Track,
Where the tanks are dry and the grass won’t grow—•
Oh, God ! but I thought he was sure to know !
Come back, old Connors, come back ! ”
Yet Connors had paused when his swag was made
And his dog stood ready to start,
And a nervous twitch on his thin lips played,
From the pain of his aching heart,
And with outstretched hand he had said “ Good-
bye,”
As one who is in disgrace,
But the other had risen with flashing eye,
And spat an oath in his face.
“ Oh, Connors went out three days agone—
Went out on the Lonely Track !
The scalding tears in his dim eyes shone ;
I watched them fall as he faced the sun ;
Come back ! old Connors, come back ! ”
Night came and the day and the day again,
But Connors came there no more ;
Only a kelpie that whined with pain
Crawled up to the humpy door ;
And the other made oath as he journeyed out,
“By God, I will fetch him back ! ”
But he followed a trail that was laid in doubt,
For he trod on the Lonely Track.
There is horror now in the Far-out West,
Where the brown, bark humpy stands,
For the mice, unheeded, have built a nest
Where the clock holds death in its hands ;
And out of the corners, where darkness lies
With the dust of the days gone past,
There comes the glitter of impish eyes
That grip you and hold you fast.
And the birds keep clear of the open eaves
Where the wind makes endless moan,
For the rocking roof like a weird thing grieves,
And the saplings strain and groan ;
And under the bunk where Connors slept
A bat in the day-time clings—
And every inch of the floor is swept
By creeping and unclean things.
And just outside, by a fallen log
Where Connors was wont to rest,
The bleaching bones of a faithful dog
Grow white—with the skull turned west;
And over the whole is an eerie sense
Of something that nameless walks
When the spirit of Loneliness vague, intense,
With Death, as a demon, stalks.
For Connors went out long, long ago—
Went out on the Lonely Track.
And beyond the rise, where the sky hangs low,
God heard the caw of a sated crow—
“ Come back ! Old Connors, come back ! ”
Y.
MILKY WHITE.
The Farm-Laborers.
I got up earlier than the sun this morn
And turned in ecstasy to eastern skies
Where lifting clouds looked into paradise ;
But ere one hymn of praise was heav’nward borne
A band of workers passed, some old and worn,
Some in life’s prime, and some with infant-eyes
(Ah ! who will listen to the baby-cries,
Cutting with anguish in the fields of corn ?)
And as they passed, far out at Heaven’s brink,
God’s eyes threw shadows of them from the east
That ribbed the earth with restless ghosts of
wrong !
Ah ! ye who at the founts of Mammon drink—
And at the altar place of Moloch feast—
You slumber in these shadows. But how tony ?
VIC.
M.W.
Jabez Balfour, the Liberator Building
Society swindler, is said to be in a dying state in
Parkhurst Prison (Eng.).
If Admiral Charles Beresford is to be
believed, a naval war would find as many holes in
the British navy as the African campaign did in the
British army. Beresford states that coal stores at
important stations are so inadequate that'a fleet
might be unable to move for want of fuel, that the
naval reserve numbers only 19,000 instead of 70,000
men, and that the naval gunners don’t know how
to shoot. Still the chances are that other fleets
and armies are in the same condition, which
would equalise things. The war of 1858 found
both French and Austrian armies in a state of
chaos. The Austrian army was only a little less
chaotic in’66. The French army in 1870 proved
to be a mere mob. The Russian Army in ’7B was
found to be a terribly disconnected machine, and the
Turkish one was worse. The difference between
the Spanish and American fleets in the recent war
was that the condition of the Spanish fleet was
tenth-rate and that of the U.S. fleet third-rate.
The only leading nation which hasn’t been badiv
surprised at the state of its forces some time
during the last 50 years is Prussia.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the •‘Correspondence” column aU contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceed-
ing two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are speci-
ally acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper
clippings. All communications will be regarded ■as
strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand, that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 29, 1902.
Keep On!
Courage yet, my brother or my sister !
Keep on—Liberty is to be subserv’d whatever occurs ;
That is nothing that is quelled by one or two failures, or
any number of failures,
Or by the indlfEerence or ingratitude of the people, or by
any unfaithfulness,
Or the show of the tushes of powers, soldiers, cannon,
penal statutes.
What we believe in waits latent forever through all the
continents,
Invites no one, promises nothing 1 , sits in calmness ana
light, is positive and composed, knows no discourage-
ment,
Waiting patiently, waiting its time.
Wai.t Whitman.
» . •
The Australian Nationalist to-day needs,
more than all else, courage. The times are
out of joint to those who believe in Liberty.
On every side looms some cause for dis-
couragement. Hardly- perceivable is the
glimmer of hope ahead. Federation, which
promised to stimulate and re-create National
sentiment, to cast in an indelible stamp a
new nation, has so far shown a foiled
purpose. Militarism rages with reckless
bloodthirstiness. Liberty of platform and
press is seriously encroached upon. The
Constitution is twisted back towards the old-
time fetishes of king-power and priest-
power. Those in authority bow the knee to
Rimmon and abandon the democratic faith.
Those from whom authority proceeds are
careless of their rights, indifferent to their
liberties, ungrateful to their patriots.
*
But courage yet ! If the idea, the aspira-
tion of a free Australian People, is to be
quelled by its first serious trouble, die out
and fail on meeting its first struggle, then
that idea, that aspiration is nothing—was
from the first nothing. And that ic is impos-
sible to believe. The sentiment which showed
itself at Eureka, which was strong enough to
shake off the shackles of convictism from the
land, which sent back from the shores of a
White Australia the Asiatic taint in the face
of Imperial opposition, was not a weak
impulse, such as could be strangled by the
treachery of one man, or of many men, or
bv the indifference of one generation. That
spirit of Liberty which raised Australia
from the charnel-house of convictism, which
so long kept her shores clean for a white
race, is not one doomed to die by the first
great unfaithfulness of its appointed guar-
dians. It will in time re-assert itself in the
people and in their leaders ; and Australia
resume a purpose which has been not frus-
trated—merely delayed.
■» * * *
Many blame Federation for the growth in
Australia of militarism, of King power, of
Conservative tyranny. But it is a matter of
coincidence, rather than of cause and effect.
Through a long series of years the bestowal
of English titles and the upgrowth of a local
snobbish plutocracy had been sapping at the
foundations of Australian’s independent
spirit. The men who had stood on Circular
Quay to resist, by force if necessary, the
landing of English convicts, or who auda-
ciously raised the flag of Freedom at Eureka
rather than submit to tyranny and oppres-
sion, were succeeded by others, who spurned
the crosa which the patriot must always bear,
and aspired to the gilded emblems of English
Royal favor. The race of sturdy miners,
farmers, bushmen, was leavened with an
element of financiers —mainly of foreign
origin—princes in the shoddy trade, lords of
usury, barons of beer. Their money, united
with the royal gauds of England to corrupt our
public men, and Parliament, more and more
each year reflected, not the aspirations of
Australia, but the huckstering, truckling
spirit of the parasites on Australia.
This spirit did not begin, but .only
came to a head, with Federation.
Without the union it would just
as surely, if somewhat less quickly, have
grown to maturity, and there would have
been then six Jingo-Imperialistic plutocracies
for Liberty to combat instead of one.
Federation has given to the evil one neck,
and thus made it the more easily destructible.
At the same time, it has given to Australian
Nationalism one aim, one hope, Before the
union the national aspiration was hobbled in
one State by the slower progress of another.
Now all can move together, and when
Liberty “latent through all the continent”
awakes to its task, it can move forward with
a united irresistible people to its clearly-
indicated goal.
« » * *
Federation, in reality, did nothing to assist
the Jingoistic and slavish spirit in Aus-
tralian public life—only by concentrating
and centralising made it more obvious. It
was, as a matter of fact, the S’African war
which forced to quick maturity a growth
which had long been sedulously fostered by
English Tory influence. The outbreak of
that war marked a crisis in Australian public
life. The Boers, an independent, civilised,
but small people, had flaunted English
aggression as the Victorian miners had done
at Eureka, as the State of New South Wales
had done rvhen it turned back from Circular
Quay an English convict transport. The true
issue, however, did not come clearly before
the Australian people. Their leaders de-
ceived them, newspapers deceived them ; and,
instead of declaring for Liberty, throwing in
the scale the weight of influence of free
colonies on behalf of Freedom and Right, they
declared for the bloody suppression of claims
for which their own struggles should Have
taught them full sympathy. That was the
great false step, from which we have marched
on to militarism, suppression of free speech,
and the revival of the royal “divine-right ”
fetish, the general growth of Conservative
tyranny, which makes Australia to-day more
closely resemble England in the times of the
Tudors than a free Commonwealth in the
20th century. It was a sinister mistake,
the adoption of a Jingo policy by Australia,
pregnant with mischief for the hereafter, but
not an irrevocable abandonment of our
Freedom.
* * * #
To-day is the day of Judas and of
Jingoism. It may be a long, long day, and
its setting be dark and troublous. But most
surely there will be a morrow of Loyalty—
loyalty to Australia—and of Liberty. The
failure to raise even a noteworthy protest
against the sacrifice of our manhood in
an unholy cause notwithstanding—the
failure to rouse the people against encroach-
ments of the kingly power and of military
insolence notwithstanding—the ingratitude
of the people to those who strive to show
them the right path notwithstanding—
Liberty waits patiently, and will, in time,
justify her advocates. To the Remnant then
of Australian freemen—the men who dare to
brave persecution for right’s sake—Keep
on ! Keep alive the sacred embers. Fight
bravely, earnestly, knowing no discourage-
ment, waiting patiently for the inevitable
turn of the tide towards Freedom.
The Unemployed.
His Ex. the Governor-General accidentally ran
against a deputation of unemployed in Mel-
bourne, one day last week, and offered it
some sympathy and certain very dead old
platitudes. The deputation had come to in-
dignantly reproach both the State and Federal
Governments for not borrowing a lot of money
and starting some unprofitable public work or
other to find them employment and keep them in
wages. In this respect it is felt that Premier
Peacock hasn’t done his duty. The N.S.W.
Government has borrowed £6,000,000 or £7,000,000
within the last 12 months with which to find work
for the people and keep the unemployed spectre
off the doorstep—a sum sufficient for the main-
tenance of about 70,000 men and their families,
or, say, one-quarter of the population of the
State. The Victorian Government has borrowed
only about half-a-million in the same period.
And it being an understood thing that Australia
can’t be expected to find sustenance even for its
present handful of people out of its alleged
“magnificent natural resources,” and that they
must live to a great extent upon State charity
(thinly disguised as public works and paid for
with foreign loans), the community naturally feels
aggrieved with Premier Peacock. And Peacock
replies that this is not a good time for borrowing,
and that the State is tottering under the weight
of its present debt without incurring any more.
Also, he can’t be expected to do public works
without loans for three reasons : (1) It would be
a shocking violation of all precedent to do any
public work without borrowing the money for it ;
(2) The present revenue, being largely used up m
paying interest on existing debts (incurred to a
great extent to postpone the unemployed
problem in previous years) doesn’t leave any
margin for public works ; (3) That, though such
people as Englishmen, Dutchmen, Frenchmen, and
other ignominious small fry pay far heavier taxa-
tion than we do, yet this country of “ unexampled
natural resources ” can’t possibly do likewise and
thus have a margin for public works expenditure.
So there is a deadlock.
* * * *
The unemployed deputation which laid its woes
before the Governor-General didn’t put things
exactly like this, but it asked him, as a man and a
brother, if it wasn’t a shocking thing that Premier
Peacock didn’t float a big loan in order to start
public works on a large scale and postpone the
difficulty for another winter anyhow. And His
Ex. remarked that he was surprised to find men
unemployed in a new country like Australia, and a
country of such great natural resources, and dished
up the good old platitudes in a kindly spirit.
There is a vague and shapeless but apparently in-
curable idea that the newness of a_ country is in
itself an edible substance, and that in a new coun-
try there are more coppers in a shilling than
there are in an old one. And Australia has been
so long and so persistently described as the Land
of the Golden Fleece, and other like expressions,
that the theory of its amazing natural wealth has
become a permanent superstition. The public
has become so dead sure about that amazing
natural wealth that it has borrowed about
£200,000,000 on the security of it, and even the
spectacle of about one-sixth of the population
living directly or indirectly upon loans hasn’t
convinced it that either there is something very
far wrong with the alleged wealth or else with the
management of it. This country has even annexed
the Southern Cross as an asset, as if tha,t were an
exclusively Australian property, and it isn’t at all
certain that it doesn’t regard that staggery con-
stellation also as being, in some sense, a security
for its debts.
* • * *
The only natural resources, using the word in
the Foreign-traders’ sense, that can be perman-
ently relied upon are pasture and agriculture.
Gold is only a temporary asset. ' And Australia,
remarkable as it may seem in view of the Golden
Fleece legend, has always been a comparative
failure, both agriculturally and pastorally. Aus-
tralia’s flocks and herds have been decreasing
lamentably during late years, but even in its best
days the State with the biggest flocks and herds
hardly kept ahead of the United Kingdom—a
mere speck on the map, with its surface mostly
occupied by towns, cities, or agricultural land. In
1896 the record of Australia’s comparative failure
as a Land of the Golden Fleece and of other sun-
dries, compared with some other countries in this
manner:—
Area Live Stock (Sheep,
Square Cattle, Horses,
Miles. and Swine.)
United States.. ..'2,939,000 J 40,500,000
Russia in Europe .. 2,095,600 108,200,000
Argentina .. .. 622,969 107,800,000
New South Wales .. 310,700 51,270,000
United Kingdom .. 120,979 47,320,000
Germany .. • • 208,830 47,200,000
France.. .. 204,090 43,930,000
Austria.. •• .. 240,000 41,300,000
Queensland .. .. 668,497 26,650,000
Spain 197,670 21,400,000
Victoria .. .. 87,884 15,780,000
South Australia .. 903,690 6,840,000
Westralia .. .. 975,920 2,500,000
Tasmania .. .. 26,'215 1,840,000
* * * *
The Commonwealth shows up, on the whole,
very moderately. It is larger than the United
States, and 900,000 square miles larger than
European Russia, and its total flocks and herds
showed up in 1896 to less than 105,000,000 —they
are now down to about 80,000,000. Therefore, in
a pastoral sense, it produced very much worse
results than either of these countries, and this
despite the fact that Australia is mainly pastoral,
while Russia and the United States are very
largely agricultural, and only pastoral in a secon-
dary sense. New South Wales is the great animal-
raising State bf Australia—the Land of the Golden
Fleece par excellence. Yet even it, in proportion
to area, makes a worse showing than Argentina,
Great Britain, Germany, France, or Austria,
and also worse than many other coun-
tries not included in the above list—all
old crowded lands, where a little pastoral
business is done in an . accidental sort of way.
Queensland, in proportion to area, showed up,
in 1896, worse than any old crowded foreign State
on the list, and yet in 1896 Queensland was nearlfj
at the summit of its prosperity. It never even
approached the United Kingdom,- Austria, Franee';
or Germany in a pastoral sense. Yet among;
what the Foreign-traders love to call Australia’s
natural industries the pastoral one is undoubtedly
the chief. Agriculture is nowhere, or thereabouts.
All the Commonwealth last year only grew about
as much wheat in its area of 2,972,000 square miles
as Roumania —a place of 50,000 square miles and
with not very much more than the Common-
wealth’s population.
• * * *
Things like these cast a gloom over the Foreign-
trader’s theory about this country’s great and per-
manent natural resources. There is no reason
why Australia should not become a great manu-
facturing country, but as an agricultural country
it appears to be a very bad failure, and as regards
its great pastoral industry it is a very moderate
success indeed. The Land of the Golden Fleecfe
is mostly bunkum, and very bad bunkum at that.
Unless Australia can do very much better in the
sheep and bullock sense than it did in its best days
—the days before the drought and the tick and
the worst of the rabbit plague- -its population
doesn’t require to increase so very much before it
reaches the point where the country will only
about supply itself in this line of industry. It is
because of the hopelessness of the attempt to make
a great and permanently prosperous pastoral and
agricultural region of a continent with only two
big rivers, and those rivers which can be walked
across a great part of the time, that The Bulle hx
believes in Protection—far more Protection than
even Victoria ever had. When the gold gives out
and the loans give out, going on the land is a poor
thing to trust to.
« • * •
Manufactures stimulated into existence by pro-
hibitive tariffs and bonuses and all manner of
“coddling” may be unnatural and unprofitable
industries, as the Foreign-trade party asserts, but
there is great profit in unnatural industries, and
almost all industries are unnatural. Even the pas-
toral and agricultural business started unnaturally
with the introduction of alien seeds and strange
foreign animals. If Australia put absolute pro-
hibitive duties on some articles, of which it now
imports £5,(100,000 per annum, it would naturally
proceed to manufacture these articles, and, even if
they cost £8,000,000 to manufacture, Australia
would still-be £5,000,000 —or more than £5,000,000
—better off, in this fashion :
(1) lb wouldn’t need to withdraw a single man from
pastoral or agricultural pursuits. The new industry could
be carried on by the men who are now clamoring for
relief Works |o keep them from starvation. Therefore the
production Would be wholly extra production.
(2) The country would be saved the cost of supporting
these unemployed—it has to support them somehow, or
else pay the cost of burying them.
(3) There would be £5,000,000 a vear less going out of
the country, therefore the country would have £5,000,000
a year more with which to pay off its foreign obligations.
Even if the locally-manufactured goods cost twice as much
as the imported, the £5,000,000 which used to go out o
the country would still be £5,000,000 saved.
(4) Australia has to use up its waste population some-
how. If it starts what are called “ unprofitable indus-
tries” under Protection it absorbs them, and it at the
same time checks the outflow of money which is draining
this country and driving the Government into the con-
stant flotation of foreign loans to prevent Australia
getting down to a condition in which it will have no
money at all. If it absorbs them by unprofitable
charities and soup-kitchens or by unprofitable public
works then it costs the community a lot of money and
doesn’t diminish the drain on the country’s resources.
* * * *
If Australia had profitable employment for all
its people then Protection would be a foolish idea.
It is no use to take people away from industries
that pay well in order to start industries that may
pay worse. But there is a large section of the
population for which there is no room in the
existing manufacturing industries, and who can't
be employed at growing things which won’t grow
for want of rain, or rearing animals that are dead
from drought. Australia must either find these
people a chance to produce something by starting
new industries, even if they can’t produce so
cheaply as the foreigner, or else support them
while they produce nothing. In the former case
the country is richer by the value of the thing
produced, no matter what it costs. If a pair of
boots can be imported at 45., and a man for whom
no other employment can be found can make the
same pair at Bs., it is cheaper to let him make
them—there is 4s. more wealth in the country
through him doing so, because the 4s. hasn’t gone
abroad to pay for the foreign boots. It is cheaper
to pay him 4s. extra for the boots than 4s. for
sand-shifting, or other useless “relief work” —
exactly 4s. cheaper—the 4s. which would other-
wise go to Germany to pay for the imported
boots. Every man has a right to protest against
establishing unprofitable industries (such as
the making for Bs. of a pair of boots
which could be imported for 45.) if he can
find profitable industries to employ the surplus
population. If he can’t, then he has no case worth
mentioning. So long as a man is employed at
making something useful, even if he makes it
badly and expensively, it pays better than not
having him employed at all. It would even pay
better for a man or a country to make, at a cost of
10s. worth of labor, an article that could be
imported for 6d. than for that man to be unem-
ployed and not make anything—exactly fid. better,
and when the unemployed are with us sixpences
of that kind accumulate.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Lost Ideal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Is it ever the fate of the worker to wait through
the crafty years
That sap his strength, till faith at length is choked
in fears,
And the head is grey, and the heart astray, in a
mist of tears ?
Has Providence ruled that men shall lie fooled by
the cunning few ?
That Labor shall never the shackles dissever of
statesman and Jew ?
That Labor slir.il never through constant endeavo?
attain to the true ?
Searching for light through the terrible night ol
starless skies,
Our forefathers groped arid stumbled—yet hoped
their children’s eyes
Might some day gaze on the first low rays of the
far sunrise.
But our hearts have turned from the faith we
learned at our father’s knee,
When he slowly spoke through his evening smoke
of Liberty—
When his heart was strong to battle with wrong
/ that we might be free.
His sacred trust we tread in the dust at the beat
of a drum ! _
We flaunt our rags under fluttering flags where
the soldiers come ;
We prattle the story of bloodshed and “glory”
in alley and slum.
Our banners and trollies and trophies were follies
we’ve flung away ;
The buttons and braid of the bloody trade are
ours to-day—
The tools of Labor are rifle and sabre ; its motto,
“To Slay.”
Hurrah ! for a trip in a transport ship, whatever
we’re paid,
And the horrors of war in a land without law save
loot and raid,
And the smouldering farm—and scream of alarm,
with no one to aid.
A yell and a screech for freedom of speech, and a
drunken cheer,
For the Empire’s name, and her glorious shame,
lest we should hear
Through the fume of lies our children’s cries—and
awake to fear.
PEDLAR.


The High Commissionership.
Perth Herald states :
It is now evident that the post of High Commissioner
in London for the Commonwealth is to be given to Sir
John Forrest. The information that he is to be given
the post comes not only from the newspapers in the
eastern States, but also from members of Parliament.
The Herald is a bit previous ; all that is evident
is that Bigjohn Forrest is barracking very hard
for the billet and using all sorts of means to better
his chances. The lazy shiftlessness of the Premier
makes Forrest’s appointment just possible, but
there should be sufficient independence in other
members of the Cabinet to prevent such an out-
rage on Australian sentiment. It is not Australia
of the lag-time, but the Australia of to-day, which
we want to see represented in London. John
Forrest is personally a bluff, good-natured sort
of person, with a fine capacity for getting things
into a mess; but his mental attainments, as well as
his political views, unfit him absolutely for the posi-
tion of Australian representative in London. Social
ambition is the only motive which urges him to be
a (Candidate for the post; and his success would put
Australian interests in the hands of a stout person
with an eager desire to hunt for a bigger title, and
neither the wish nor the capacity to do anything
else. And those interests are by no means unim-
portant. A man is wanted whose intellectual
qualifications will command respect, and will
enable him to stand up against any “bluff ” on
the part of English authorities. Points of differ-
ence will inevitably arise in the near future, with
regard to the Pacific Islands, alien immigration,
the Northern Territory, naval control, and so on,
when Australia will want one thing and England
another. The controversy will always begin by
the declaration on the part of an English
Minister that such and such a thing is “im-
possible,” and it will always end there, if
Australia is represented by an amiable nin-
compoop with the keen desire for a bigger
title. If, on the other hand, our High Com-
missioner is a man of political ability, and of
some diplomatic training, and with the advocate’s
habit of fighting out his case anyhow, it would be
very often found that “impossible” doesn’t mean
much in negotiations between nations. There is
only one name which occurs to us as that
of a man who, by training, by ability, by life
habits, is marked out for the post, and that
man is B. R. Wise. From a Bulletin point
of view, Mr. Wise wouldn’t make an ideal High
Commissioner, because his standpoint would not
be sufficiently Australian, but undoubtedly
he is by far the most able man available, and
probably has as much of the Australian National
spirit as would be permitted in any nominee of
the present Government. Whilst he is available
(and it is generally believed that he would accept
an offer of the post) the discussion of any other
name is a serious reflection on either the honesty
or the mental capacity of the Barton Cabinet.
4
The Proposed Sydney Protectionist
Daily Paper.
The promotion of the mooted new daily paper in
Sydney seems to be mostly in the hands of Mr.
William John Lyne and Mr. Edmund Barton.
Reports of Melbourne progress come linked with
the name of the former as the ’bus-boss Carey of
the new concern; of Sydney progress with thename
of the latter. There are also more Or less definite
rumors that Mr. Austin Chapman is a leading
light in the venture, and that various aldermen
are “getting together a staff,” and : generally
advancing its interests. The Bulletin—
earnestly desirous of seeing established a new
daily paper in Sydney, with an independent
cable-service to Europe, a habit of telling
at least some of the truth in regard to
local politics, and a National policy—looks with
a gloomy eye on all these preparations. A
National daily paper will never be founded by a
mob of politicians, appealing for their capital to
manufacturers and others with a business axe to
grind. Such people may establish a newspaper—
may even, though that is extremely improbable,
establish acommercially-successful newspaper—-but
they will never, never, never build up a National
journal, truthful, honest and independent. It is
against the nature of . things. An honest news-
paper must be the fearless critic of the politicians,
the hated enemy of nine-tenths of them—not their
tool. If, as a matter of business, people care to
risk their money in a paper run by politicians for
politicians, that is their own business, and at
least they will give a few hard-up newspaper
men jobs for a while. But let no one put
cash into such a concern under the idea
that thereby they will better the community,
advance the National Cause, purify public life;
for such a delusion would have a sorrowful end-
ing. The grudge which Mr. Barton and Mr.
Lyne have against the present Sydney morning
papers is well-founded enough ; for at present
these papers consistently misrepresent both on
the fiscal question (though willing enough to
barrack for them in matters of Jingoism). But a
paper promoted by Mr. Barton and Mr. Lyne,
whilst undoubtedly it would please these gentle-
men, would do no better service to the people
than existing organs. It is a People’s newspaper—
not a politicians’newspaper—that is required; and
for ourselves we are astonished that Mr. Bill
Lyne is so anxious for a new paper when he has
shown himself so able in “influencing” an exist-
ing one.

The Religions of N.S. Wales.
Mr. T. A. Coghlan is the first of the Australian
statists to publish facts gleaned from the census
of 1901 as to the religions of the people. His very
detailed return shows that there are something
more than 144 Christian sects growing on the
Cornstalk’s lone and dusty patch of the earth ;
144 are detailed, right down to the sect which
describes itself as “Pleading for the Return of
the New Testament, Christian in Faith and Prac-
tice,” with just one adherent; then other churches,
of less importance than that, are summed up as
“other Christians not classifiable.” This con-
temptuous grouping is possibly due to the fact
that they have only a fraction of an adherent,
or, mayhap, to the. consideration that some
church with one adherent persists in describing
itself by a title running into 2200 words. Churches
with only one adherent have a failing in that
direction. But there are very many sects with a
congregation of one which find mention ; for in-
stance: “Saint” and “Believer in God spelt
Good.” “Christian, Acts XI., 26” is a church
with two members, and “Assembly of God” has
three. Dealing with the larger sects, one joyful
fact recorded is that the Salvation Army—that
hideous combination of soulless sweating, dis-
gusting cant and discordant noise—is a dwindling
pest. In 1891 it had 10,315 adherents, In 1901,
with a largely-increased population, it has only
9,585. Ten years ago its adherents formed 0.92 per
cent, of the community, while they now form only
0.71. The Church of England boasts the largest
numerical increase for the decennium, having
623,131 professed adherents in 1901, as against
503,054 in 1891; the percentage of its ad'.iere.ity
to the total population was 44 '75 per cent, in 1891,
and 45 '99 per cent, in 1901. There is no doubt of
the growing popularity of Anglicanism in Aus-
tralia. The Church of England makes no
awkward demands on its adherents; it is suffi-
ciently broad to keep within its fold even those
who don’t believe in anything; and it has a solid
air of Respectability which leads the man who is
really an Anythingarian to describe himself as an
Anglican. In snort, it is the refuge for
the man who is too tired to have any par-
ticular religion, and being too tired even to
give an explanation to that effect he passes as
an Anglican. Roman Catholics show a nume-
rical increase, and also a very slight proportional
increase, the first for three decenniums. In 1871
R.C.’s made up 29'29 of the population ; in 1881
the proportion had fallen to 27'63, and in 1891 to
25'53; now, in 1901, it is 25'63. Hebrews keep
the same percentage (0'48) as in 1871, and number
altogether 6447. Indefinite religions total among
them 128 followers, and include such items as Ark
of the Covenant, 1; Canopist, 2; Secret Indepen-
dent Believer, 1; Celtic, 1 (surely not E.W.O’S. !).
There is no such thing in the State as a Calli-
thumpian, but there are 11 Mormons (7 males
and 4 females, if you please), and 660 Latter Day
Saints, exactly half of whom are saintesses. Alto-
gether a community of less than 1,400,000 souls
runs more than 144 Christian religions, 15 non-
Christian religions, 33 indefinite religions, and
several different sorts of Agnostics. Unfor-
tunately, there is no hope of an export trade in
religions.

Prison Labor.
Writes an indignant correspondent:
How would the K.O. who barracks for long-sentenced
prisoners in N.S.W. gaols being taught trades take it if it
were proposed to teach them law and qualify them to stare
practice when they get out? Goose and gander should
be fed alike.
Which is a very common and also a very absurd
argument. Every man in gaol who doesn’t know
a productive trade should be taught one. Even if
he knows a productive trade, it may be one that
he can’t follow while in gaol, and in that case he
should be taught another. If he knows a produc-
tive trade when he comes out he may work at it
and thereby add to the country’s wealth. If he
comes out and becomes a lawyer he won’t add to
the country’s wealth, for lawyers are not produc-
tive, and if he comes out and remains a criminal
he is not only unproductive of wealth but he
probably destroys wealth and damages wealth-
producers, rendering them incapable of follow-
ing their calling. In a country which im-
ports some £30,000,000 worth of goods annually
from outside there is room to turn 100,000
criminals into productive workmen with-
out overstocking the market with goods—it is
only a question of shutting out imports. Not
only should every criminal be taught some trade
while in gaol, but he should be compelled to work
hard at that trade, and the product should be sold
in the open market outside—but only at the
current price of the free man’s product, so as not.
to reduce wages. The trades should be varied, so
that the rush of reformed criminals won’t congest
any one of them. The imports should be so
reduced by a vigorous tariff as to make it certain
that the criminal’s competition won’t deprive the
free man of employment. And the prison trade
should be taken seriously; the criminal should,
if possible, be made to earn enough at it
to pay the cost of his capture and trial,
and his board and lodging while under de-
tention, and to leave him something over
with which to start the world when he comes
out. In short he should be made to pay his own
way, ’ i stead of being a burden on the outside
worke. So long as the Commonwealth imports
£30,000,400 worth annually from abroad—so long
as there is £30,000,000 worth of work waiting to
to be done —only the most incredible mismanage-
ment can make the products of gaol labor a dan-
ger to the free workman.

Arthur Griffith Kicks Back.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., hits out hard and
neatly in his reply to the Jingo electors of Wara-
tah (N.S.W.) who,called on him to resign his seat:
Your letter practically informs me that some of the
electors of Waratah met one day last month, and passed
some resolutions condemning me and calling upon me to
resign my seat in Parliament.
In reply, I beg to state in the most respectful manner
possible that I am really pleased to hear it, considering
the personnel of the committee that organised, and the
speakers that addressed, the meeting. Almost the whole
of the members of your committee are either mere popu-
larity-hunters or men who have ever been the bitter
enemies of the Labor movement, and I hope in the future
to give them still greater cause for their malignant hos-
tility towards me.
I hope I am not expected to take seriously a request to
resign my seat, made by men who, for eight years past,
have striven by every means in their power to deprive me
of it.
At least one of the resolutions meets with my entire
approval. I refer to that which condemns the slanders
on our troops uttered by the German press, and by other
persons enjoying the protection of the British flag.
The latter portion of this resolution is certainly
apropos, as the foulest slanders that have been
printed against the Australian and British troops are
those which have appeared in the columns of the Jingo
Press of Australia. I have a cutting from the Newcastle
Herald giving the details of the hanging of a woman in
the Transvaal by some New Zealand and Australian
troops on the suspicion that she had fired at them, and
one from another local paper stating that a New South
Wales trooper, along with some Highlanders, wantonly
mutilated the bodies of dead Boers near Graspan, and
finally plundered them, cutting off their fingers to
get their rings, &c. In these cases the names are
given with all the abominable details without a word of
censure or disapproval, but rather, it seems to me, in a
spirit of approbation. I take these two instances out of
hundreds published in our Australian papers, and I will
ask you if there occurred anywhere in the gutter-press of
Germany any such foul slanders on the good name of our
troops. Of course, I do not believe that these incidents
ever occurred, for I am convinced that, if they had, Lord
Kitchener would have shot the culprits like dogs—but
what are we to think of the papers that publish them ?
In conclusion, I desire to say that those of your
speakers who stated that our petition contains any anti-
British sentiments or slanders on our troops, lied, and
what is more, on this occasion they knew it.

Wanted: A Sanitary Autocrat.
An indignant correspondent, “J.A.C.,” who
evidently doesn’t understand politics—State or
municipal—in N.S.W. :
Re published facts as to the condition of Kidman’s
Buildings, Sydney. They were in the same state two
years ago, when the plague attacked the same premises,
and have been allowed thus to remain ever since. What
then have the City Council’s “ duly-qualified ” sanitary
inspectors been doing meanwhile ?
For the information of “J.A.C.”:—There is" no
possible hope of effective sanitary administration
in N.S.W. under the present system. The
municipal authorities can’t be trusted to keep
things clean. The municipal mind seems—as a
rule—to revel in filth, and the average municipality
doesn’t trouble to keep even its own property
decently clean. Whenever a plague-outbreak
compels an independent sanitary investigation of
any town, it is generally found that some of the
worst filth-spots are directly owned by the - town.
council. The State Government cannot be relied
on to keep things clean, because it is also a grave
sanitary offender on its own property, and is
responsible in N.S.W. for such disease-dealing
blunders as the Bathurst and Tam worth con-
taminated water supplies. It is not merely a
question of the influence of dirty-minded property-
owners, though that influence is probably
responsible for the existence of most of the
sanitary scandals in Sydney and elsewhere : there
seems to be inherent in the elected person a
generous tolerance, if not an actual love,
of dirty drains and filthy backyards. The
only method of getting the cities and towns
of this State clean seems to be the appointment
of a Sanitary Autocrat with the powers and
privileges of a Chief Justice, or a Sanitary Board
of Commissioners on the lines of the Railway
Commission. A sanitary autocrat would be best
because, when even two or three men are assembled
together, there seems, from experience, to be often
a mental infection which leads to toleration of
dirt. But an autocrat, with medical and adminis-
trative training, a cold grey man without pity
and without rich relations holding city property,
so well-paid and so well-minded as to be above
suspicion of bribery— he might keep the city and
the State clean, ruling in matters of sanitation
with a rod of iron, absolute Czar of all health
officers, responsible for his actions only to a vote
of both Houses of Parliament. And The Bulle-
tin would take its hat off to him in the street, as
the most useful dignitary on the continent.

“A Melbourne Mother” hits “Gumleaf ” where
it will do him most good :
The murmurings of “Gumleaf” (in “ Plain English,”
8/3/’O2) against the callousness of woman, incite me to
write, in a humble, unobtrusive way, and point out that
he is begging the question. Why doesn’t he define “ cal-
lousness”? Chinese women throw girl-babies over the
wall, and bind up the feet of their still-worse-treated girls
in torturing bandages. Are they callous? We say
“yes”; Celestials say “no.” Their public opinion
differs from ours in its standards, that’s all. Besides,
when babies fall into tubs of boiling-hot water, it’s not
callousness that leaves the vessels there; it’s just
plain, ordinary drink. Sober mothers don’t let
their infants fall about promiscuously. But too
many women, although not drunkards within the
meaning of the Act, muddle themselves with the too-
frequent jug of beer from the nearest pub., and Provi-
dence, who’s always on the look-out to be spiteful, does
the rest. Woman is not “callous,” to her children at all
events. Happy is the child who has no history. The
papers which record the heart-rending child-murders and
burnings and scaldings are silent about the thousands
of well-cared-for and dotingly-watched-over babies, and
these are the overwhelming majority. Take a walk down
Smith-street or Bridge-road, or Chapel-street, where the
working-class women wheel their go-carts of an
afternoon —you can’t help but notice that, of the
hundreds of children, hardly one is ugly or neglected
or shabby. Like E. W. iEsop’s elephant, “ I am a mother
myself,” and know what amount of genuine hard graft it
requires to turn out two or three children with hair
brushed, shoes polished, faces and pinafores clean. How
many of those elaborate little bonnets and frocks do the
patient mothers iron during the week ? And remember
these women do everything for themselves, scrub, wash,
iron, cook, look after husband and babies, and then when
perhaps one infant in thousands falls into a tub, up go
hands and eyes and “ Oh, the callousness of women 1”

Randwick Children’s Asylum (Sydney) has too
much money for its present needs. The asylum (a
huge building erected on Govemmentland, in which
£lOO,OOO of Government money is sunk) is largely
endowed by private funds, but lacks sufficient
inmates. When N.S.W. Parliament adopted
the boarding-out-system for State children, it
decreed that any institution receiving Govt,
subsidy was liable to have its inmates taken away
and boarded out. One result was the emptying
of Randwick Asylum. The directors thereupon
decided to drop the Government subsidy, and no
further claims were received from the boarding-
out department. But the system of immuring
children for years within the walls of an asylum
has fallen into such disrepute that the society
cannot fill its large building. All efforts of the
Government to acquire the building and grounds
have been frustrated, and the proposal “to
resume Randwick Asylum” is now a hoary joke
in the Chief Secretary’s Department. The
directors have, however, put forward a
new scheme —that the police should arrest every
child found begging, flower-selling, or singing
in Sydney streets, and hand them over to the
asylum. This does not commend itself to the
authorities. The Bulletin suggests that the
building be turned into a Foundling Asylum, and
thus save citizens from stumbling over babies’
bodies as they go home o’ nights. It would fill
up quickly enough then.
— : *
Federal Attorney-General Deakin’s second-read-
ing speech on the Judiciary Bill was one of the
best he‘ has ever delivered; He spoke for 3h."
25min., and, of course, in that time got off a lot of
spell-binding nonsense calculated to make the
thoughtless sit up and ejaculate “Wonderful,”
“Beautiful! ” If Deakin could but suppress his
peacock delight in contemplating the spread of his
own tail of eloquence he would be an effective
debater, for he makes his points clearly and
strongly when he gets on to them. He hit the
spot exactly when he claimed that there should be
no delay in establishing the Federal High Court
in order to prevent foreign English courts getting
to business on a democratic constitution with
which they are not likely to have any sympathy.
In vigorous language Deakin pointed out that it
would be a grave mistake to allow the British
courts to give the first interpretations to our
constitution. To continue to work the Federal
legal business through the State courts with an
appeal to the Privy Council he characterised as
“ the administration of a Federal system on pro-
vincial lines.” That touches the heart of the
matter. To allow the Privy Council to give the
first decisions under the constitution would be a
foreign administration of our Federal system ; to
allow the State courts to do the same work would
be a provincial administration.

Australian barrackers for the P. and O. Company
constantly say that it is not by reason of their
cheapness that coolies are employed on the mail-
boats. Listen to Sir Thomas Sutherland, chair-
man of the company, at last annual meeting :
Indian seamen and firemen are an absolute necessity to
this company in connection with the carrying on of their
business, and I say, with all respect for legislators in
Australia, that these measures appear to me to be rather
of a short-sighted character, because if these disadvantages
are to be imposed upon us, and upon others, it follows we
must be recouped by higher rates of passage money and a
higher subsidy.

The registration of a second N.S.W. Shearers’
Union seems, on the surface, to be a smart move on
the part of pastoralist employers to improve their
chances in the forthcoming settlement, by N.S.W.
Arbitration Court, of the industrial conditions of
the pastoral industry. The original Shearers’
Union (the A.W.U.) has demanded a 25 per cent,
increase in rates this season, and some other con-
cessions in re the contract system. The new
Shearers’ Union professes to be satisfied with the
old rate of pay, and does not make such large de-
mands for the limitation of “ freedom of contract.”
Obviously, if the Arbitration Court has before it
on the one side the employers and a section of the
employees, on the other the A.W.U., the
position of that Union will not be so strong
as if the issue were between it (as presumably
representing all the workers) and the Pastoralists’
Union alone. Perhaps the A.W.U. will retort in
kind by promoting a second union of such em-
ployers as favor the granting of their claims. The
Arbitration Court will need to keep a very keen
eye open if it is not to be pretty extensively bam-
boozled by more or less bogus organisations. The
Bulletin does not wish to infer that the new
Shearers’ Union is a bogus body, only that it is a
somewhat remarkable organisation, since it objects
to ask a court of justice whether, as a matter of
justice, its members are entitled to an increase in
wages. But that the bogus-union game will be
played by both employers and workmen is ciear :
it should be the business of the Arbitration Court
to see that it is played in vain, or the Arbitration
Act will be stultified.

Westralian mine managers are rejoicing over an
amendment of the Employers’-liability law, mainly
because they now know better where they are.
The biggest bill they can get for a man is £4OO
(dirt-cheap compared with, say, a horse, when you
consider their respective working lines and the
earning capacity of each); for totally or partially
smashing-up a man, £3OO (dirt-cheap, too, when
you consider how long a smashed-up man may
live and how much he may eat and drink and
wear). Also, a clause of the old mining law has
been knocked out which ought to have stood—that
holding an accident to be prima-facie evidence of
owner’s or manager’s neglect. Anyhow, the onus
ought to be on the manager to prove that his
workings and his methods were reasonably safe,
and that it wasn’t through their unsafety that an
accident occurred. In other words, an accident
challenges his methods, and lie ought to be obliged
to defend them.
»
Dr. Maynard Pain, son of Canon Pain (of
Darlinghurst, Sydney), and lately a successful
superintendent of Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney,
goes to Cairo as a medical missionary. At his
valedictory meeting, he said that he had been told
he need not go so far as Egypt when his own
country could put up with a few medical mis-
sionaries. Still, he felt he ought to go. There
are three things that beguile the missionary to far
lands : (1) The glamor of remoteness —the distant
heathen looks green ; (2) the glamor of change,
romance, new scenery, old ruins, old historical
associations, old towers, old cities, and old his-
torical races of niggers; (3) the satisfaction of
going to a country where the dominant white man
can pose as a superior being, while, in the Aus-
tralian backblox, he would be merely regarded
with good-humored contempt as the sky-pilot.
And these three things make up one vague yearn-
ing which he mistakes for a Divine call. It is
only the same sort of Divine call which sends men
cohentingenteering.
Some Plague Items.
* * Plague funk ” is not so general in Sydney in 1902
as it was in 1900, when wild herds of people rushed
the Health Office to be inoculated, and every man,
woman or child with an undigested morsel giving
stomach-twinges rushed to hospital to be
diagnosed for plague. Still, it is pretty general,
and the scientific exhortations of the doctors are
listened to with reverence.
* • * *
One in authority speaks : “One of the greatest
blunders imaginable is that perfect cleanliness
will stamp out the plague. It won’t. One may
live in a perfectly sanitary house, but if scraps of
food that will attract rats are left about the
plague-laden flea may reach the human denizens
all the same. Fleas like plenty of dust. Rats
like plenty of rubbish, and dirty, littered premises
invite rats; and dusty floors, musty carpets, and
things like that harbor the fleas that may have
just left diseased rats. Therefore, place all
garbage in rat-proof tins, and sweep and disinfect
and make the home untenable to fleas.”
* * •+ 0
Said that owners of certain business premises
affected by plague in 1900, which were not repaired
in accordance with the instructions of the health
authorities, and have been closed owing to
another outbreak this year, are going to ask for
compensation for the loss incurred. The com-
pensation they deserve would be at the end of
a long rope over a lamp-post—one end attached
to the compensation and the other to the necks of
the guilty parties. Both g.p. and compensation
to be about 18in. above ground.
* * * *
' ’ The most plague-scared people in Sydney are
drapers. As a class they have not much to fear, for
in a city of antique refuse-boxes rats are seldom
driven to eat shoddy. The man who literally
carries his life in his socks, and knows not the day
nor the hour when a plaguey flea may sample him,
and, sampling, leave him with a passport for
Heaven, or when he may encounter with his
hands some bubonic-rat-infected goods, is the
Eroduce-merchant’s employee—the fellow who
umps chaff and pollard and so on from the back
of the yard to the front of the shop. All produce-
stores in Sydney seem to be rat-infested, and
where the rats are there will be found the plague.
But up to date no increase in the wages of produce-
store employees has been reported.
* * * m
One eminent draper hurries out of town when a
fresh case is reported, and stays out till the
papers report “no fresh cases since Thursday.”
Then he kisses the wife and kids, and ventures
back fearfully. In town, about every 25min.,
he draws up his pants as far as they will
draw, and hails the message-boy to spray his legs
with a strong solution of formalin, carbolic-acid,
perchloride of mercury, and other savoury germi-
cides. He has not had dry legs since the outbreak
commenced, and is rapidly developing some vile
form of rheumatism.
* * "* *
Another rag-seller struck an original idea.. He
soaks his legs three times daily in a strong solu-
tion of bluestone and water. The bluestone
hardens the skin, and now the hide of his ankles
and hind legs would turn the point of a needle.
He reckons that any flea tackling him would
simply qualify for dental treatment.
* * » »
Another pinned his faith to closely-woven silk
underpants well sprinkled with formalin. In order
to reassure his faith in underpants he used to call
at the Board of Health office to inquire if the
daily case was wearing underpants when dis-
covered, and if so, of what material and texture
were they composed ? N.S.W. Board of Health
never was great on matters of detail, and as often
as not could nob supply the information. Often
he would send round a clerk to ask at the patient’s
house—eager in pursuit of knowledge by which to
test his theory. The yarn goes that investigation
in this direction was suddenly stopped when the
clerk, unaware of the fact, called at a house where
the patient was a lady, and a male relation
vigorously resented his olothing inquiries.
