The season of “ Ben Hur ” ended drama-
tically at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) last Sunday—
Palm Sunday, by the way, on which occurs one
of the big incidents of the play—when the theatre
was gutted by fire. The people could scarcely
have dispersed after the Saturday night’s per-
formance when the fire got its first start, for at
6.15 Sunday morning, when the brigades arrived,
it was practically all over, and a curtain of fire
and smoke had blotted out the handsome
theatre. The origin is the usual mystery:
all that seems certain is that it wasn’t the
fumigating apparatus ; and the habit that theatres
have of burning down is the only explanation
that can be hazarded. The flames, working
from the stage, licked up the scenery and rushed
unchecked across the auditorium. The building,
with its four square walls and its many doors on
the ground floor, acted admirably as a furnace, and
in a couple of hours there was nothing left of the
interior fittings ; ironwork crumbled away like
ant-eaten wood in the fierce heat, and the flooring
went in cinder-flakes to the sky. At 10 a.m. Sun-
day, passing up the uninjured staircase to the dress
circle, one suddenly came on a vast gap, smoking
and fuming, its contour broken by a few ragged
black strings of cinders hanging precariously to
the walls—whitened by the intense heat —just a big
holein abuilding, where there had been a brilliantly
decorated theatre. All the stage fittings, scenery
and properties were, of course, destroyed—a loss
of quite £14,000 to Mr. J. C. Williamson. Of this
six-sevenths was not covered by any insur-
ance. The theatre building belonged to Mrs.
M'Quade. It was, from everybody’s point of
view, a most unlucky burn, and from no one’s
more than from the theatre-employees’, some 500
in number. The fire closes their contracts and
throws them among the unemployed. The prin-
cipals—mostly imported from England—will suffer
severely, but the local supers., auditorium hands,
and so on, more severely still. To them, in many
cases, the fire probablyjspells beggary.
**” * •
Her Majesty’s was associated with some of
the most notable productions of the Australian
THE BIG SYDNEY FIRE: WRECK OF HER MAJESTYS THEATRE.
Photo, by the Exchange Studio, Sydney.
stage. There for many years Georgeßignold staged,
with splendid scenery, with armies of supers, and
with magnificent fidelity to the best traditions of
the craft, a series of Shakesperean plays, followed
by standard melodramas. There, too, the great
Sarah Bernhardt rehearsed, with much vigorous
use of the one little English swear-word she knew,
and played to the delight of a big audience, and
with special admiration from Mr. Dan. O’Connor
and other such local cognoscenti. The greatest
triumphs of that essentially Australian organisa-
tion, the Royal Comic Opera Co., were within the
walls of Her Majesty’s—-when Nellie Stewart, and
Violet Yarley, Flora Graupner and Florence
Young, Charles Ryley, Henry Bracy and
Wallace Brownlow played, and such pieces as
“La Cigale,” “Ma Mie Rosette,” “ The Rose of
Persia ” and “Florodora” were produced. The
theatre has also seen such notabilities as Mr.
Wilson Barrett, Miss Nance O’Neil and Mr.
John L. Sullivan. Just before the “ Ben
Hur ” production the house was for some
weeks occupied by the Williamson Grand
Opera co. It is almost certain that the theatre
will be rebuilt, and that in the rebuilding there
will be better provision for exit in case of fire. It
was not until the destruction of the place that the
Sydney public was informed by the Fire Brigade
of radical defects, from afire-suppression point of
view, in the building. It takes the Plague or a
big fire to secure publication of facts in Australia.
Sympathy with J. C. Williamson in this,
his latest misfortune, will be general. _ Mr.
Williamson has of late struck a streak of bad
luck. Woes have tumbled on him in quick suc-
cession as on the house of (Edipus. The outbreak
of plague at Her Majesty’s, the non-success of the
last season of the World’s Entertainers, this fire
winch, in addition to causing a very big cash loss,
will disorganise his whole arrangements for the
year—all occurring within three months, represent
almost a record accumulation of calamity. He
should be due now for many years of steady
prosperity on the law of averages.
** * *
“ Sapho,” as produced at the Empire by
the Cosgrove co., proved to be an amusing farce,
more especially at the points where it was billed
to teach a “ strong moral lesson.” The production
on Saturday night was further enlivened by one
or two persons in the audience who arose and re-
buked the drama and told it not to go too strong.
Harry Beaumont, as the good young hero, is first
seen in his simple, suburban home. Jack is going
to Paris, and his French Ma, in the outspoken
manner of her country, warns her son to beware
of strange ladies. Jack then sports with his
sisters to the neglect of his fiancee, Irene,
and as the first young man who preferred
his sisters to another girl, he deserves to
be recorded. In Act 11. Jack is at a very
meagre masked ball, where he is seen resisting
various strange ladies, clad mostly in harlequinade
costumes. Jack’s method of repudiating the ad-
vances of these ladies proves clearly that, though
a woman resisting temptation may look sublime,
a man under the same circumstances merely
appears ridiculous. Then Sapho appears in Greek
draperies and red flowerets. The act ends with
Jack succumbing to Sapho, mainly because a large
man has rushed into the building and laid a claim
for the lady on the plea that he taught her the
piano. In Act 111. the famous staircase scene is
merely a rush up some steps that are mostly
turned away from the audience, so that Sapho, who
is a large lady, can do the top ones herself.
* * * *
Harry Beaumont as Jack Gaussin is rather
an infliction, but both he and Miss Rose Phillips
as Sapho rose rather above mediocrity in the
quarrel scene, after Jack had been annoyed by
what other gentlemen had said as to his lady-
love. There are probably only one or two women
in Australia who could play Sapho even decently,
but if Miss Phillips didn’t “ fall ” on the floor, by
just sitting down on it and then spreading herself
out, one would think her better. In other
respects her failure was not a marked one ; it was
indeed fairly respectable, and this is saying more
than it looks, as 99 actresses out of 100 who
attempted the part would fail in it. Mr. Tom
Cosgrove caused considerable amusement as the
vegetarian Uncle who loses his clothes while
bathing. Some of the masked-ball ladies are
entertaining.
** * *
Bland Holt’s “ Hearts are Trumps ” will
this week give place to the “Span of Life,” a
drama which deals with a lighthouse and three
gentlemen making a bridge of themselves over a
chasm. The poverty-stricken ladies who wail
over their coinless condition in Parisian frocking
will wail only for a few nights longer; and the
nice girl (with a bad habit of poking her chin out,
by the way) who is horrified at seeing a drawing
of her supposed anatomy on the walls of the Art
Academy will soon be horrified no more.
** * *
Fitzgerald’s Circus raised its magnificent
tents near Sydney Railway Station on Saturday.
There are some good new items in the show and
some good old ones, and the menagerie includes
the lion that lately distinguished itself by running
away at Broken Hill, thinking it recognised its
native desert. The performing ponies, Barbelle
and Barbette, do some clever feats, while the well-
known Commodore, Oscar, who plays up to
Lizzie the Elephant, and some martial-looking,
gaily festooned Hungarian animals further show
the high intellect of the Horse. Drilling and waltz-
ing aresomeof their accomplishments; a few mokes
of a religious turn of mind walk about backward
as if they belonged to the Salvation Army. The
performing dogs 1 with their nice neck-ribbons and
dainty ways are highly amusing; one of them
indulges in a skirt-dance, being attired for the
performance before the audience. There are
many other clever performing animals. Among
the humans the Moultons and Master Bertie
return temporarily to the status of their monkey
ancestry, and sport among the trapezes as did
our long-armed forefathers among the cocoa-
nut palms of a bygone age. Master Bertie is
a gymnast to whom any intelligent ourang-
outang would feel proud to be related.
One of the prettiest items in the show is con-
tributed by La Belle Rose. Miss Katie Mont-
gomery, as an equestrienne, Master Curdello and
Master Shand in their Double Jockey act, and
the Beautiful White Lady of Dusseldorf form
some of the other attractions, while the show con-
cludes as before with a High Jumping contest and
a Performing Lion taking a ride on an Elephant
and looking absurd and rather ashamed of him-
self.
** * *
There will be an almost entire change of
programme at Sydney Tivoli this Saturday, and
of some of the new “ turns ” report speaks very
highly. Miss Lydia Yeamans Turner, an Aus-
tralian who has risen to a star position in American
vaudeville, the Jackson family of musicians, Rig-
ley, a celebrated flautist, and Dan Conroy, come-
dian, are to be the principal new performers.
The last nights of Lotty, Peggy Pryde, the Lund-
greens, and Nellie Randall are announced.
* * * *
Mr. Rickards opens his new variety theatre
(the Royal) in Brisbane on Saturday night with a
company including Peggy Pryde, Nadine, Colby
and Way, Fanny Powers, and the Rianos. This
will be the fourth house in Australia to be perma-
nently occupied by Mr. Rickards.
** * *
Sydney Palace reopens this Saturday,
March 25th, with a new company of World’s
Entertainers including Salerno, the only rival of
Cinquevalli as a juggler, the Ferraris, Professor
Powell, Platt and Sutherland and many other star
performers. There will be in all 15 turns —each a
strong one—and in view of the very limited
number of theatres now open in Sydney there
should be a prosperous season ahead of the co.
** * *
Easter attractions in Sydney, in addition
to the Agricultural Society’s show, include two
concerts in the Town Hall—sacred music on Good
Friday night and Marie Narelle’s farewell on
Easter Monday night.
William Anderson’s Dramatic Organisa-
tion, assisted by 30 ballerinas, a carriage and pair,
and a fire-brigade, will repeat the strange story of
the Night Birds until further notice at Mel-
bourne Royal. All things considered, the co.
deserve credit for taking their parts seriously.
Also, the stage-manager will be worthy of great
praise when he has taught the lady denizens of
the Night Birds’ Club to shriek on a feminine go-
as-you-please system instead of firing off their
fictitious alarfn in volleys. It should be easy for
those young women to shriek naturally at any
point of Anderson’s festive four-act drama. Pre-
sent scribe speaks from personal experience. This
is not the most shrieksome drama he ever saw, but
as a source of innocent merriment it ranks very
high.

The Princess’s is closed this holy Easter
Thursday. “ Sweet Nell of Old Drury ” sailed on
Tuesday for Tasmania, where the co. are to pro-
duce three or four other plays, in which Miss
Lucy Wilson will make her first Australian
appearances, and Nellie Stewart will not appear
at all. On Saturday the Broughs start their final
farewell season at the Princess’s. The season can-
not last longer than five weeks, and if Melbourne
intends to rise to the occasion, the managerial
Brough must be prepared to .make special matinee
arrangements. In the revival of “The Amazons,”
which is announced for the first of the five weeks,
Miss Brenda Gibson will appear again, after a
long absence. Eight weary years nave elapsed
since Miss G. wore the brown knickerbockers of
a blameless life in Pinero’s pretty comedy.
#* * *
The Bijou also will be closed for two
nights after the issue of this week’s Bulletin, the
World’s Entertainers (whose large assortment of
dumb “turns” is considerably brightened by the
recent engagement of C. M. Rodney) having
passed on to another State.- On Saturday happens
the first Australian production of “The Wrong
Mr. Wright ” with which farcical comedy Edwin
Geach inaugurates his managerial career. Many
of the people who took parts in the “Jones ” and
“ Smith ” successes are now listed in the legion of
“Wright,” and the new members of the old
Arnold Co., include Tom Cannam, Miss Roxy
Barton and Mias Watts Phillips. A good per-
formance of “ Wrong Mr. Wright,” is assured, so
it only remains for the 3-act joke to prove half as
funny as it is said to be.
** * #
“ A Runaway Girl ” at Her Majesty’s will
continue through Easter week and until further
notice. Melbourne generally admits that the
present musical comedy is a more substantial
entertainment than any previous thing of the
kind except, pe.’haps, “Florodora.” It has a
little bit of backbone to its narrative, and several
catchy songs, and none of the principals, bar
Bantock, are very unsuited to their parts. True,
Florence Young doesn’t look like a damsel whose
style was formed in a convent, but she is a fine
healthy soprano for wandermg-minstrel purposes,
and her genial dimple and infallible top-note com-
mend her to approval all the time.

At Rickards’ Opera House an extensive
alteration in the bill is promised. Callahan and
Mack, Winton, Zobedie, Mdlle. Nadine, and one
of two other people will pass out after Thursday
night, and some more others will make their first
appearance at the Saturday matinee. The Per-
mans, late of Musgrove’s panto., have contracted
to cake-walk and otherwise gambol under Rick-
ards’ management; also Mdlle. Lotty, the silken-
tighted Venus, will bring her rounded outlines to
Melbourne notice, and evoke beads of admiration
from the glittering scalp of respectable old age.
** * *
The Good Friday fixtures for Melbourne
are rather more numerous and inviting than usual.
At the Exhibition Building, where the Ancient
Druids gather at this period, Miss Grace Palotta
will discuss the subject of a Faithful Soul,
and a musical festival will be provided,
with Kenningham, Miss Olive Kingston, Miss
Vollugi, the gentle Walter Kirby, Miss
Grace Ward, and many artists on the programme.
At the Town Hall the Philharmonic Society gives
its 23rd performance of “Elijah” ; and at the
Athenseum Hall a new violinist—Bernhard
Walther—is to make his first Australian appear-
ance. M. Walther, a compatriot of Musin, has
lately toured America, and he comes from that
country with the reputation of being one of the
best.
Marie Lloyd and Lottie Collins don’t appear to have
improved their health by trips to Australia, so, con-
sidering that Rickards gained nothing by paying them
£l2O and £lOO a week respectively, it seems a pity that
they ever left ’Ome. Marie and Lottie were both of them
too ill to appear at the Tivoli (Lon.) one night last month.
One of the substituted “turns” was Hamilton Hill, the
Australian baritone, who went from Rickards’to America,
and is now making big money in London on the merits of
his voice and his ’Murikan reputation.
Mrs. Brown-Potter threw up her part in the new
poetical play, “ Ulysses,” owing to the persistent protests
of the author against her elocuting her rhymed lines, or
“reading his meaning,” or something. Said author,
Stephen Phillips, appears to be a masterful young man,
as well as a poet, so it isn’t strange that he came to log-
gerheads with Cora B. Potter,, whose “ golden drone ”
d la Bernhardt is an acquired taste even where prose is
concerned. “ Ulysses ” promises to prosper, despite Mrs.
Potter’s eleventh-hour retirement from the co. She was
cast for the character of Calypse. A Miss Nancy Price
stepped into the vacancy, with results that “ fully justi-
fied her selection,” consequently Mrs. P. is now kicking
herself to think that she provided another girl with a
foremost place in what is said to be “ the finest produc-
tion the English stage has known in recent years.”
A Maoriland pressman writes: “Wilson Barrett was
splendidly advertised in M.L., mainly through the fore-
sight of his insinuating advance rep., who, with a keen
eye to dollars, bit the ear of various mayors for nicely-
arranged public receptions in their town-halls. After
their washups had extended their flabby paws in welcome
the great tragedian usually got off * some high-flown
observations about the beauty and intellectuality of his
noble art, and these were scattered broadcast. This
manoeuvre either struck Percy Dix, the variety show-man,
as good business, or else Dix has a large sense of humor,
and when the Great Mummer was billed for a reception
in gusty Wellington, Dix approached the portly mayor
and suggested that a similar honor be accorded his Oragg
family of acrobats. His Washup, however, glared like an
angry flame against a window-pane, and scouted the
suggestion with contumely. Whereupon a press corres-
pondent slated the chief citizen in vitriolic terms for his
one-sidedness, and pointed out that it was no part of his
duty to bolster up one business enterprise against
another. His Washup lav low and said nuftin.”
Prank Thornton and Co. will be around again shortly.
Nellie Stewart tells her friends that her success as
Sweet Nell of Old Drury gives her little pleasure, because
the atmosphere of the play and the surroundings are so
different to what she had been used to in comic opera and
extravaganza. But Nellie’s complaint that the new
atmosphere doesn’t suit her may be taken with several
grains of salt. All actors, male or female, affect to hold
themselves cheap in their dearest characters.
There is one glaring error in the plot of “ Hearts are
Trumps. ” The Earl of Burford —the owner of the painting
of the picture in the nude of Dora—sent it to the Royal
Academy. Only artists can send pictures to th^Academy.
It is now cabled that Methuen wasn’t
surprised by De la Rey—that the attack was
made on an open plain by daylight, that the
Boers were four miles off when first sighted, and
that without any artillery they charged in the
teeth of the British guns and the heavy British
rifle fire, and finally settled the matter by turning
some of Methuen’s captured guns on the remainder
of his forces. The original tale about the sudden
springing of the Boers out of the ground, and about
their success arising from the fact that they were
disguised in British uniforms, has apparently
evaporated. It is now stated that the British
opened a heavy fire on them when they were still
three-quarters of a mile off, so evidently the
clothes their nakedness was covered with had no
bearing on the result.
From the Federal Parliament lobby, a cor-
respondent :
Regarding the Lyne-Wise feud, about which a Bulletin
paragraph last week, it is said that Bill Lyne has made a
mighty resolve that B. R. Wise shall have no Federal
billet if he can prevent it: there is an active campaign
between the two factions—the Lyne and the Wise. Bill
Lyne’s best friends will hope, for his sake, that he doesn’t
succeed in keeping Wise, with a grudge to work off, in
Australia, for Wise’s memory is long, and the tenacious
animosity with which he followed Reid, and finally dished
that gentleman’s chances of the Federal Premiership, was
a masterpiece of enmity in politics. Lyne has lots of
sense—more sense than most people credit him with—but
he is too lumbering to fight successfully with the limber
Wise.
There’s a Night Bird of London, his front name
is Eric,
Whose dear little spouse seems a trifle hysteric;
For ere she first met him she’d married a thief
In order to load up her future with grief.
The robber she wedded ne’er took her to wife,
But passed from the church into criminal life,
And was said to be killed in a railway train,
Which tempted his widow to marry again.
When she’d duly presented a plump little son
To Number 2 hubby, her lost Number 1
Turns up from the grave like a penny that’s bad.
But a woman in pink, who is awfully mad,
Appears on the scence with a countenance placid
And burns out bis eyeballs with sulphuric acid.
Meantime Eric’s cousin, to make things absurder,
Is mostly engaged in committing a murder,
Or stealing the kiddy and turning the light down,
Whilst poor Mrs. Eric, arrayed in a night-gown,
Lies drugged on the bed whither Number 1 hubby
(Not the wandering Pa of young Rupert so chubby,
But the man-from-the-grave, whom she “chuck’t ”
ere he kissed her)
Has lured her by means of his dissolute sister.
Now, the frilly-robed wife who partook of the
claret,
And the lunatic-wife with the rats in her garret,
They wrestle and fight, never heading theories
Of him who’s lamenting the loss of his eyes.
But somehow this crisis is brought to a close,
And the virtuous people continue their woes
Till Robert, the cousin, gets taken to “quod ”
Because he is guilty “ in sight of his God,”
Although the police have no tittle of proof,
Save the word of a corpse who was blown through
the roof.
Then Eric the roamer and Bertie, his friend,
Remark that the Truth always scores at the end ;
And the flames of affliction are finally laid
By the steam-squirting hose of a Fire Brigade.

and Metallurgical Engineer, Stock Exchange Buildings,

owners who have properties worth handling.*

Vanning Tables.— The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Oollins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Victoria last week offered locally a little 3 per
cent, loan of £250,000 at £94. It got only £76,000 from
the public and £lOO,OOO from the Savings Bank, but
presumably the rest of the money will come. That
is to say, the State will pay about £3 4s. per cent,
on the money actually received, and during the 30
years’ currency of the loan the interest will amount
to £225,000. Also, £15,000 of interest will be added
to the permanent debt and called discount. If the
State offered 3J per cent., then it could probably sell
the loan at par, and avoid adding any of the interest
to the permanent debt ; a mean swindle upon pos-
terity is done to save one shilling per cent, per annum, or
thereabouts, in interest. The whole loan business is a
good deal of a swindle, but when a State, whose credit is
only good enough for a 3J per cent, loan, borrows nomin-
ally at 3 per cent., and adds the difference to the
permanent debt under the name of discount, the
swindle is a shade worse than usual. And when
it is considered that Victoria will pay, during
the lifetime of most of its present population, about
£240,000 for the use of that £250,000, and will still
owe the 6250,000, the garish foolishness of the whole
loan business stands out conspicuously. It would be
£250,000 cheaper to pay £BOOO a year into a public works
fund out of taxation. The public works might arrive a
little more slowly at first, but they would arrive sure
enough, and the annual payment during the first 30
years would be no more, and there would be no £250,000
debt at the end of the 30 years.
* » * •
Victoria has already struck one awful streak of
bad luck over the war ; a £3,000,000 loan fell due just
when the London money-market was almost at its worst,
and the Cabbage Garden State lost about £300,000 in dis-
count on the deal—tthat is to say, it added that amount
to its debt. It has £5,500,000 falling due in 1904, and if
by any possibility the war drags on till then, or the
London market continues sick from other causes, it will
probably lose £550,000 over that. That is where the joy
and gladness of having loans falling due at fixed
dates, instead of redeemable at the option of the
State, as in England, comes in. In England the
public creditor can never demand repayment; there-
fore, when there is a glut of cheap money, England
does a little conversion operation, and gains by
it, and, when money is scarce, it leaves things
alone. Here, almost every loan must be repaid on a
certain day, and the State has to borrow the money
however bad things are, and at whatever expense. New
South Wales has £50,000,000 of debt falling due in five
separate years—an average of £10,000,000 in each year.
If those happen to be bad years it may drop £5,000,000 in
discount, commission, expenses, and sundries over these
simple operations. There is one year when the Common-
wealth, unless it has established a big sinking fund by
then, Will have to borrow £31,000,000 in one stupendous
heap to renew loans. If that happens to be a war .year, and
a time of trouble, it will be a great episode.
Treasurer Turner was supposed, some time ago,
to be inquiring into the Canadian banking system, the
adoption of which has been so often recommended by
The Bulletin. Have his inquiries led to anything yet?
The Canadian system requires the banks to hold 40 per
cent, of their cash reserves in State notes, but the pro-
portion might as well be 50 per cent., for they hold over
50 per cent. The gold reserves in the banks of the Com-
monwealth—that is to say the amount of gold lying
permanently idle, bringing in no interest, and doing no
active service whatever —is never more than a fraction
below £20,000,000, and is usually over £20,000,000.
It all represents borrowed money and it costs Aus-
tralia over £700,000 a year in interest, which is a
dreadful drain when there is no return to show for it.
Ten millions in gold and ten millions in Federal Govern-
ment notes would moulder in the banks’ cellars quite as
successfully as the present £20,000,000 of gold. And with
the £10,000,000 thus obtained the Federal Government
could take up all the State loans falling due for renewal
in the next three years, and save the disastrously expen-
sive process of renewing them in London. It would then
hold £10,000,000 of State securities; it would have an
annual income of at least £300,000 in the shape of interest
—which now goes to the British money-lender ; and it
could use this for the further repurchase of State securities
so as to further reduce this country’s foreign debt. There
would be £300,000 a year less going out of the country
which would be so much absolutely saved ; and the
States would probably save somewhere between £500,000
and £1,000,000 in discounts and renewal expenses. It is
a good many months since Treasurer Turner said that he
had written to Canada for information about the Cana-
dian banking system, and it is time someone asked him if
the reply hasn’t come to hand ?

No special prosperity has come in the way of
the Emu Bay (Tasmania) Railway Co. during 1901. When
all the paper or goodwill items, such as “ Rights acquired
under V.D.L. Co.’s Waratah-Zeehan Railway Act and
other concessions in terms of prospectus, £lOO,OOO ”;
“ Preliminary Expenses, etc., £41,125” ; and so on, are
eliminated the situation is about as follows :
Losses on Pr ffi??£U ,d Total
Business. Ex D pense s, &c. Deflcit
1898 .. £1395 .. .. £104,404
1899 .. 5078 .. £39,055 .. 348,537
1900 .. 9181 .. 61 .. 157,779
1901 .. 8078 .. .. 165,857
The situation hasn’t improved to any material extent.
Earnings are a little over £39,000, and expenses, rent,
and interest a little over £47,000, which represents a
lamentable and almost impossible-looking gap. Of the
paid-up capital of £360,000 only a little over £194,000 is
now represented by tangible assets. In fact, a very large
portion of the capital was apparently never represented
by tangible assets.
The Emu Bay Railway Co. is a dreadful
example of the ways of Private Enterprise when it de-
votes itself to railway construction in this country. The
principal part of the system—the Emu Bay-Mount
Bischoff section—was constructed by the Van Diemen’s
Land Co. —an English land-grabbing concern which owns
an immense area of Western Tasmania. Over the greatest
part of its principality the V.D.L. Co. refuses to make
roads, refuses to sell or lease, refuses to do anything. It
just moulders. Even the town of Burnie (Emu Bay),
which is on its grant, makes little progress because the
co. will only condescend to sell a little ground now and
then for building purposes. The Tasmanian Government
hasn’t the courage or honesty to break up this ghastly
incubus with a heavy graduated land-tax and absentee-
tax, though it is to be hoped the Federal Parliament will
do it, and soon. The utter stagnation of the V.D.L. 00.
may be gathered from the fact that there is no road from
the prosperous seaport of Emu Bay to the large mining
town of Zeehan (population about 10,000) —in fact, there
is no road from anywhere to Zeehan. The stagnant
V.D.L. 00. sold its railway to the Emu Bay and Mount
Bischoff Railway 00. (which raised the money to buy it
mostly by borrowing at 4£ per cent.), and this latter
leased it to the Emu Bay Railway Co. for £lO,OOO a year,
with right of purchase for £198,000. <
The Emu Bay Railway Co., like most private
railway companies in Australasia, refused flatly to put
up anything like sufficient capital. Therefore, instead
of purchasing its railway for £198,000, it pays £lO,OOO a
year rent —which is equal to borrowing money at £5 Is.
per cent, per annum. Then (because it wouldn’t put up
enough capital of its own) it raised money for the con-
struction of additional lines by borrowing £200,000 on 5
per cent, debentures, hut as it only received about
£160,000 cash (the rest went in “ expenses of flotation,
&c.,”) this money costs it £6 ss. per cent., or, including
the loss on redemption of debentures, about 7 per cent.
Then the co. raised more money by issuing £lOO,OOO
worth of preference shares on which 4 per cent, must be
paid whether it is earned or not, and which must receive
8 per cent, dividends before the ordinary shareholders
get anything. Of course such an insanely-financed line
doesn’t pay—there is hardly a line on earth that would
pay with these burdens on top of it.
In 1901 this disastrous railway earned .£12,122
over and above working expenses. If the ordinary
shareholders who started the business had put up enough
money of their own to buy the original line for £198,000,
and to build or buy the additions thereto, without any
of this insane financing, borrowing, selling of absurdly-
costly debentures at a huge discount, and issuing of 8
per cent, preference shares, about £480,000 would have
covered everything. Even in the howling wilderness of
Western Tasmania their spec, would then be at least pay-
ing them 2£ per cent, with great possibilities of improve-
ment. As it is, this little 100 miles or so of line has as
many hangers-on and useless dead-weights to support as
many a 2000-mile system, and it can’t stand the strain.
* • • *
Lead producers and the people who would like
to be, hoped that the low prices would soon knock out the
supply; and they expected that, as there is not a big
accumulated stock of the metal, the market would soon
pull itself together again after its recent drunk and the
bad time that followed in the morning. But instead of
the supply being starved by low prices, it grows fatter
and fatter, so that combined Jan. and Feb. imports to the
U.K. for the last three years are this size :—l9OO, 30,758
tons ; 1901, 35,2G7 tons 1902, 40,372 tons. Consumption
certainly is not increasing at this rate ; it is doubtful
whether it is increasing at any rate at all. And if, as this
seems to show, people can get all the lead they want for
£l2 10s., it doesn’t seem highly probable that they will
pay over that figure. How long producers and exporters
can fill them up at that price is, of course, the * in the
case ; it depends a good deal on the metallurgical de-
velopments of the near future. And as some sort of a
guide to the length of time taken to make a complete
recovery, it is recalled that, after the lead market had got
down to £9 10s. in ’94 (this was the bottom of the slump
which followed the collapse of the American silver-legis-
lation and the Indian currency changes), it was April,
1898, before the price passed £l3 and stayed above it.

The co. owning the Illawarra (N.S.W.) ore-
reducing works has lately suffered another change—it
is the Smelting and Refining Co. of Australia now. The
London directors are moving round ; big contracts have
been made, and two ’Murkan smelting people have been
sent out to do the hustling this end. The co. is reaching
out for a considerable New Caledonian trade.

* » * •
The New York Life has a surplus, over and
above the face-value of its policy contracts and the sums-
set aside for payment of bonuses, of £3,216,759. In making
this calculation, future interest-earnings are reckoned as
regards all policies issued last year at 3 per cent, (which
will be the basis for all future business) and as regards
some of the old policies at the same rate ; on the rest of
the old policies the basis is 4 per cent. The interest
actually earned during 1901 represented £4 os. Bd. per
cent, on the average funds, or, if the large sum realised as
profit on securities sold during the year (£632,793) is in-
cluded (which cannot safely be done, as this is not a regular
or reliable item of revenue), the interest-receipts were
about £5 2s. lid. per cent. The surplus is sufficient to re-
duce the assumed interest to an eminently safe 3 per cent,
basis. The New York Life is one of the very few offices-
which publishes a list in which every individual property,
and its value, is set forth, and thus subjects its assets to
the most searching criticism. This is a system that can-
not be too highly commended in the policy-holders’ inte-
rests, for no inflated asset can avoid being noticed by
somebody, and it furnishes about the best guarantee that
the policy-holders could possibly have. And, in addition,
there is an independent Government audit in the U.S.
which is an additional assurance that the funds are all
they profess to be.

L.I.A. only pays 3 per cent, interest on its reconstructed

A reduction of £12,664 in six years is not very promising,

period have been some £32,000. At this rate of progress
there bids fair to be a very large deficit when the
realisation is completed. The concern is very cheaply
managed, but its investments appear to be a lamentable
collection. It has paid no dividend since 1891, and the
whole dividends paid in the 10 years of its comparative
prosperity were only £44,644, so the shareholders have
already lost nearly twice as much as they ever got out
of it.

From Westralia: Thirty 15001 b. stamps are
coming out for the Westralia Mt. Morgans—the heaviest
in this State, and, as far as the writer knows, in Australia.
The Mt. Morgans (last month’s return, £14,400) is going
to make a struggle to line up with the biggest of the
Boulder mines. It is adding to its cyanide plant as well
as to its battery. Great Fingall (last year’s profit was
£89,372 on a capital of £125,000) makes so good a show
because it pays for much of its equipment and develop-
ment by letting go parcels of Sons of Gwalia and other
stocks which it holds. Great Boulder is now carving
its way down to 1600 ft. The 1400 ft. stone averages 26dwt.

The recent jump of Brown Hill Extended shares
from 15s. to 325. was the result of cutting a new lode at
the 600 ft. level in a crosscut to the East. Up to the
present no lode so far East has shown any permanency,
and it seems unlit (v that the new find will lead to much.

How these 1-""listers and boodlers love one
another I Whitalu.l Wright in his examination said that
Kaufman was appointed consulting engineer to the
London and Globe on condition that he should not take
secret commission. Kaufman, he said, cabled advice to
sell the Horseshoe shares which the company held, on the
ground that the mine was no good, and at the same time
advised other people to buy, which they did, and became
the possessors of the most productive mine on the field.
Now that the self-confessed manipulator is bottom dog
Kaufman can afford to “ vink ”at the charge. He is now
in London, and will doubtless volunteer to go before the
liquidator and give his version of the transaction.
♦ * * *
The Associated is producing about £15,000
worth of gold monthly for the benefit of the directors and
miners. The patched-up plant which has cost about
£500,000 saves the gold, but at a prohibitive cost. As the
average value of the bulk of the ore in the fields is about
15 to 20 dwts., it, cannot return the cost of mining,
roasting, milling, grinding, cyaniding, filter pressing,
etc., and Associated people cannot realise this too soon.

Great Fingall running 40 of its 60 heads of
stamps during February treated 4704 tons for 8002 oz.
This is at the rate of 4f tons per stamp daily, which will
open the eyes of the Victorian and N.S.W. millmen. The
new Day Dawn shaft will soon be ready for hauling, when
the remaining 20 heads will start and bring up the mine’s
output to about ll,ooooz. monthly. Company ought to
pay at least £1 div. this year on its 10s. shares. Bewick
Moreing’s engineer reports that the value of the ore in
sight is £1,180,000, and that the average of the lode in
No. 8 level is 2oz. 3dwt. per ton. Truly, a great mine 1

The current year’s gold output for W.A. is
nearly certain to reach 2,000,0000 z., for, though some
mines, such as Lake View Consols and Associated, will
show a decrease, a number of others, such as the Fingall,
Cosmopolitan, Horseshoe, Perseverance, Boulder, Ida H.,
Kalgurli, South Kalgarli, Oroya, Associated Northern,
Ivanhoe, are expected to show a considerable increase.
» * » *
The Kalgurli, which has been equipped with
nine Edwards’ furnaces, announces its first output from
the new plant of 1010 tons for 12650 z. for a broken
period. The plant is expected to treat a minimum of
3000 tons monthly. Development work in depth is going
on, and enough ore is opened up to ensure continuous
running for a long time. In the boom period of 1899 the
shares were run up to £ll, a price they are never likely
to see again. The mine will probably earn dividends of
25 to 30 per cent, on capital, so extreme value of shares
is not more than 60s.
* • * *
Hannan’s Oroya has been driving north at the
600 ft. level towards the rich chute worked by the North-
ern Associated and Brown Hill, and will shortly start
driving from the 700 ft. level. As the chute is dipping
south towards the shaft, it should soon be cut at the
latter level. The Oroya, it is asserted, should have nearly
1000 ft. of the rich pipe within its boundary and, on its
being located, shares ought to improve in value.

Lake View: Diamond drill bore from the
seventh level has cut the Perseverance lode, which is
three feet wide and worth only three dwts. per ton.
“ The mineralised character of the ore is regarded by the
management as encouraging.” Is it so to the “ poor
deluded fools” who were induced to pay over £2O for the
shares ? A London paper says that “ Lake Views are the
Ireland of the Westralian market”—whatever that may
mean—and it is prophesied that the question of providing
further capital will become acute within a few months.
* * « *
A strong sidelight is thrown on Mount Lyell’s
successful operations by the half-yearly report of North
Lyell. Quantity of ore sent to Mount Lyell in the half-
year, 44,249 tons ; average, 7'53 per cent, copper. The
contract between the two companies has never been dis-
closed, but it has been admitted that it has been very
satisfactory to Mount Lyell. Probabilities are that from
deductions for losses (which do not occur in well-
regulated works) and profit on smelting and realisation
charges, it has netted at least £2 per ton on this ore,
besides the indirect profit from the use of the ore as a
flux. With their own plant at work North Lyell would
have made more profit on the ore sent to Mount Lyell
than it made on all its consignments, including 12,494
tons of 17'33 per cent, ore shipped to England and the
continent. North Lyell people threaten to start their
smelters next month. This will be a nasty jar for Mount
Lyell. North Lyell, on appearances, is the champion
benevolent mine of the continent.

Mt. Garnet: Chairman informs shareholders
that, if copper does not improve in price, they must wait
some time for dividends. Latest report from mine
states that the ore in the bottom level carries 5 per cent,
copper, which, with Mt. Garnet systems, would be unpay-
able even if copper were to improve in value.

N.S.W. dredging returns for last week show an

Jembaicumbene pays a dividend of fid. on the 7th April,

• • * »
At Lloyd’s Burraga mine, mining operations
have been suspended temporarily, owing, it is reported,
to the failure of the concentrating plant. The co. has
been most unfortunate with its mechanical appliances.
At the outset two 120-ton water jacket furnaces, com-
bining all the latest improvements, were ordered, but
never erected,although every preparation had been made
for putting them in position. The process was condemned
without being given a trial. Writer is aware that a party
of practical smelters- as distinguished from laboratory
theorists—offered to find the fluxes and run the furnaces
for three months without pay if they did not get satisfac-
tory commercial results, but the proposal was pooh-poohed
by the governing authorities. The concentrating plant,
which has cost over £50,000, is said to be a dead failure.
It is an open secret that several mechanical experts have
been working at it for nearly three months trying to effect
adjustments to make it workable. Mine has developed
far beyOnd the most sanguine anticipations, and had the
company been content to follow Lloyd’s systems on an
enlarged scale it would have been in the dividend list
long since. Writer does not assume that-there was no
justification for adopting more up-to-date methods, hut
he asserts that a wrong might have been done to an indi-
vidual, and possibly to the shareholders, in not giving the
metallurgist who recommended blast furnace treatment
an opportunity to demonstrate whether or not his judg-
ment was sound.

BRANDY
Despite the Premiers’ stickling for recogni-
tion of alleged rights the State political magnate
looms ever smaller. Half Victoria isn’t quite
sure of its Governor’s name yet and not one in a
hundred could say where he or any of his Ministers
is at a given moment. T’other day when Gov.
Sydenham Clarke went to Daylesford to open a
poppy-show or something the Argus summarised
the whole proceedings—speech and all—in a fl-
inch par.
Admiral Rawson, who will shortly be a
kind of damaged or second-class Governor in
N.S.W., seems to have had his leg pulled pretty
considerably as to the scope of his powers. Gush-
ing to an interviewer:
Being a naval officer in control is a certain kind of pre-
paratory education for a Governor, I believe. The
handling and manoeuvring of a naval squadron gives a
man firmness, an unhesitating faculty of making up his
mind, and some nerve.
And, sad to relate, the most undecided man on
earth would fill the State Governor’s billet just as
well as Ad. Rawson. He might not open the dog-
show or the new bridge with such resolution or
strength of mind, but even if an embarrassed and
weak-minded Governor fainted from excitement
while trying to open the bridge and had to be
carried away; the bridge would be just as open as
if it had been opened by all the master minds of
ancient and modem times.
* * * *
Seven days after departure of last
detachment of the A.M.C. for Africa, two horses
left behind by them were found in stalls at the
Sydney Agricultural Society grounds. During the
seven days or more that these unfortunates were
without food orwater, a number of the cohentingent
men were quartered at the ground, but these par-
ticular horses were, somehow or other, nobody’s
business, and were only discovered by accident.
When examined they were in a terrible condition,
and though slowly pulling round are still on the
sick list.

Probably those devout local legislators who
appoint days of “humiliation and prayer ” have as
much faith in their efficacy as Sir Robert Walpole
Geor g e ll-’3 queen, Caroline, was
said to the Princess Emily, “Pray, madam, let
this farce be played ; the Archbishop will act it
very well. You may bid him be as short as you
will. It will do the Queen no hurt, no more than
any good; and it will satisfy all the wise and good
fools who will call us atheists if we don’t pretend
to be as great fools as they are.”
* * * *
N.S.W. railways provide an electric alarm
in each carriage, by means of which a passenger
can in case of emergency, call the guard and stop
the train ; a fine of £lO is provided for using the
alarm “without due cause.” Now, supposing a
defenceless man found himself bundled, just as a
train moved off, into a carriage where the only
other occupant was a woman under 75, and he
stopped the train. Would any intelligent magis-
trate inflict a fine ? If so what fraction of the £lO
fine would meet the case ?
* * * •
When will N.S. W. Railway Commissioners
arrange for the decent ventilation of their
sleeping cars ? To occupy one in the hot, Aus-
tralian summer, with some dozens, perhaps,
of fat hard-drinkers, and a consumptive or two,
is to run the risk of an early grave; any life
society knowing its business would load the lives of
those who travel by sleeping-car. Go into a
sleeper about 6 a.m. and you encounter Air on its
deathbed, gasping in hot sweating corruption ;
and the human beings immersed in this oily and
nauseating medium swoon heavily rather than
sleep. It is a fine hunting-ground for bacilli, the
N.S.W. sleeping-car.
» • * *
A Minister (of Health!), several M’sL.A.,
and about 150 councillors and assorted sundries,
journed to Spring Vale, lately, to open Melbourne’s
new cemetery. There was a subsequent “lun-
cheon,” on the adjoining racecourse. The local
inhabitants opined that the “openers” were
hard-up for a job, but they manifestly overlooked
the depressing effect of viewing 766 acres of pros-
pective graves and the thoughts of the 2,070,000
late-lamenteds who will ultimately be planted
therein. The trustees of the new ground have
catered for Melbourne’s tastes to a nicety. The
different churches are carefully portioned off, so
that there will be no quarrelling, and in the centre
of the ground is a “superior” section for mixed
religions a sort of dress-circle for the Naicest
only—for “ those whose memories relatives may
desire to perpetuate by specially-elaborate monu-
ments.” When Gabriel blows the last trump
there will be no loud, unseemly blast on this
hallowed spot: a white-winged messenger will tap
gently on the sod, present his card, and murmur,
daintily, “The Almighty presents His compli-
ments, and would Mrs. Toorak kindly step up-
stairs.” Wrote Francis Adams: “I hate rich
Melbournians, they are such snobs —they are worse
even than English people.”

What may happen to a man in a Sydney
eating-house:—
At Sydney Hospital, last night, a man presented him-
self in the casualty-room in a state of terror. He was
partaking of mince-meat in a city restaurant,' he ex-
plained, when, to his horror, he discovered on his plate a
tiny mouse. His thoughts at once turned to plague.
And a daily newspaper calls this an “amusing
experience.”
* • • •
N.S.W. Government is most inconsistent.
When drought comes along it appoints a day of
humiliation and prayer for rain ; when the plague
comes it appoints a day for exterminating the
rats. If prayer is efficacious for one evil, why not
for the other ? O ye of little faith !

A new professor of clairvoyance in Sydney
undertakes to reveal to students questions on
examination papers which must be tackled at a
near day. He explains that not earlier than a
couple of days before the examination is he to be
consulted as the “papers might not be fully
printed.
A very great potentate indeed was im-
prisoned for 20 minutes recently in a back yard
and subjected to great contumely. An under-
strapper, who knew him not, found him languidly
picking a flower in a Sydney park where such
things are not allowed, refused to be crushed by
presentation of the great man’s card, pointed with
a cold unbiassed hoe at the printed regulations,
drove the great man before him like a clothed
bullock to the curator’s house, and, the official not
being there, locked him in a yard till further
orders. Words fail to express what happened
when the official looked into that yard and found
who was illegally confined therein.

Sydney City Council, on the suggestion of
Alderman Meagher, sent round 18 carts, after,the
great Day of Rat Poisoning, and collected 50 rats,
a fraction less than 3in each cart. It might have
got more, but many public-spirited citizens, after
getting their poison gratis, refused to part with
their rats unless the dead were paid for
at the current rate of 3d. each. What these
citizens really require is to be made to pay for
their rat poison, to be fined £2o—with the
alternative of three months’ gaol for owning dirty
rat-infested premises, and to be made to deliver
their dead rats at the incinerator in a humble and
apologetic spirit. The unspeakable insolence of
dirty people requires to be put down. Apropos,
has anything been done yet with that Sydney J.P.
who wrote to the papers stating that his house (he
gave the address) was swarming with rats, and
admitting almost in so many words that he was
doing nothing to make his premiss less of a public
danger, but demanding to know why the city
authorities didn’t come to clean up the establish-
ment for him ? If any case ever called aloud for a
htiavy fine and costs that one did.
Half-a-dozen ancient and inter-
esting volumes, containing reference
to some first families, have been re-
moved, for obvious reasons, from the
jurisprudence shelves in Sydney Free
Library. Perhaps they have been
added to the Australian collection.
The volumes are the “ Newgate
Calendars,” of dates early in the last
century.

“ Coogee ” : Scene, King-street, near
Pitt-street, Sydney. Time, Rat Day eve, 5 o’clock.
Going along I noticed the crowd giving a wide
berth to the gutter and discovered the reason in
two dead rats lying therein. Just as I was pass-
ing, a shabbily-dressed man spotted them. His
face brightened arid he grabbed them and put one
in each pocket of his coat. Satisfying himself
that there were no more to be picked, up in that
gutter, he mixed again with the crowd. Don’t
know if he had any more about his person.

The real objection to the Melbourne
street fruit-hawker has not yet been so much
as hinted at. The property owner and the wealthy
trader have often spoken, but not a single voice has
been raised in the interests of public health. And
yet the health of the community is in much greater
danger from the street fruit hawker than is
property or trade. Melbourne is a city of
magnificent distances and its streets have no
turns and bends to impede the rolling dust-clouds,
It staggers anyone who considers the subject
to think of the millions of disease germs carried
around in the Melbourne dust clouds. To these
germs the uncovered fruit on the street barrows
is open for a perching place. Then there are the
hawkers themselves. For the most part they
live in the filthiest portions of the city. They
are dirty in their habits, and their clothes in
many cases reek with dirt. An inspection of the
men in Swanston-street any day will revea.l the
fact that many of them are diseased—diseased , in
a large open public way that there is no denying
or concealing. They paw the fruit with grimy
hands and polish it with cloths which
serve many purposes. They come out unwashed
THE MORBID NATIVE.
MOTHER: “ Why are you. looking, so glum, Edie?”
THE KID : “ Mummer, mine has been a wasted
life.”
and they remain unwashed all day. It is argued
that the public want the fruit hawkers in the
street, and that therefore there should be fruit
hawkers. The public that is prepared to buy
fruit under these circumstances is a hog public
and the clean members of the community should
be protected against it.

LULLABY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lambs are sleeping in the fold,
All the little birds take rest,
And my Baby dreams —behold !
Warm asleep upon my breast.
Two wee feet within my hand,
Whither do those feet now stray ?
In what realm of Slumber Land
Does my Baby wend his way ?
It is where the Lady Moon
Holds her revels in the sky ...
Where the stars all sing the tune
Of a Baby’s lullaby ?
It is where the mermaids swim
Through the waves’ phosphoric gleam,
While, asleep in sea-caves dim,
Silly mermen lie and dream ?
Hush 1 . . . The fire sinks low—is dead—
Ah—how short a night is this—
Soon the waking Day burns red,
Underneath the Sun’s warm kiss !
The Young ’Un.

Albert Park (Melbourne) reserve, extend-
ing from Clarendon-street, South Melbourne, to
St. Kilda, has a bad reputation as the happy
hunting-ground of two or three small parties of
blackmailers, The night-prowlers “fox”
couples, and then hold the unhappy man
up, threatening all sorts of things if
not bribed with cash or valuables.
One moonlight night, recently, a St.
Kilda citizen, riding home on his bike,
heard angry voices and a woman’s
scream coming from a grove about a
hundred yards from the road, and
was plucky enough, or curious
enough, to, investigate. He found
three blackniailers “dealing with ” a
young man, who was fighting the
band with considerable vigor, while a
.young woman lay on the grass sob-
bing violently. The newcomer
chipped in, knocking out one bad lot
with a heavy left in the place
where it would do most good.
The fight that ensued went all
against the freebooters,and pre.
sently the party drew off with
great loss of prestige, leaving
the rescued warmly thanking
the rescuer. Then the former
lifted the sobbing female off
the grass that she might thank
him also. About one minute
later another free fight commenced
on the spot. All over such a trifle,
too. The lady wailing on the grass
had proved to be, the rescuer’s own
wife.

The very latest way of
making a living in Australia.
A man who claims to be a
B.A. argues on any and
every subject with anyone in
the streets, and, when a
crowd assembles, sends
round the hat. A heated
argument always draws a big
crowd, and the collection is gener-
ally fairly satisfactory.
For the common or garden offence of
“ bilking ” his landlady an ex-Vic. Fifth Cohen-
tingenter was summarily given a month by P.M.
Panton t’other day,—though the sentence was
afterwards suspended to give him a chance to
square the bill—a small 16s. .But a few months
ago any landlady who dared to take such action
would have been chased into the Benevolent
Asylum as a Pro-Boer. The name of this ex-
swaddy—hold your breath—was Robert Emmett.

The noble art of convoy-capturing is pro-
gressing in Australia. Hardly had the two
alleged perpetrators of the Port Adelaide capture
gone into retirement before a Bendigo mine-
manager’s convoy of wages was captured in an
even more artistic, and peculiarly S’African style.
The operator didn’t encumber himself with a
mate, or fool with a revolver. His tool was a
Winchester rifle—surer and hardly less handy.
He began by shooting the team —that is to say,
the buggy-horse. Always shoot the teams if you
want to stop a convoy. Mine-manager tried to
jump out of the buggy with the money-bags,
tripped, fell, dropped the bags, got up and found
himself surrounded by this solitary De Wet and
looking down the barrel of the rifle. He
stepped back and De la Rey picked up the
bags. Meanwhile a zealous youth had left his
dinner. He came over and asked the manager if
he had a revolver. He also tried to pull Paul
Kruger off his horse, and got knocked down for
his pains. Then Stevn galloped off and vanished
into space, and all search for Cronje proved fruit-
less. If the police have any glimmering of sense
they will hunt through the description of cohen-
tingenters (if any official department,has had the
sense _ to keep them) and they will probably
identify the lost commando.

A GIDDY LITTLE THING .
THE SHEPHERD: “ I cannot imagine what pleasure
you find in going to the theatre.”
SHE: “Oh! there are three pleasures. First,
thinking about it all day beforehand. Second,
enjoying it while you are there; and third,
rolling it as a sweet morsel under your
tongue all the next day."
Public bathing bids fair to become a source
of profit. Recently the beautiful legs of a Sydney
society girl, bathing at Bondi in the scantiest
attire, attracted the attention of two dabblers in
“ color,” one of whom, after waiting till the
damsel had dressed, offered her £5 to let her legs
be photo’d—entirely, of course, in the interests of
art. The lady hesitated, but decided, like a
sensible girl, that her legs were safer where they
were.
• • • •
“ E.E.” writes:
Mr. O’Sullivan was so highly shocked at reports he
received about mixed bathing at Little Coogee, that he
hied himself off to the spot without even announcing his
trip to the newspapers. After a minute and careful
inspection of the costumes worn by the bathers, Mr.
O’Sullivan concluded “that the ladies’ costumes were all
right,” but he was shocked at the scant costumes of the
men. Minister for Lands Crick, however, whose sense of
propriety is more tender, was inexpressibly shocked at
the whole show, and said that the present state of affairs
must cease. Bathers are in doubt as to whether he
means mixed bathing or scanty costumes. Mr. Crick
also promises to erect shelters for dressing and undress-
ing, which no doubt are urgently wanted. Though a dip
at Little Coogee is indulged in by many respectable
people, there are a tew undesirables who make this spot
objectionable by their disgraceful antics. On Sundays
that the writer has gone to this spot for a dip he has wit-
nessed indescribably disgusting incidents. Every Sunday
a crowd of roughs enter the water and act most indecently
Besides making the bathers wear more clothes, ;the
Government ought to appoint a special constable’to be
daily in attendance, and to have the power to order
away, or arrest, the roughs and street women who make
this favorite bathing resort objectionable.

The table upon which the late Queen
Victoria signed the Commonwealth _ Act is
now on exhibition under a glass case in Melb.
Parliament House. For long it stood in the hall
uncovered, and its garish legs were used by the
country visitors of members to strike matches on.
Prominent in the pawnshops of Melbourne
are boxes of Queen Victoria’s chocolate, bearing
the medallion of her late Majesty and the crest of
Windsor. How much the chocolate was pawned
for is a mystery.
• * * •
Te Aroha (M.L.), which burned Campbell-
Bannerman in effigy as a low-down disloyalist, is
the cockiest borough for its size (about 500 popu-
lation) on earth. At the time of the Dreyfus
trial it burned the French flag, and Gov. Ranfurly
had to apologise to the French consul for the
“ insult.” Te Aroha proudly claims that the first
telegram of congratulation received in Mafeking
by Birnum Powell was from “the Mayor and
Councillors of Te Aroha,” and firmly believes that
Chamberlain, glancing at his Times of a morning,
always looks first to see what Te Aroha is doing.
That was a dreadful crime alleged against
Albert Edward Macnamara, at Melbourne —
setting fire to his house to burn his wife and two
children (one of whom was killed), so as to collect
certain insurance-moneys. He was convicted
under the clause which makes it a capital-offence
to set fire to an inhabited house. Accused was
sentenced to death, but there seems room for a
shadow of doubt as to the man’s murderous inten-
tions, since he made a brave and successful effort
to save the second child.
Apropos fire-raising for fee. In Nov., ’B2,
at Sydney, Wm. Dunn was sentenced to death by
Judge Windever for causing the death of Mrs.
Wright by setting fire to the premises in which
she resided. Mrs. Wright’s death was caused by
jumping from an up-stairs window. Dunn had
frequently earned the five shilling fee for first
information and the fire-brigade supt. began to
suspect him. Dunn was reprieved and was
released after serving 15 years. He admitted that
he always spent the paltry fee in beer.
• * * *
The Lance divorce case (Newcastle,
N.S.W.) evoked one witness—a full-grown white
cabman wearing the British name of Smith —who
said on oath that he didn’t know what month he
was in or what next month would be, who couldn’t
tell the name of last month, who didn’t know what
month New Year’s Day fell in, and who declined
to commit himself as to the number of days in the
week or the names of the days.

A curious legal point is likely to arise in
England. Under the Child Messenger Act
children must not be sent to public-houses for
beer. Therefore the old man goes himself. He
stays. He gets drunk. In the end the mother
sends the child who used to fetch the beer to
bring the old man home. And seeing that the
child goes to the public-house for the old man,
and incidentally, for the beer that is in the old
man, is that sending the child to the public-house
for beer ?
One of the Q. S’Africa contingent was con-
victed in Brisbane last week of stealing jewellery
from a sleeping man. Accused said he couldn’t
help it (evidently had acquired the habit on the
veldt) and asked the Judge to let him off on con-
dition that he left for S’Africa at once. He was
sure he could make a living over there ! ! Judge
Noel gave him 12 months “with leave to apply”
on condition that he returned to the field of glory
—and unconsidered trifles.

"""The Jewish clergy in Sydney take the
“ mixed marriages ” question much more seriously
than do those of the Christian sects. Rabbi
Landau, preaching on the subject recently,
Exhorted the members of the Jewish Community not
to continue to countenance such marriages ; as the action
was a disgraceful thing. Jews or Jewesses who had con-
tracted these marriages should suffer social ostracism.
And he threatened further —
Although he hoped to end his days in the service of this
congregation, he would resign his connection with it and
seek another field for his usefulness if he could not very
shortly find some attention was given to this, his most
sincere appeal for assistance to stop this growing evil,

The flooding of the Cardigan mine at
Ballarat recalls the similar disaster at the New
Australasia mine at Oreswick, on Dec. 12th., ’B2 ;
but in the earlier case there were 22 drowned
miners. The fund raised for the sufferers by that
catastrophe reached £20,000, and though booin-
time bungling reduced it to £ll,OOO there are
still a few hundreds over annually after providing
for the widows and the few remaining infants.

Revolting piece of cable-grovel:—
Long-sentence prisoners,
with all their blushing broad -
arrows thick upon them,
are employed digging, weed-
ing, etc., about Hobart
Botanical Gardens. It is
also a common thing in
Hobart to see a procession
of loaded drays coming
in from the bush with
firewood for the "gaol
and other Government
institutions, each team
in charge of a shame-
faced chap in prison
clothes, and the whole
procession superinten-
ded by a warder in
uniform, who walks along
the pavement within re-
volver-range of the first and
last dray. It is probably
hereditary in Tas. rulers —this
desire to parade the prisoners in
Sublic; so like old times, you know.
governor Havelock, the recently ‘im-
ported occupant of the big brown
mansion on the banks of the Der-
went, evidently takes kindly to the local fashion,
for the prisoners are now employed as scrubbers
and sweepers within the Great House itself, even
in the drawing and bedrooms.
THE LITANY OF ANNNEXATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By smoking hearths on wasted plain,
’Mid tears of women shed, shed in vain,
In lust of loot and greed of gain,
Hear us, good Lord !
For foreign land, ’mid foreign sea,
To yoke a race that once was free,
To smite and grip, we pray to Thee,
Aid us, good Lord !
From sneer of friend and curse of foe,
From brand of avarice, hour of woe,
From mills that grind, increasing, slow,
Deliver us, Lord !
JOHN CAREW.

Hr ; My dear, I was dreaming last night
(Though it seems rather heartless to tell),
A dream of your first attempts—
Even now I remember them well:
Your puff-paste that wouldn’t puff,
Your jelly that didn’t quite jell—
Lost souls were eating them wearily
In the dining-room section of ——.
She : How strange too that I should have dreamt 1
(Though I don’t know how it befell)
A dream of your first attempts.
Is it strange I remember them well ?
Your metre that wouldn’t quite meet,
Your satire that didn’t quite tell—
Lost souls were conning them drearily
In the library section of .
PAULA.
THE KING’S GIFT.
London, March 19.
It has transpired that after last Friday night’s Court
reception King Edward, on his own initiative , forwarded
to the various hospitals the surplus viands and delicacies
for the patients.
The banquet-board is cleared again
And from the remnants Jeames and Jane
With bellies round go bedward :
Now from the rags of broken meat
The beggar tribe is bid to eat
And bless King Edward.
To those worn limbs whose wasted thews
Pay for what kingly stomachs use,
To inspire their noble wishes,
What Royal merit never earns
A Royal insolence returns —
In dirty dishes.
Yours be the scraps, poor hungry crew 1
Dukes must be fed, and flunkeys too,
If Kingship to survive is :
