A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 25,1902.
My Dear Arini,—
. About the biggest theatrical entertainment
m England is to take place at Westminster in a
month or two, and various magazines of the Bits
order, besides numerous local publications, are
breaking out into “Etiquette at Coronations”
and advice as to the number of gold knobs a
duchess must wear on her top-hamper as compared
with those on a countess. Papers with names
like the Bundaroq Advertiser are very anxious
and overworked nowadays, telling duchesses about
these knobs. The Duke of Norfolk, who, as grand
Keeper of Something or Hereditary Loser of
Somebody, has control over the ladies’ costuming,
some time ago fell publicly into the soup by
decreeing that the peeresses should wear —in
midsummer—ermine bodices, stiff, old-fashioned
kirtles, and red velvet mantles, but he has been
so severely warned of the folly of his proceedings
by the Hungerford Banner and the Boppy Moun-
tain Express that the shattered nobleman is
going “to see what he can do.” As most of the
duchesses—on the authority of a Maoriland agri-
cultural paper—are young and lovely, it is likely
that Norfolk will have to reconsider things a good
deal. There is a legend, by the way, that one
elderly widower of the peerage decided to marry
again, as it would not look respectful to Edward
VII. to be without a wife at the Coronation. The
nobleman who could marry out of a desire for
symmetry, and to please Edward’s sense of tidi-
ness in the peerage, should be a man worth know-
ing. _ He is probably descended from that sym-
metrical baron of long ago who, having only three
malefactors to hang at a place where four roads
met, hung a casual passer-by rather than have
these cross-roads looking unfinished and dis-
orderly.
Fifteen year old premier-Duke-of-Ireland
Leinster was doing the Jenolan Caves when the
eold snap of last week painted the Blue Mountains
white and froze him back to lower levels. The
boy of many titles, acres, and thousands has an
uncle, Lord Charles Fitzgerald, living in the
neighbourhood of Sunbury (Vic.) on a small patch
of land which he cultivates intensely. Primogeni-
ture law is a hardship for such as he. Lord
Cholly, who is a very fine-looking young man,
married, some ten years ago, a tall, thin girl, of
Greek extraction, who had been awhile on the
dramatic stage. When he and his consort were in
town they would would call upon his ma-in-law in
East Melbourne, he carrying the old baby and
Lady C. the new. Every one who knew them
said of them good things and that if Lord C.
hadn’t the strawberry leaves jn his hair, no better
man had. 1
So Mr. Frank Gardiner has married the
Parisian beauty whom his connoisseurish eye and
heart desired. As he appears to be worth many
millions he will not miss the one million that he is
said to have given his second wife, Carrie Swain,
to make a “ widower ”of him. His first wife, an
actress named Phillips and her (and his) family
reside in America, and for them also he has well
provided. And yet when Gardiner and Carrie
Swain were here, not so many years ago, pretty
well all the gold they had was the gold of Carrie’s
hair. King Edward VII and F.G. once went
motoring together but, so goes the story, they
don’t speak now. The King didn’t like being
snap-shotted on the swivel seat with the actor-
miner by the latter’s agents. Mrs. Browne-Potter
had made the Personage and the person
acquainted.
When Mrs. J. C. Williamson was leaving
Sydney on Sunday night to join her husband in
Melbourne, Redfern platform was crowded with
friends and sympathisers requesting her to tell her
husband “to keep his tail up.” The lady heard
them all in a resigned sort of manner, and then for
answer passed round her husband’s telegram:
“Don’t worry, Mammie. A great misfortune,
but we’ll pull through if God spares us our health.
My chief sorrow is for my people thrown out of
employment.” The tail of J.C.W. evidently still
waves.
Perhaps the most disagreeable revelation in the
Willoughby divorce case to the principals therein
was the publication of their real names—Dowse,
not Willoughby ! Reminds one, somehow, of a bag
of wet sand. But there was a Baron Dowse, an
eminent judge, so the petitioner dowses in good
company. The wife was alleged to have had an off-
hand, simple way of notifying important matters.
She informed her husband, then in S’Africa, by a
post card that “ she didn’t want anything more to
do with him.” No trouble of moistening en-
velope-flaps and stamps. You can say but little
on a p.c., but the import may be great.
The age of chivalry is not dead, but chivalrous
practice has only one really distinguished ex-
ponent in Sydney, an Em Pay of impressive
gallantry. T’other afternoon, as the last rays of
the setting sun were illuminating King-street,
this picturesque personage recognised, standing
in front of a florist’s shop, a young man of his
acquaintance talking with two attractive girls.
Magnificent was the sweep of his saluting hat as
the Em Pay greeted “moi dear-r-r young friend.”
Of course he was introduced to the girls. A
brief, though impressively friendly, interview fol-
lowed—the gallant man the while grasping, with
an air of playful paternalism, the arm of one of
the young ladies. After a space of some four
minutes he retired with another most elaborate
bow and disappeared into the florist’s shop close
by. Presently he emerged shining with gaiety,
carrying with empressement a paper-bag. Again
approaching the young ladies, the courtly saluta-
tion was repeated, the bag opened, and each of
the young dears presented with a charming spray
of flowers, which the donor, after asking permis-
sion with a deferential lowering of his crested
head, was allowed to admiringly fasten to their
fair forms. The gallant then floated away, leaving
behind him that subtle atmosphere of mature
charm which is usually associated with the
“ Indian summer ” of a beautiful woman. The
little scene smacked of the boulevards of Paris—
it was altogether incongruous in bustling, jostling
Sydney.
So many children wear sandals now that the
child sock-trade in Sydney must have fallen off.
After a morning about the city with ma the little
sandal-wearers’ bare legs and feet are very grimy
and must be germy. Why not let the youngsters
wear digitated hose like those worn by the
“ upper classes ” in “ Ben Hur ” ?
“ The law out here (Australia) allows an animal
to be kept 48 hours without food or water. In
England it is only 24.” So writes, in a local
paper, a former sec. to the Animals’ Protection
Society remonstrating with the Railway Commis-
sioners for not watering stock in transit. Think
of the difference between the Australian and
English climates, then ponder on the brutality of
the Australian law in respect to dumb animals !
Railway authorities in England and America are
compelled by law to water stock in transit; here
they may truck them for two days (under a broil-
ing sun) without bite or drink ! And when shunt-
ing occu rs it isoften done, as the writer remarks, with
such violence that animals are thrown from end to
end of the truck. It is a pity that the Animals’
Protection Society is not independent of Govt., so
that it could prosecute the Commissioners until they
were compelled to run stock-trains direct and
water the animals once in 24 hours. Those re-
sponsible deserve to be trucked through all
eternity with intervals for violent shunting.
A proof of what a factor is smart coiffing in a
girl’s appearance. Directors of a certain Sydney
restaurant employ hairdressers to build and wave
the collective hair of their waitresses. So these
girls have got the name of being a stylish-looking
phalanx. Also, they are without exception fair-
haired and thinnish. Perhaps as a counter
contrast to the girls in the restaurant on the other
side of the street, who are mostly dark-haired and
plump, with good complexions—-their very own
complexions. In yet another restaurant, one in
George-street, the employer’s fancy evidently runs
to stature and willowyness. All the girls are as
tall as the cedars of Lebanon were once.
When the “new figure” came in some three
years ago—when your body from your hips up-
wards was a sloping roof for your lower limbs —a
fashionable Melb. drapery store furnished its
employees (women, of course) with the best of
corsets that produced that outline and effect. But
they had to shed the sheaths every evening after
shop-hours. _ I suppose those once long-fronted,
slanting bodies now demonstrate the possession of
hips and abdomens by the wearing of the three-
inch length of corset-belts.
Returning to hair: Artificially fair sort must be
out of fashion. A Sydney draper told me that
two years ago his import order for “hair nets for
gold hair ” was 500 dozen. His latest order for
hair nets included only fifty dozen of the blonde
shade.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ ‘ The Dear Little Shamrock ’ had its most
fashionable hour on 17th at the Town Hall con-
cert, when the Hopetouns and the Irish of quality
rallied to the front, and sat in a framework of
greenery. The Countess, in white satin and
chiffon, with a faint sprinkling of diamonds, sat
cheek-by-jowl with her priestly countryman—Dr.
Carr. The pair talked between the green intervals
with the stately glass-walled reserve characteristic
of each. Smalkum M‘Eacharn’s lady was a shy
neighbor of a big-frontage Redemptionist Father,
and, as that dame has a soft corner in her heart
for the religion of rosaries, she chatted as easily
as might be the missionary. White Knight
Fitzgibbon was in evidence, together with all the
Jay Pay Ryans, Fitzgeralds, O’Loghlens, and
ardent local Hibernians.
“ The Countess drew in her horns considerably
when, to please the exuberant audience, her-
sprightly spouse dwelt on the subject of Irish
girls, relating how he followed ‘ Murphy’s ’
advice to choose a wife in the Emerald Isle. He
could record 16 years of perfect happiness as a
result of that choice, and he recommended all
men to do likewise. Two thousand hands and
feet at once stamped the advice with approval.
His Ex. hit the bull’s-eye to a nicety. That little
bit of palaver will carry him with credit in the
ledgers of Melbourne Paddy till the next 17th of
March.
“The best number of the Snake Saint’s pro-
gramme was Queenie Burroughes’ recitation. The
girl has a melodious voice and an Etna of dramatic
fire. But her appearance didn’t at all come up to
her talent. She stood with feet wide apart, and
she wore tan boots and stockings. Ballarat should
have really seen that so gifted a girl was better
shod. She needs to be entirely re-upholstered.
“Nellie Stewart, apparently, acts on the advice
‘Don’t go into the water at all, unless you make a
splash.’ T’other evening, when the Gov.-General’s
party patronised the Princess, she adver-
tised the gubernatorial ‘turn’ by flower-decora-
tions, Hopetoun blue and yellow lace drapes,
and the presence of powdered, padded flunkeys in
scarlet and green manning the crimson carpet for
the vice-regal pathway, and playing waxworks at
the head of the staircase. In the v.r. box the
Countess took off a gloomy pelisse to show a plain,
black evening-gown and long, white gloves,
stitched with black. Sartorial woe doesn’t suit
the dark Excellency, and as she doesn’t ‘do-up’ a
scrap, disdaining even a hint of powder, she
looked rather brown and massive-faced that
evening. However, she turned away her profile
as much as possible from public inspection, and
her neck aspect is always decorative, thanks to
effective black hair and small, neat ears.
“ From another bedizened-box Dorothy Vane
(Mrs. Menzies) peeped out from behind her
powder-pink cloak. In the d.c., Captain Val-
lange sat ‘all alone with himself together.’
Hopie, himself, seemingly, didn’t enjoy ‘Sweet
Nell ’ quite so much as a farce —say ‘ Tom, Dick
and Harry—though he cracked his thin face in a
cynical smile when jealous Charles, wishing to
cold-shoulder Nell Gwynne, leaned on Lady
Castlemaine on one side and on the Duchess of
Portsmouth on t’other. Nell was cute enough to
know that there’s safety in numbers.
“Another thing that must have made the
Hopetoun wink the eye on his off-side from the
audience was the rapturous approval of the house
when Charles says, ‘the king is the law.’ The
playwright, I suppose, inserted that touch as
characteristic Stuartism, little guessing that so-
called democratic hearers in Australia would not
only swallow his motto of old-fashioned abso-
lutism, but audibly smack their lips over it.
“ Miss Duff, daughter of the late Governor
Duff, of N.S.W., and sister of Captain Duff—the
cipher in the Hopetouns’ suite—has been staying
at the V.R. St. Kilda-road quarters. She is a
tame young thing, and struck recent polo gather-
ings as of small interest from the dress point of
view.
“ There was quite a refreshing number of men
sandwiched among the large petticoated audience
at Marshall Hall’s orchestral concert last Satur-
dao afternoon. Every part of the Town Hall was
comfortably filled, and the long, lean conductor,
with a thick shock of straight black hair inking
his head and three-quarters of his face, bowed
witli baton in hand to the full house. Mar-
shall Hall would look lugubrious were it
not for the expression of his high black
eyebrows, which seem lifted in a suspended
expression of quizzical laughter. ‘ Really ? ’ those
raised brows are saying, in a tone of laughing
incredulity. Herr Edouard Scharf’s contribution
was the delicious morsel of the high-class banquet
of sound. The pianist’s wife—formerly Olive De
Hugard— sat in the balcony, a wrapt soul to the
spell of a master’s touch. Mrs. Scharf is uncom-
monly fresh and pretty. Madame Wiedermann,
plumed in black voile over silk and side-
panels _of sparkling jet, with a well-chosen
head-piece of black chiffon and other inky
trimmings, also satisfied the souls of the
elite, and sent her pupils into violent ecsta •
cies of applause. Her Austrian husband—Carl
Pinschoff—sat in a front row and stamped his stick
stoutly at the close of Madarne’s warble.
“The dear Dani, with his wife, had a seat in
the balcony. The latter was richly decked out in a
cream toilette. The tenor regarded the audience
and the orchestra in turn over a high rail of linen
collar. Our old Melbourne tenor with the historic
name husbanded the Dani’s, and threw out his fat
chest with considerable gratification as he caught
some of the crumbs that fell about in an overflow
from the visiting Orpheus’ table. Near by were
Professor Peterson and his wife—the clever little
lady, who is so great a help to her hubbie, being
tricked forth in a smart biscuit and ink-spotted
foulard with heaps of lace for garnishing, a big
soft chiffon ruff, and a suitable black chiffon
toque. Down in the south gallery Mrs. Eccles,
the most decorative of Marshall Hall’s pupils,
languished, according to her wont, in a navy blue
rig-out, complements of black and blue about her
millinery helping to attain a smart effect. Dr.
Helen Saxton, the first medical woman in Mel-
bourne to ply the lancet, sat in her stolid, common-
sense, mannish style in a straw _ decker, white
dicky and white tailor-built garb, listening closely
to the numbers under way.
“ Major Bartrop, whose revered old snowy
’head is visible in the front rows at every high-
class concert in Melbourne, used his stick fre-
quently at periods of applause, and steered his way
over to Madame Wiedermann to give forth some
pleasant praise at the conclusion of her number.
What a hold the conductor obtains over his
audience ! He commands silence, and he gets it.
One breathes gently, coughs under cover; and dare
not ruffle a silk flounce while that autocrat’s baton
is on the beat.
“Lady Hutton, wife of the Federal Com-
mandant, had hardly shaken the dust of Pall
Mall from her trailing skirts, ere she was elected
to the catmittee of Melb. Women’s Hospital.
That body represents the very Holy of Holies of
Melbourne’s Naicest, and vies with the president-
ship of a golf-club as an emblem of social distinc-
tion. By the way, boycotted by all the young
medicoes, the catmittee is now advertising for an
experienced resident doctor at £250 a year. As
>no medico with any practice would find it worth
while to go for such a salary, it simply means that
the hospital subscribers will be asked to pay £250,
where they formerly paid nothing, for exactly the
same sort of man, and that this money is to be
annually thrown away merely to gratify the
vanity and obstinacy of a lot of meddling women.
“Italy’s newly-arrived Consul-General is one
Signor Camillo Bertola. The unconventional
Corte has departed, and on Tuesday night the
Dante clan welcomed Corte’s successor—an old,
bald-headed gentleman as much like James Smith
as one proverbial pea resembles its podded
brother.
“At that Dante shivoo, Cesca Vollugi, with her
plump figure, ruddy sunny hair, and sweetly
indolent style on the platform, was a real treat,
for she sang her very best, and Cesca’s best is
always charming. In the audience were one or
two social stars, such as Mrs. Dunbar Hooper,
who has enough foreign blood in her composition
and ideas in her brain to enable her to take a real
interest in the Dante Society gatherings. There
is nothing swagger or smart about the general run
■of members, and it is only once in a way that a
■few cultured souls of the more expensive class
come along to enjoy the good music provided, not
forgetting the sandwiches switched on at the
interval.
“Maclaren confessed himself perfectly sick of
•cricket during the final match at Adelaide. On
the last day of play he escaped, immediately after
being bowled, to the races at Port Adelaide, where
he scooped the pool with four winners. Mrs.
Maclaren, who looks carefully after hubby’s in-
terests, saw some of the cricket in the company of
the Tennyson troupe, and then went on to the
races with her self-satisfied lord.
“Rather a joke to read in Clement Scott’s
Free Lance of the prestige of England being
‘effectively maintained by the relatively great
success achieved by Mr. Maclaren’s personal
success, which has so far been one of the most
marked characteristics of the tour.’ This is surely
meant for satire. Most people seem to think the
captain’s ‘personal success’ amounted to zero.
“A young widow, with four children, whose
wealth is described as colossal, has come to Mel-
bourne, and the moths of Sassiety have already
begun their flutterings about her neighborhood.
The wealth is all in the children’s names, but the
mother is trustee, and derives a substantial income
for her maternal protection. At all the recent
race meetings and the polo carnival, Melbourne’s
bachelor of the Forum shepherded her closely.
The interesting stranger, who may be induced to
take notice again, and has emerged from her
purple period of lamentations, is tall and slender,
and just a little—in figure and quiet style—like
the cold Countess. She is going to toast Toorak’s
toes at wintry feasts in her new abode out that
way.
“Sassiety is to be enlivened by a swagger
marriage next month, the principals being Miss
May Harper, the dark-eyed, olive-skinned and
most debonnair daughter of merchant Robert
Harper—(of starch renown) —and Mr. Alfred
Taylor, son of an old Parliamentarian. The
merchant, who does a little in the manufacturing
line, has not all the stiffness attributed to his
starch, but his wife boasts a somewhat rigid back-
bone and considerable gloss. Mrs. Harper has the
air of ‘ La grande dame,’ uses her aristocratic pair
of glasses on an ebony stick, and keeps up at
‘ Myoora,’ Toorak’s toniest mansion, the state of a
Duchess. None of the maternal mannah, how-
ever, has descended to her only daughter. The
bride-elect is as breezy as the capricious and chic
daughter of a certain dear old Judge who is chiefly
known to Sassiety as the father of a dashing
beauty.
“ Rumored that the brilliant and distinguished-
looking representative in Melbourne of Sydney
D.T., for so long a bachelor, has at last succumbed
to Cupid, and will shortly wed a charming and
well-to-do widow. I trust that the lady is not so
rich as to take her husband out of the journalistic
ranks—-for the sake ofjthe public of your
city.
“Miss. Hilda Cumniing will sdon mourkCthe
absence of her fiancee, Lieut. W. Robertson, who
starts off with the Victorian wing of the Common-
wealth warriors. Another engaged girl bids a
weeping au revoir to her best boy, and two or
three charmers who have been greatly in evidence
at the Sunday and holiday visits to the camp at
Langwarrin will take damp farewells this week.
“ A nail-hammering contest for women and a
hat-trimming contest for men were among the
side-shows at a St. Kilda fete the other day. The
picture of a woman with a hammer in her hand,
and a look of set purpose on her face, is always a
funny business, whether it be in a serious case of
urgency or casual competition. It was more
foolish than funny to see the mere male building up
a millinery structure. Captain Dangar tried his
hand at it, and convulsed the giddy young specta-
tors. This is ju-it about the fat, frolicsome cap-
tain’s style. The giggles moved him to greater
awkwardness, and he pretended to make Herculean
exertions before he twisted the trimmings into
shape. Lady Hutton opened the little shivoo,
which was organised to help the lo'S-water funds'
of the St. Kilda Girls’ Club. \
“The scribesses of Melbourne are striving to
form themselves into a club, to engender better
fellow feeling and all that sort of thing; besides
getting a common place of meeting, and a field of
tongue operations, for not content with pen talk
they want the tongue, too, in active service. Two
or three scouts are out looking for rooms, and
meanwhile some eighteen or twenty journalists
and women writers are preparing to enjoy club
privileges.
“ The pen-ladies ran, in a well-gowned:and des-
perately punctual crowd, to thresh out their pro-
posed club. But they will have none of the Salon,
a majority of everyone else to one politely but
firmly declining the honor of ‘reciprocal relations’
with that luxurious home of the middle class
dame. Things in trousers were talked of, not with
bated breath, but with the good-humored cynicism
of the Newest Woman. For the present they are
to be barred, but if they’re wanted later on, to
quote one ingeneous damsel, they can be asked in.
“Deaf-and-dumb-mission promoters want to
build an institute for the adult deaf-mutes of Mel-
bourne. The deaf-and-dumb schools dump their
scholars on the world at the age of sixteen,
strangers in a strange land, cut off by their mis-
fortune from the rest of us. The institute as pro-
posed will combine in itself the advantages of a
social club —employment bureau, relief-agency,
lecture-hall, etc. At an entertainment to raise
funds, held in the Town Hall, t’other night, we
saw three methods of deaf-mute communication.
(1) Oral, (2) language of signs, (3) finger alphabet.
Signs are the most attractive. It’s quite pretty
to see a band of girlish spirits in prison reciting
by gesture ‘ Will you meet me at the fountain ? ’
delineating most gracefully with their hands the
rising and falling of the fountain-spray. Sign-
communication also merits approval as the best
system for brightening up the general appearance
of those who use it. Face expression, smiles,
arm-gestures, attitude, all come into play instead
of mere finger-drill. A clever deaf-mute, Mr.
Muir, grandson of the late Dr. Cairns (a well-
known local pillar of the kirk), did ‘ The Village
Blacksmith ’ in sign-language.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

The Bulletin was recently knocked flat
by a machine called the Burroughs Adding and
Registering Machine, of which the Commercial
Banking Co., of, Sydney use seven. It has a key-
board of figures'as a typewriter has letters; and
these are arranged in numerical order from one
half-penny up to one million pounds. To add
on the machine, you print off the amounts as you
strike off words on the typewriter, then you press
a key and the machine thereupon prints the exact
total. The machine is infallibly correct. Users
after a little practise can manipulate the machine
at the rate of two thousand cheques or dockets an
hour and have the total thrown m, while with the
pen they could not write the amounts let alone
add them. The United Typewriter and Supplies
Co., of Sydney, Melbourne and Perth, is the
concern responsible for introducing the benefits of
this machine to Australia. They state that a
large number'of Banks,- and many of the leading
Drapery establishments in Sydney and Melbourne,
such as David Jones and Co., Ball and Welch,
Craig, Williamson and Thomas have them in use
for listing and adding up the daily dockets.*
Ladies recommend Martin’s Apiol and
Steel Pills. All chemists sell them throughout
Australasia. Agents for N.S. Wales: Sayers,
All port and Potter, Sydney. Sole proprietor, W.
Martin, pharmaceutical chemist, Southampton,
England.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Bill Lyne—the rogue-elephant of Aus-
tralian politics.
Still does the daily press hang on the
sacred utterances of General Hutton. You would
think the Messiah had arrived.
“ McMullock” is a Melb. Herald mis-
print for M'Culloch, Public Works Minister and
rabid Jingo. The sinful comp, strikes the eternal
truth at times.
Rumored that C. A. Lee, leader of N.S.W.
State Opposition, thinks of giving up politics and
entering the church. Solemn Lee must surely be
going to do something.
A lot of the men who are talking of
“founding a great daily newspaper” in Sydney
just now, have never had five shillings to spare to
register themselves as the sorry dogs that they are.
Premier See has accepted a seat on the
Ocean Accident Assurance Board. The number
of directorships which Mr. See holds is marvellous.
He is prabably the champion “guinea-pig” of
Sydney.
Of the Federal Parliamentarians, Conroy
is the most heard, Keating the most seen. Turn
up a photo, group of Federal politicians, and the
smooth and curly Tasmanian will be found right
in the eye of the camera every time.
A Sydney suburban paper :
The deceased gentleman leaves one widow and five
children.
Well, now-a-days, such particularity in the enu-
meration of a man’s relicts is not utterly indefensi-
ble.
Henniker Heaton defends himself in
the London newspapers against Premier Barton’s
charge of “intermeddling.” Henniker doesn’t
say, as he might have (for H.H. is equal to it) that
an old club quarrel made Barton particularly glad
to tread on his corns.
HER AVERAGE WAS HIGHER.
THE MISSUS : “ When the gentlemen were going
away last night I distinctly heard someone
being kissed twice in the hall.”
THE MAID : “It wasn’t me, ma'm. Nobody
kissed me only twice, ma’m!”
John Norton announces in his paper his
“serious illness” in Brisbane—two operations
within a week, and a third pending. Yet, withal,
he publishes in the same issue eight columns of
self-signed articles ! It is a very industrious man
that scribbles thus between “serious” operations.
In making the appointment to the High
Commissionership in London the Federal Govern-
ment should be careful not to add to the drinking
strength of Australian officialdom there. Some
of the State representatives already in England
can do all the drinking that is required.
An Englishman of a noted family, who, in
the days of Gov. Bowen, was a well-known and
much-admired man of fashion in Brisbane, and a
favorite at Govt. House, was the other day sent
from Dunwich poorhouse—of which he had long
been an inmate —to the leper-station half-a-mile
away on the same island.
Arthur Galton, who was private secretary
at Sydney to his kinsman, Gov. Duff, is now
Srivate chaplain to the Anglican Bishop of Ripon.
Ir. Galton was originally ordained an Anglican
clergyman, but left that church for the R.C. faith.
Then he reverted to Anglicanism, of which he is
now a most militant champion.
Despite the Argus and Age branding of
King O’Malley as a mere empty blatherskite who
travels on the size of his hat, it is a fact that the
O’M. never has to talk to empty benches—nor
empty galleries either. Some of the allegedly
strong men of the Federal Parliament would give
much for a similar reputation.
Absolutely the best-dressed, best-groomed,
and most portentously imposing person in the
Federal House is Malkum M'Eacharn. His
frock-coats and fancy waistcoats in the afternoons,
and his lovely evening clobber, are the envy of all
other smartly-dressed members. A gallery scribe
fittingly described him—“ The Immaculate S’Mal-
kum Mack : the one perfect thing in an imperfect
world.”
There should be joy in the law-ranks at
the prospective establishment of the Federal High
Court—five Judges, one at £3500, and four at
£3OOO ; five associates, a marshal and deputy mar-
shals, a registrar and deputy-registrar in all,
about £33,000 a year in salaries, and the proba-
bility of plenty costly litigation.
The widow of Mr. Michael Stokes, one of
the founders of S. M. Herald (he died about 10
years, ago), passed away last week in Sydney,
where she was born in 1809, a year before the still-
surviving Dean Cowper. An older native than
the Dean still lives—William Byrne, of Princess-
street, Sydney, born at Appin in 1808.
Reported in Melbourne that the benighted
Quick is to have a Federal High Court billet.
Since his beknightment Quick has steadily drifted
away from democracy ; in politics he is now not
quick but dead ; and the Federal High Court,
from all appearances, is to be the fashionable
mausoleum for dead political reputations.
Even eminent King’s Counsel at times
forget themselves. In Adelaide Supreme Court,
t’other day, when Symon, K.C., submitted his
“personal belief” to the jury, Nesbit, K.C.,
objected that it was not evidence, and, in a burst
of ingenuousness, added, “My learned friend
believes what he is paid to believe, like every
other lawyer.”

thing, by the way, to arrange with De La Rey to

war is over”—Hutton’s term of engagement will
have expired by then.
Mr. J ustice Stephen, from a hint he re-
cently gave, is expected to retire from the N.S.W.
bench in May, when his pension becomes due.
Mr. Justice Owen is due for pension, 18th Oct.,
and will also, it is believed, retire. Mr. Stephen
was 73 in December last, and Mr. Owen is 68.
Acting-Justice Pring will, no doubt, get a per-
manent appointment; others eligible have their
eyes on the Federal High Court.

A N.S.W. paper complains with solid
reason:
The officers of the present contingent are a lot of
youngsters, and, according to all accounts, were ap-
pointed through outside influence. The Brigadier called
them together, and openly told them to perfectly under-
stand he had no voice in appointing them, and there-
fore would not be responsible !
Jacob Garrard, J. F. Burns, and Joe Cook
carry life passes over N.S.W. railways for their
‘ ‘ distinguished political services ” to that State.
Brunker and Mary Jane Gould also run free.
Thus, if they gave themselves wholly up to travel-
ling the country with samples of tea or softgoods or
to buy fats for butchers, they would have a consider-
able start over all their business competitors.

crown is surely the testimonial of attorney
“Honest Tom” Slattery, given at the Thaddeus
picture-testimonial presentation :
Mr. O’Sullivan’s record was an enviable one. As a hus-
band there was none better, as a father a model, as a
friend true as steel, and as a public man he had as clean a
record as any man in the world.
Talk about praise from Sir Rupert!
Baronet Clarke is to have a statue at Mel-
bourne, the site being in the Treasury Gardens,
facing Spring-street. What S’William did to
deserve a statue,.except to own a great deal of
money, even his most enthusiastic admirer
couldn’t tell. Certainly he was the most gentle-
manly-lnokmg of all the Victorian nobility and
didn’t seem to lose his dignity even when he
walked down Collins-street with a brown-paper
parcel in his hand.
About the most uncomfortable time
Treasurer Turner ever spent was when he first
donned Windsor uniform on a very hot night to
go to Melb. Govt. ’Ouse. The perspiring Turner
leaned across the turkey and things and asked
Peacock the Mirthful how he liked Ids togs,
whispering that he had found it necessary to leave
off everything underneath but his singlet. That
was probably the only time that a shirtless
Treasurer dined with the Duke of York. There
was a time when a Premier without any trousers
on moved the adjournment of N.S.W. Assembly
—but that is another story.
A FELLOW-FEELING.
THE ACCUSED: “ You see, me and Jenkins was
’avin' a bit of a time together the night afore,
and next mornin ’ — well, yer Worship knows
as ’ow a man makes a bee-line for the water-
jug.” (Discharged with a caution.)
This was Barton’s epitaph on the late James
P. Garvan :
An entirety of life adorned with consistent principle,
fitted up in the discharge of virtuous duty, with nothing
to conceal; no friendship broken, no confidence betrayed;
no timid surrender to popular clamor, no eager reaches
for popular favor.
Which probably represents Premier Barton’s
ideal of a public man. Unfortunately, some men
stumble over many things in the pursuit of an
ideal.
The grandson of one of Australia’s greatest
Judges, now a larcenist of some standing, and of
considerable gaol experience, was re-captured
recently in Tasmania after escaping from custody.
The old man took a leading place at the bar,
and afterwards took his seat on the Bench. The
son took to drink, and in between his orgies he
took the pledge. He also took the horrors. The
grandson took things that didn’t belong to him.
A curious streak of heredity might possibly be
traced here.
“Bob R.” : Mention of “Dicky” Threl-
fall reminds me of a yarn which is characteristic
of that distinguished scientist, who fairly detested
anything in the way of “ side.” Richard had just
commenced his lecture when in strolled a haughty
student resplendent in white pants and gaudy
“blazer” and a tennis-racket. Dicky stopped
until the apparition had taken its seat, and then
his raucous voice was heard : “Mr. , will you
step down here for a moment.” Dicky’s word was
law. “Now stand there; ladies and gentlemen,
look at it.” To the student: “ Turn round, pleasej
a back view, ladies and gentlemen ! Now, sir,
continued the burly Richard with sudden fury,
indicating the door, “get out! and never you dare
to mistake my lecture-room for a circus again.
When you come here come in the garb of a gentle-
man, not of a clown.”
A Vic. Senator, renowned for the inco-
herence of his utterances, made a vigorous attaok
recently on the enemies of the Empire,
and compared the said Empire to what
sounded like “Herr Colors” triumphing
over his foes. The Hansard reporter,
being unacquainted with any country-
man of Sandow’s possessed of such
strength, consulted the heated legislator
when the House rose, and asked him
which German he had been alluding to.
“ W’at! w’at!” cried the legislator;
‘ ‘ don’t you know how to spell Herr
Colors? I never heard of such ignor-
ance in my life. I don’t know how some
of you fellows keep your billets. Never
heard of Herr Colors ? Why, it’s spelt:
H-i-r-c-u-l-l-e-s —Herr Colors.”
In Cooktown (Q.), where the white
man is said to lose his energy, one publio
servant is “ Police-Magistrate, Warden,
Deputy-Marshal, Deputy-Sheriff, Min-
eral Lands Commissioner, Inspector of
Pacific Islanders and Harbor Master.”
How many more things he might be but for his
exhausted condition it is difficult to imagine.
The Evolution of King Seddon ! As a
working miner—on the early goldfields—Seddon
was a red-hot Socialist. Later on, when, as a
politician, he began to acquire some coin, his
socialistic views had to give way to “liberalism.”
Now, after being Maoriland’s Premier for some
years, he is reputed to be worth £50,000, and he
has blossomed into the blindest Imperialist this
side of the line. In all his long life Seddon never
once offered his carcase to be shot at by Her
Majesty’s enemies in any capacity whatever.
Customs Minister Kingston received a
terrible shock by the death of his only son, to whom
he was devotedly attached. The cause was con-
sumption. The victim—a bright, handsome youth
of 18 —was as tall as his father and was his con-
stant companion when Federal duties allowed
C.C.K. to get to Adelaide. During the later
stages of the illness Kingston spent all his time
either at the bedside or within call and he only
returned to Melbourne when it was thought that
the crisis had passed. The death was very sudden
at the end. Only his intimate friends know how
infinitely tender is Kingston’s nature under his
aggressive demeanor.
There is quite a touching friendship
between Premier Seddon and Governor Ranfurly
in M.L. Every session now Parliament is asked
to vote sums for the Governor which should come
from His Ex.’s own pocket. And on Ranfurly’s
side there are, of course, compensations. When
Ranfurly went away over a year ago to “ annex ”
the Cook Islands, Stout, C.J., became deputy-
Governor, and he made things so warm for King
Dick that Ranfurly will hardly be allowed off the
chain again before his term expires. Stout and
Seddon don’t drink in the same bar nowadays.
This is only a figure of speech, of course, for
Seddon is able to afford a private room these
times, and Stout quenches his honorable thirst at
the pump.
Musical critics Nesbitt and Morrison,
respectively of Melb. Age and Argus, are
notably versatile men. Nesbitt, whose adulatory
criticism gave the Castles her first boom, goes in
for a queer combination of music and mathematics,
besides much writing on astronomical and general
topics. He has just been appointed mathematical
lecturer at Trinity College, and teaches harmony
and counterpoint, as well as advanced hydro-
statics, at various seminaries. Critic Mor-
rison, a barrister, does some law re-
Sorting, and is perhaps unequalled in
lelbourne as a modern linguist. He
will probably succeed the late Prof.
Morris in the University chair of lan-
guages, if the governing pedants do
not fitly decide to abolish the teaching
of modern lingoes, and restrict them-
selves to pawing over the remains of
the dead speech of dead peoples.

called to the whisky-bar.
E. F. Knight, by far the most capable of the
mediocre band of journalists sent to this country
by London dailies with the mannikin Duke,
devotes nearly all the Australian section of his
book (and that the largest) to “the marvellous
loyalty of Australia to the Throne and Empire.
But Mr. Knight moved in a narrow circle of tuft-
hunting politicians and rich vulgarians. _ He
wouldn’t meet any “National” Australians,
partly because one of their characteristics is a
strong antipathy for the “ haw ” sort of English-
man. It comes somewhat as a surprise, after
hearing such stories of the pains taken by English
journalists to secure accuracy, to find many glar-
ing errors of fact in Mr. Knight’s work. Good
Australian press notices are secured for the work
by unlimited slobber of Jingo local journals
Cable to the dailies :
Mr. Gilbert Parker, M.P., in a letter to the Times,
doubts the financial success of colonial remount depots
during, peace, and advocates as an experiment the
colonies and the mother-land co-operating' for naval and
military defence with common financial responsibilities.
Mr. Parker also suggests the establishment of recruiting
stations for Imperial forces serving in the colonies and
upon acting foreign service.
Why on earth Australia should be asked to take
any notice of the opinions of Mr. Gilbert Parker,
M.P., on horse-questions is as unfathomable a
mystery as that the Sydney Morning Herald,
during his associate-editorship of that paper,
should have been gulled into printing as an
original article and as an introduction to the
description of Melbourne Cup meeting for the
then current year, the first chapter of Ouida’s
novel “Chandos.” Mr. Parker, so far as Sydney-
ites are aware, doesn’t know one end of a horse
from the other. He originally turned up in these
parts as the Rev. Horatio Gilbert Parker, elocu-
tionist and lecturer, a nice, lady-like blue-eyed
curate sort of personage, and presently became
attached to the Herald staff. Soon he left Aus-
tralia and later on made a considerable literary
success with certain French-Canadian stories.
Then he was heard of as having married the
daughter of a rich N. Y. Japanese curio-dealer;
now he sits in the legislative halls adorned by
Henniker Heaton, and patronises this beastly
country.

“Viator” confesses some of his sins to
The Bulletin :
Stone-broke, owing the liquor-dispenser a
week’s board, I flitted from Townsville (N.Q )
making for Ingham (80 miles) per boot. "
Tramped first 15
miles at the
double, and
reached a
dairy at
6 a.m.
Found a
large he-
woman
cleaning
up after
half-a-
hundred ,
cows.
Tackled
her for a
job, hop-
ing to get
a feed,
anyhow. “ I want
a man,” she said.
“The chap I ’ad
’as left. He was
here three years,
and was getting
6s. a week. Would
you like the job ? ”
“Yes,” I replied.
So she brings out a
large plate of
meat and bread
and a pint-pot
•of tea. Finished
—I hand back
plate, shoulder
“Matilda,” and
make for the
road. “Where are
you off to ? ” she sings
out. “ Oh,” said I, “ I’m only going
to take my morning: constitutional. What do you think ?”
At sundown, struck the humpy of a “ pumpkin-squatter,”
and put up a yarn about losing a horse. “ Muse charge
you a shillin’ a time,” was the answer, “ the ticks have
ruined me —lost 500 head in two years ! ” Salt goat,
damper, post-and-rail tea. “ Meal ” finished in perfect
silence. “ You can ‘doss’ there,” pointing to a ragged
stretcher, with a dilapidated blanket. Left about mid-
night, and struck an “idle hut” at daylight. Here I
stayed two days of constant rain, and was then driven
out by moisture, mosquitoes, and starvation. By this time,
the country was ankle-deep and all the creeks bankers.
A 12-mile tramp brought me to two tents, in which were
two wretched travellers, lying rolled up in blankets, wet
through. They had been there two days, foodless. Three
miles further on, struck a station. Put up horse-yarn
again. Had dinner, and stayed the night. Next day,
crossed a river up to my neck, and then waded through
flood-water for five miles up to my waist. Fortunately
struck the road, and, at 10 p.m., came to a church, and
camped in the porch. When I eventually reached Ingham,
42in. of rain had fallen in seven days, and 23in. in 24 hours
at Macknade plantation. What would the Westerners say
to that ? This was in the first week of January last.

“Yar.” : I can remember when M’s.P.
were thought a lot of, and when, if one showed
himself in a country town, there’d be a general
turning out of the inhabitants to gaze upon the
great man. T’other day one drove into pub. yard
here, and attended to his own horse. Traveller
asked who the gentleman was, and the landlord
said it was the auctioneer. “But isn’t he member
for the district?” asked the bagman. “Well,”
carelessly replied Bung, “I believe he is some-
thing of that kind, too.”
A voice from Marble Bar :
TheNor’-Westcan be appropriately termed Hell, especi-
ally Marble Bar. It consists of 45 people—white, black, and
piebald. Heat remarkable ; have often seen it 124 in the
shade. There are two pubs. The publicans, when things
are slack, sit in the middle of the road (150 ft. wide), and
play cards for drinks, loser shouting in opponent’s house.
There are four women in the town, generally at logger-
heads. No school. Children brought up in ignorance
and bliss. No church, but Holy Joe preaches on Sunday
nights under the only tree in the street. This tree is
called the policeman’s tree, because at one time the
coppers used to stand (by compulsion) under same, and
watch the pubs. The lads (some half-dozen or so) muster
around, lounging on the grass—when there is any. Joe
always has a lamp when preaching ; it is suspended to a
branch of said tree. He reads the Gospel by the aid of
this light, and every now and then there is an interrup-
tion while he lends the lantern to the congregation which
wants a light for its bumper. The stories of the hard-
ships endured by the Nor’-West blacks are quite true.
Know a squatter whose manager is a member of the
Aboriginal Board. When things are slack, sends the
blacks (signed-on) whom he does not want, out on what is
termed pinki (or holiday)—that is to say, he stops their
rations and turns them out where there is no food till he
wants them again. Saw a mob of 35 at one time on such
pinki. They were starving, diseased and naked. Was a
policeman at the time, and reported the matter. Never
heard any more about it. Presumably, report thrown in
w.p.b. Great habit among the white teamsters (there are
black teamsters) is to commandeer a young gin, whose
hair they invariably crop. Then they rig her out in male
attire, as a blind. She acts as musterer, cook, and other
things. I sometimes suspect that most of the black boys
here are girls. They get no wages, but plenty of work.
* * * *
“ Atriplex ” : Re curious things found in
the stomachs of cattle (B. 20/7/’01). Some years
ago, at Hawkesbury Agricultural College, a herd
of about 40 young heifers were pastured in a
swampy paddock, in which the local volunteer
rifle-butts are situated. A number of them un-
accountably died, and post-mortems disclosed
fragments of bullets in every carcase opened—one
stomach producing no less than 3£lbs. of lead.
Present writer once took an ordinary salmon-tin
from a cow’s throat, wherein it had become
embedded, a growth having formed around it.
Cattle will eat anything if they can’t get plenty of
salt.
A FINANCIAL DIFFICULTY.
OLD BUSHY : “ Say, boss, you’ll want a man in
my place when ice strike the Border .”
DRPVER: “ What’s up? Health cronk, old
fellow?”
O.B. : “ Well, no; but it’s just this way — I’ll be

“ Riverina ” : I have an idea which I con-
sider brilliant and original. It is to form a
settlement, consisting of three buildings—and, of
course, the inevitable pub.—somewhere in the
back-country. The money necessary can be
taken from the sums recklessly thrown about in
the “ Aboriginalities ” page—mostly consisting of
unaccepted challenges. The first of these build-
ings shall be a large hall, wherein Mr. M'Cooey
will lecture on natural history—admittance, one
lizard. M'Cooey shall be expected to make, in
the course of half-an-hour’s lecturing, at least six
insulting statements regarding other scientific
Aboriginaliars. Round the corner shall be the
second building. This Mr. Grant Hervey shall
occupy with his following. He shall be employed
in contradicting Mr. M‘Cooey, andsneeringlyallud-
ing to his Latin. At other times he will sally out
and slaughter Mac’s lizards (including the “pet” if
possible) and give Darling pea to M'Cooey’s crow-
ing beetles, crimson-crested serpents, iguanas,
whizzlegophers, &e. The rest of the aboriginaliars
shall lecture in the third hall, the biggest liar get-
ting possession oftenest; and they shall all contra-
dict M‘Gooey, and Grant Hervey, and each other,
and tell eel-stories. At the adjacent pub. liquor
will be sold which will enable the students to see
all the animals that M‘Gooey says lie saw and
that the other people say he didn’t see. Thus the
Australian Aboriginal University will be estab-
lished and will boom greatly, and the bodies of
the students who succumb to intense study will
be buried aboriginally with great corroboree, and
their kidney-fat hung on the tree overshadowing
the fountain where the ’roo laps in peace, and in
that tree the bell-bird can be a minah if he wants
to, and if he doesn’t want to he needn’t.
“ Myall ” : The minah (spoken of with un-
deserved kindness in Bulletin) is often fervently
cursed by kangaroo and wallaby shooters for its
assiduous attention to the safety of
bush animals. It acts as an unpaid
and uncalled-for sentinel, and got
pipes an unmistakable note of warn-
ing on the approach of danger.
Have often, after much trouble,
within range of a’roo, only to have
my chance of a shot spoiled by the
screech of a minah, which is a
bird that doesn’t attend to its
own business, and isinterferin
enough to be on the commit-
tee of a benevolent society.
“ Jack Cook, the hoy evangelist who made
such a stir in America and England, intends to
visit Australia.”— NEWS ITEM.
It’s a good thing that all the doddering
old octogenarians and centenarians of Australia are
not quite so “ willing ” as that violent specimen at
Colac (Victoria). Although over 85, he cheerfully
took on the job of shifting a lady and gentleman
out of an orchard the other day. The lady was on
the ground giving directions ; the gentleman was
up the tree. Old 85 ambled over to them and
dealt freely with the lady first ; then, when the
gentleman rushed down out of the tree to her
assistance, the old fellow biffed him one in the
eye that will leave it black for many a day. It’s
an interesting country where a man doesn’t know
the day lie’ll be assaulted by a great-grandfather.

Essay on the rabbit, by a N.S.W. country
hoolbov '
„ ~ ’ , . . . . .
Rabbits are very good at running. They are fond of
eating vedgetables. They make burrows very deep with
their claw’s, they have long ear’s, they can hear the guns
going off. They are very good eating, for currys and all
other things. As soon as they hear the guns going off,
they rush as hard as they can. The rabbits stommock is
very funnier than the other animals. Evry rabbit has the
same kind of stommock The rabbit has thinner legs
than all others. Animals have thicker legs than the
rabbit.

“ Scar ” : Once, near Lake Eyre (S. A.), I
met a blackfellow who, after telling me that he
had lately been working on the Cooper’s Creek
mission station (Moravian), asked for tobacco. I
had none, and told liim so. He was incredulous
and persisted, and at length, with uplifted arm
and finger pointing to the zenith, he sternly
asked: “Don’t you know ’im God? That fellah
God killum you blanky quick s’pose you tellum
lj e y>
“ JEM?.” : I once set my dogs on
to a stag on the banks of the Motueka
River (M.L.), when the bird, instead of
rising, dived into the water. It soon
after rose and disgorged a trout, which
I secured, and found that it weighed
4yb. Looks as if the shag had to get rid
of this burden before it could fly.
“ Trotter : In some parts of the
land of the hot, brown wild cat, they
chain prisoners up to a post, not posses-
sing a lock-up. Recently, in Coolgarra
(N.Q.), I saw a man so detained. When
it came on to rain heavily the kindly
policeman shoved him under a house,
and chained him there till it cleared up.
Even in this sorrowful land, the finer
feelings of humanity are not extinct.

“ Uloola ” : Re native bees
(“ W.5.T.,” 8., 10/8/’01.) Can’t see the
appropriateness of that nest in Sydney
Botanic Gardens being in the trunk of a
stinging-tree, since the native bee doesn’t
sting —only nips. As “ W.S.T.” says,
the native bee is about the size of the
small house-fly, and totally unlike the
English or Italian varieties. The “nest”
is made in a knot of a hollow limb, and
the quickest way to locate one, when you
come across a likely tree, is to get the
tree between yourself and the sun;
otherwise, you might pass one a dozen
times without discovering it. The comb,
which is irregularly built, is of a dark
color, but the honey is as clear as
schnapps, and very rich.
Wellington Evening Post :
A remarkable sight is to be seen right alongside the
roadway which leads around Waikaremoana. This is a
rata tree, a branch of which has picked up, and is sup-
porting a huge rock weighing about 20 tons.
A tree that steals rocks is at least a novelty.

“D.H.R.”: Re heavy pulls for horses.
A draught horse, owned by a Walgett
firm, some few days ago pulled a boiler,
weighing a little over five tons, up a slight
incline at a wool-scour four miles from town.
About 30 men witnessed the feat. [And wasn’t
one of the thirty humane enough to shove behind ?
-Ed. B.]
Re the precocious Australian kid. One of
him, aged nine years, was up before a country
court t’other day charged with causing a bush fire.
In evidence it transpired that accused, desiring a
smoke, and having but one match, and, deciding
to take no chances of it failing him, set fire to
a handful of grass, from which he lit his
pipe ! Result, £2 or seven days. Aged nine,
and unable to do without his pipe in a parched
and withered paddock !
* * » *
“Australis”: Re leeches (“Wer,” B.
20/7/’01). In the Neerim-Noojee districts of
Gippsland leeches swarm. The country is
not swampy, but inclined to be mountainous.
The leeches frequent the dark, wet gullies,
where the wire-grass and scrub are thick,
and if you stand here for a couple of
minutes you will see them coming for
you in dozens. In the winter milking
cows prefer scrub country to pasture,
and as a result they get covered with
leeches. Have taken four out of one
cow’s teat, and 80 per cent, of the ,cows
in above-mentioned districts a few years
ago were blind from this cause in at least
two of their teats. A leech of this kind
usually has a good “fill” of your blood
before you find it out. Was one day work-
ing in the scrub, and felt a tickling in my ear.
It was a leech, which my father had much
trouble in extracting.
* » * *
“V.M.”: Some bush youngsters are
cruelly worked. I know one little chap of 11, on
a Richmond River farm, who has to get up at 4,
round-in the cows in the dark, milk 15 himself,
harness up a horse and cart, and drive the milk to
the factory to be “ separated.” Then lie goes to
school till 3 ; when he runs home, saddles a horse,
rounds-in the cows again, milks his 15 and gives
them some green stuff. After tea he husks corn,
in the season, for an hour or two, and then drags
his tired little carcase to bed. When calves are
being reared, he has to poddy-feed his share daily,
and I’ve seen him holding the plough, while his
sister (aged 12) drove the bullocks. Asked the
local teacher how the youngsters could learn
under these conditions. “They don’t,” he said.
“It takes me all my time to keep the poor little
beggars awake. ”
“D.T.”: Re shoeing of bullocks (8.,
29/6/’01). A timber-getter on the Brunswick
River regularly shod his team. I used to make
the shoes for him. They were made in two halves,
without clip, and were put on with six No. 5
nails. I used regularly to shoe some Welsh
drover-bullocks in the old country in the same
way.
MISS MARIE NARELLE.

“Vril”: Re Chinese Josses. In the
middle ’9o’s there was one at Stanthorpe (Q.),
which informed a Chow acquaintance of mine
that his “Tatt.” tickets on Melb. Cup were no
good, and advised him to hurry up and get more.
This Ah Sin did, trading the first lot off to* a
Christian Chow. The second lot drew £2OOO, and
the first lot nil. This Joss, however, grew lazy
from surfeit of offerings ; so, after he had allowed
their gardens to do a bad perish, his disciples laid
hands upon him and burnt him with ignominy.
Then they set up another —duly advising him of
his predecessor’s fate, and asking for immediate
moisture. The new Joss rose to the occasion with
a well-distributed four-inch downpour.
THE BEERS WE USED TO DRINK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where are the Beers of long ago ?
I often deep and darkly ponder ;
Where are the Beers that used to flow ?
Where are they now ? I guess and wonder.
Lie they at rest in tombs afar
Along with thoughts we used to think ?
Say, Bacchus—tell us where they are,
Those long, cool Beers we used to drink ?
Do they, re-drawn all far away,
Scent fairy-fashioned Hebes’ bowers 1
Or fail to cheer the dead men gray
With brown and softly-falling showers ?
Perhaps behind some spectral bar
They’re quaffed with winks we used to wink,
And wealthy goblins globular
Drink ghosts of Beers we used to drink !

“ Entomologist ” writes :
“Fraulein” (8/3/’O2) writes: “Have never seen any-
thing printed in reference to the kangaroo fly.” The
kangaroo or wallaby-fly (Olfersia Macleayi) is one of a
remarkable group of properly two-winged flies, which,
having degenerated in life-habit, have in many oases
become wingless—£.#•» the sheep-tick. These insects are
popularly known as “ spider ” or “lice-flies.” They sel-
dom fly unless their host is killed, but run about among
the hair or feathers so rapidly that they are very difficult
to catch. The forest fly (Hippobosca equina) is common
in Europe. It infests the horses in the New Forest in
England. The wild ponies do not seem to mind it,
though a horse brought into an infected district becomes
frantic when these'flies settle upon him, probably on
account of the pinching of the sharp, forceps-like claws,
with which they are furnished to enable them to cling to
the skin. In 1895 Mr. Copeland (now N.S.W. Agent-
General), after a visit to New Caledonia, furnished the
MISS UNTIE RUSSELL (of Melbourne). A
charming amateur actress and dancer.
Photo, by Falk.
papers with an alarming account of a “ flying tick,” of
which he gave a very fair description, but, instead of
figuring it a quarter of an inch across the outspread
wings, one of the Sydney papers printed a sketch of a fly
over two inches across the wings as being “ half natural
size.” The quagga, the camel, the dog, the red deer, and
many birds, as well as our native fauna, have distinct
species of lice-flies parasitic upon them. A paper upon
“ Spider or Lice-Flies ”is printed in the N.S.W. Agricul-
tural Gazette, Dec., 1900.

“ J.D.C.,” to “ Fraulein ” (B. 8/3/’2) : I
think the fly referred to only appears when the
’roo or wallaby is dead. If the animal is killed on
the spot, and one gets to the body quickly, there
are no flies to be found, but in a minute or so one
or two arrive—reddish, transparent-looking in-
sects, slightly smaller than the house-fly. They
immediately make for the dead animal’s shoulder,
disappear in the fur for a few seconds, then
emerge and fly away. If there are other ’roo flies
in the neighborhood they do not show up, though
I have watched the carcase for a quarter of an
hour.
Miss Marie Narelle, whose portrait
appears in this issue, is to be tendered a com-
plimentary concert at Sydney Town Hall on
, .Easter Monday night. The gifted soprano, whose
services have ever been freely at the call of
charity, intends leaving for Europe shortly for
further work in her art, and her numerous Sydney
friends and admirers will all wish her the success
and progress that she deserves.
A Sergeant-Major of Scouts writes fron»
S’Africa (20/l/’O2) :
Dear Bulletin. —The war is still dragging miserably
on. There are men leading regiments—nay, columns —
that are no more fit to be soldiers than Tichborne was to
be a jockey. Times out of number the columns go to
sleep at night without a picquet or post out —in fact,
totally unguarded. I said mi one occasion : “Captain,
I can find you men for a picquet for this kopje if you
can’t supply it from your own squadron.” He replied :
“ Oh, never mind ;we don’t want it. There’s no Boers
about here.” I told him I, or the men, didn’t mind a fair
fight, but to be caught asleep was quite a different thing.
He answered : “ Oh, it’s the Major’s way ; let them come.”
In a big chase the other day, 20 miles full gallop without
drawing rein, my horse, amongst others, was shot under
me, four dropped dead, and 32 were made useless for a
considerable time. This caused about 50 men to be dis-
mounted. Yet the officers pile up the waggons with
chairs, tables, sofas, etc., for their own convenience, and
make the men walk. I myself have just done, with
others, a 45-mile journey on foot, because the waggons
were loaded too heavy to get a lift, and a lot of the load
was furniture.
As to looting, everything is smashed to atoms that is
likely to contain money, and I have seen the books of a
public library tossed through the window into the street
to make a breakwind for a picquet. Eight pianos were in
the middle of another street ; big mirrors were smashed
up to get a piece to shave with. Clocks, wearing-apparel,
music, beds, bedsteads, ornaments, all in conglomerate
masses on the floor, verandahs, or the streets.
“F.R.” : As to
“ Wongim’s” inquiry
(B. 8/3/’O2), for the
origin of the term
marum (not “ mar-
ram ”) grass. The
grass was “discover-
ed,” so far as Aus-
tralia was concerned,
by the late Baron
Von Mueller, who
did so much practical
work in his lifetime,
and he is responsible
for the name, which
is derived from the
Latin mare - “ the
sea.” It is a wonder-
ful grass for resisting
the encroachments of
the sea, and at Port
Fairy and Warrnam-
bool, on the West
Vic. coast, hundreds
of acres of land have
been reclaimed by its
use. It is planted in
the sand, where it
flourishes amazingly,
and spreads in the
teeth of wind and
brine. In a few
years a line of shift-
ing sand hummocks
is a mass of verdure.
Every year the Vic.
Govt, allows several
hundreds of pounds
for the planting of
the grass along the
various foreshores,
and it is money well spent. The fame of the
grass is spreading steadily, and every year con-
signments of it are sent abroad, to Maoriland,
Westralia, and elsewhere.

Now listen to me and I’ll tell you my views concerning
the African War,
And the man who upholds any different views, the same
is a rotten Pro-Boer 1
(Though I’m getting a little bit doubtful myself, as it
drags on week after week :
But it’s better not ask any questions at all —let us silence
all doubts with a shriek 1)
And first let us shriek the unstinted abuse that the Tory
Press prefer—
De Wet is a madman, and Steyn is a liar, and Kruger a
pitiful cur 1
(Though I think if Oom Paul—as old as he is—were to
walk down the Strand with his gun,
A lot of these heroes would hide in the sewers or take to
their heels and run 1
For Paul he has fought like a man in his day, but now
that he’s feeble and weak
And tired, and lonely, and old and grey, of course it’s
quite safe to shriek !).
And next let us join in the bloodthirsty shriek, Hooray
for Lord Kitchener’s “bag” 1
For the fireman’s torch and the hangman’s cord—they
are hung on the English Flag 1
In the front of our brave old army 1 Whoop 1 the farm-
•house blazes bright.
And their women weep and their children die—how dare
they presume to fight 1
For none of them dress in a uniform, the same as by
rights they ought.
They’re fighting in rags and in naked feet, like Wallace’s
Scotchmen fought 1
(And they clothe themselves from our captured troops—
and they’re catching them every week ;
And they don’t hang them —and the shame is ours, but
we cover the shame with a shriek 1)
And, lastly, we’ll shriek the political shriek as we sit in
the dark and doubt;
Where the Birmingham Judas led us in, and there’s no
one to lead us out.
And Rosebery—whom we depended upon I Would only
the Oracle speak 1
“ You go to the Grocers,” says he, “ for your laws 1 ” By
heavens 1 it’s time to shriek 1
THE WHALLABEE AT BAY.
“When brought to bay by the kangaroo dogs, the
whallabee, an Australian animal found in New South
Wales, puts its back against a tree so that it cannot be
attacked from behind, and waits the onset of its pur-
suers.”—Christian World (Eng.).
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

(BOWLER MARSH)
There’s Bowler Marsh the darkey
Who’s a native of the soil
I thought against the English team
They would give him a trial
But his cobbers up at Bathurst
Were little put about
When Mac Claren rose objection
They quickly passed him out
In fact it had been whispered
That to England he would get
But through the Bathurst verdict
They could not pick him yet
The team is picked for England
But Marsh is not to go
To prove his skill at cricket
They wont give him a show
But if they want a bowler
The best of judges owns
That Marsh should be selected
Instead of Howell or Jones
He might have got to England
But for the Bathurst burrs
For yielding to Mac Claren
They are white livered curs
He’s got to stay at home lad
Though we think it rather harsh
That England should play Ranji

PREMIER BARTON: “No more have I. We’ll have to go ourselves, I s’pose ?”
S EDDON: “No, tell you what —You go, and I’ll stop behind to cable ’Ome assurances of continued attachment to the Throne and Person.”

SPEECH BY DR. CAIUL^*
Dunng the interval, the Archbishop of Mel-
bourne made one of his happy little speeches ;
drawing an analogy between the career of St.
Patrick and that of Lord Hopetoun. His Grace
pointed out that just as the patron saint whose
festival we were observing that day had been
caught young and taken as a prisoner to Ire-
land, where he remained six years, so Lord
Hopetoun had been “caught young” and held
in bondage here in Victoria for a similar term ;
while just as to St. Patrick, when at length he
got away from Ireland, it had been said.
"Come back and walk amongst us,” so. too, the
same had been urged upon his Lordship. And
that, finally, just as St. Patrick had in the full-
ness of time returned to his Irish home, and
found the clans riven with internecine feuds and
jealousies, which he succeeded in assuaging and
composing, so, too. Lord Hopetoun had come
back to a congeries of semi-independent colonies
with divided sympathies and few common in-
terests. and had welded them into a cluster of
States, proud in their possession of common in-
terests and sympathies, and with a common
fervor for the great Empire to which they all
belonged.

THE SECEDING OF SALVATIONISTS:
“THE- ELIJAH MATTER.”
In a recent number of the London War Crt
appears a letter which Mr. W. Bramwell Booth,
“chief of the staff” of the Salvation Army, has
addressed “to the officers, soldiers, and friends
of the Salvation Army in Holland.” on Che sub-
ject of the defection of Mr. and Mrs. Booth-
Clibborn, son-in-law and daughter of •• General ”
Booth, who have joined Mr. Dawie’s sect of
“ Zionites.”
ds
WITNESS PUNISHED.
Judge Gaunt then directed the
Charles Paget, to be brought before him.
Paget came forward.
His Honor • In giving evidence in this
case you were guilty of wilful contempt of
court, and of wilful prevarication. Do you
know what prevarioation is ?
Paget shook his head.
His Honor : It will be fully explained to
you in gaol. You will be imprisoned in the
gaol at Melbourne for twenty-one days
A Bench warrant having been made out,
Paget, who seemed stunned at the sudden-
ness and seventy of his sentence, was re-
moved by a constable.

RAILWAY STATION,
SYDNEY.


Of the Times’ leading articles in verse—
Kipling’s ‘ ‘ Islanders ” being the latest—Labou-
chere, in London Troth, says :
The prose leading article is generally poor enough
stuff. The only object of turning it into verse seems to
be to conceal poverty of thought by violence of language
or obscurity of expression. The last article of the series
is called “ The Islanders.” The Islanders are apparently
the people of Great Britain and Ireland. The poet abuses
them in the most truculent fashion. Had I used such
language about my countrymen, I should have been met
on all sides with shouts of “ Yah, pro-Boer ! ”
A wool-ship caught fire recently at Wel-
lington (M.L.). When the alarm was given the
fire brigade was holding a dinner in the upstairs
dart of its building, and several city councillors
were among the guests. Of course, the captain
and lieutenant slid down the pole after their men,
but this was no reason why one of the heaviest of
the city fathers should do the same trick. He
came down so fast that his feet struck the head of
the man below him. Then he hit the ground
with a bang that stampeded the horses. It was
not a record “ turnout ” as far as speed was con-
cerned, for several of the men were unsober. Dur-
ing the progress of the fire two of them started a
row over the possession of a hose. Eventually
one hit the other over the head with it, and then
a policeman grabbed the one with the hose by his
chin-strap and upset him, and, as he went down,
the jet hit another city councillor in the weskit
and upset him, and he fell on a respectable
grocer. After that the row became more or less
general. It was a grand fire.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Go on the Lands Department, young man,
go on the Lands Department ! There wealth
awaits the enterprising.
The Australasian political situation—Pre-
miers Seddon and Barton bidding for Imperial
favor with their countrymen’s blood at a sort of
Dutch auction.
Dreary Pulsford is reported to be in hard
training for the Senate tariff debate. If his
friends get him to the post fit and well they expect
to see a run of at least five hours.
Significant that every mining electorate in
Q. returned Labor members in recent State elec-
tion ; Gympie and the Towers returned two each.
Yet Philp professes to be the miners’ friend.
Magnificent sentiment expressed on hand-
bills of a Victorian A.N.A. art-union :
ONE NATION, ONE DESTINY.
CHANGE OF A FORTUNE FOR A 808.
AND A DOCTOR FOR 3d. A WEEK.
Senator J. C. Neild tells The Bulletin
that he never endeavoured, in the Senate or else-
where, to prevent Reynolds’ Newspaper from
circulating in Australia; he has “ never at any
time said or done anything in connection with
that paper.”
The splendid consistency of the Jingo
mind makes all things possible. It strains at the
gnat of the Pro-Boer Jack Murray, M.P., giving
a non-political lecture on Drink, and swallows the
anomalous camel of Pro-Boer Higgins as an
authority on the Federal High Court.
The daily papers, which have persistently
abused the Federal Government for opposing the
preposterous demands of the Eastern Extension
Do., have, it is said, been promised a big con-
cession in press-rates if the Government gives
way. The E.E. Co. is cutely managed.
Victoria has eleven royal commissions now
wandering round at great expense taking evidence
on eleven different subjects. A twelfth one is
badly wanted—to find out why on earth the
Peacock Ministry can’t decide for itself about
anmet.hing, and have some sort of a mind of its
own.
A FINAL EFFORT.
“A rumor was abroad yesterday that the Commonwealth Government intended to offer more troops to the
Imperial Government, if needed, in order to hasten the end of the guerilla warfare in South Africa. Age.
AUSTRALIA : “ It’s Hobson’s choice, Mr. Chamberlain. These are all the men that I have left."
“ Wealsman ” : Australia’s numerous
Governments, Governors, and the like—rulers out
of all proportion in number to the ruled—suggest
a 2oz. horse trying to wag a 2cwt. tail with
dignity and decorum. Both animals only succeed
in looking ridiculous, and neither can keep the
flies off.
At Q. general elections, Brisbane was ap-
pointed a polling-place for all electorates, thus
enabling Fatman to register his property-vote
against the local flesh and blood. And Fatman
voted to some purpose, too, as more than one
Philpite got home on the corner-allotment vote
straight.
The proposal to urge N.S.W. Parliament,
if it cannot secure payment for the general worker
for public holidays, to cut off payment for these
days from the civil service shows a rude sense of
retaliatory justice. At all events, if N.S.W.
public servants lost their pay for public holidays,
these festive occasions would be few and far be-
tween.
Take the case—it is not imaginary, but
can be found in every State of the Commonwealth
—of a member of Parliament who uses his free
railway-pass to conduct a business in rivalry with
members of the non-political community. Was the
pass meant for such purposes ? If it wasn’t, is the
member misappropriating Government property
or what ?
Poor South Australia, which is already
threatened by its Treasurer with more direct
taxation, looks with horror on the added expense
of Australia’s military establishment. Once she
spent £15,000 a year on guns, generals, and glory,
but her share of the Blood Tax under Federation
is £40,000 annually, with nothing extra to show
for it.
No gainsaying that the defeat of Laborite
Turley by “Grizzler” Foxton, at Stanthorpe, Q.,
was a set-back to Q. Labor-party. Seeing that
Walter Bentley Biggs, the Scotch by terian actor,
gave Foxton a close run, three years ago, it looked
a soft thing for a good Laborite, but the hero of
the Cambooya election-trouble is back again for
three years.
Cabled that Edward VII is going to
celebrate his coronation by giving a dinner to
500,000 of the poor of London. Think of it—
half-a-million people in one city who are supposed
to be so poor that a pauper feed is reckoned the
kind of celebration that their case calls for ! Free-
trade is indeed an infallible recipe for making
people Jolly and Fat.
Nineteen years ago, on the motion of Mr.
Teeee, N.S.W. Leg. Assembly by 46 to 26 votes
carried the discontinuance of fees for elernentary
education. Now Government and Opposition are
practically in favor of continuing the fees, and
unless the Labor party whip strongly, the illogical
3d. fine on parents for doing a legally compulsory
act will deface the statute book indefinitely.
The political salvation of Premier Philp
at the Q. elections was the shameless electoral
law. An analysis of the voting shows that,
according to latest returns, Ministerialists polled
51,330 votes; Labor, 40,787; and Independent
Opposition, 9146. Add to these figures one-half
the number of votes on the rolls in the uncon-
tested electorates (four) and it pans out thus:
Government (less 10 per cent, plural votes) 49,850
votes, returning 42 members ; combined Opposi-
tion, 50,000, with 30 members. Thanks to the
unequal Q. electoral law the Ministry has a
majority of 12, although in a minority in the
country. No wonder Philp shows no disposition
to give electoral reform; it would mean political
suicide for the Continuous Ministry.
NoticeableduringQueensland elections that
the wobblers from Democracy were, with one ex-
ception, “passed out.” Lilley, Curtis, Cross, St.
Ledger, and “ Dinny ” Keogh are back to ob-
scurity, and Jason Boles got his title simply
because his opponent was new to the district, and
an unknown quantity.
The S. Australian Laborites are in trouble
again ; this election, like the last, bumps Tip
against an Adelaide race-meeting—and it wasn’t
the race-outing that was damaged last time. The
Govt, will gain most, for the Nonconformist con-
science takes the Jenkins-Foster crowd most
kindlv to its apron.
