Despite denials, the rumor still circulates
that Premier Philp will take the job of Q. Rail-
way Commissioner owing to Gray’s continued ill-
health. Philp’s distinguished ability as Treasurer
makes him peculiarly fitted to boss a big financial
institution such as the railway department. Philp
as Commissioner, with “Janny” Leahy as
Minister, would indeed be theQ. Heavenly Twins.
The selection of motions of adjournment in
N.S.W. Assembly is, when more motions than
one are submitted, determined by lottery ; the
sheriff’s selection of the names of jurors to serve
in civil and criminal cases is determined by lottery;
the proceedings of several of the church assemblies
are determined by lottery ; and yet George Adams’
horse-lottery is denounced as immoral and soul-
destroying.
If Premier Barton would show half the
velocity in taking over the Northern Territory,
which is part of Australia and a matter of real
Australian importance, that he does in taking over
British New Guinea and Burns, Philp and Co.’s
land in the New Hebrides, and similar extraneous
matters, it would be better for Australia. The
Minister for External Affairs appears to be
altogether too external.
Senator Higgs deserves commendation for
his courage in protesting in the Senate, during the
present state of public feeling, against the Third
Commonwealth Contingent of 2000 men. The
Victim is just now on such friendly terms with the
Vampire as to enjoy the process of blood-letting.
But tnere will be an awakening and then, but not
until then, will the bold patriotism of men like
Griffith and Higgs be recognised.
When the Western Lands Bill was going
through N.S.W. Parliament, Lands Minister
Crick made a strong point of the fact that the
Board was to be strictly non-political. Now we
read that “the Minister intends to sit with the
Board and travel with it some.” What for?
The Act clearly defines the duties of the Minister
and the duties of the Board and there is nothing
in it to sanction Mr. Crick’s course.
Premier Philp is going to open the present
Q. Parliament on Ist May, and give members a
chance of saying how the money is to be spent ?
Not much ! Parliament will be opened, as usual,
about the middle of June, and five months will
be wasted in discussing claptrap and syndicate
legislation, as usual. Then a few millions of tax-
payers’ money will be rushed through per guillo-
tine about midnight, next Xmas Eve, as usual.
What has become of Local Government in
N.S.W. ? The See manifesto and the Governor’s
speech contained specific promises of Local Go-
vernment. Premier See now professes to believe
that the Municipal Bill before the House last
session is all that the country needs—-a merely
consolidating measure, which did not advance
local government one inch. The roads-and-bridges
member is as great a nuisance as ever—and just as
big a source of strength to a O’Seeullivan Ministry
as ever—which accounts for the milk in the
cocoanut.
Sydney Freetrade press, after inciting the
Freetrade and Labor members to abolish the duty
on tea, as a move to embarrass the Federal
Government, is now—frightened by the indignant
protests of the big advertising tea-dealers—squeal-
ing woefully over its work. The Freetrade
papers, Sydney D.T. in particular, throw bricks
about in so large and generous a manner that
they very often have to mourn a friendly window
broken. The tea-duty was not felt as a burden
on the people, and its loss is a staggering blow to
the Federal revenue —already in so poor a condi-
tion that money has to be borrowed to buy guns
and put windows in post-offices, and carry through
other “permanent and reproductive works.”
A set of mouth-moralists have been trying
to induce Victorian Railway Minister Trenwith to
discourage Sunday trips to the seaside. The other
day a deputation representing the Christian
Citizens League and the Sunday School Union
called upon Trenwith and arrogantly requested
him to charge double rates on the rail-
ways for journeys to seaside resorts on
Sundays. The argument was used that
there should be no Sunday traffic on the
railways. But one black-coated sham
after another sat down without asking-
for the abolition of the Sunday morning
trains which take black-coated individuals
from the suburbs to the big city churches,
or for any increase of fares on them.
Apart from that phase of the question, it
would be interesting if an examination
could be made of the unhealthy minds of
people who wish to prevent the dust-
laden population of the city getting a
breath of fresh air on Sundays. They
are something akin to those dour creatures
mentioned by Oliver Wendell Holmes,
who would cut off the tail of a kitten if
they found her playing with it. Tren-
witli told his insanitary-minded callers
that he had no sympathy with their
request.
Crick is “ doin’ splendid ” as
N.S.W. Lands Minister been burnt
twice in effigy—and now the “Farmers
and Settlers’ Association of N.S.W.” has
its knife in him. The secret of the dis-
like to Crick is that he doesn’t consult
local personages. When seventeen in-
habitants with carefully-prepared speeches
come as a deputation to Crick, he tells
them he knows what they want, makes
the speech they had all prepared to make,
and does it a lot better than they could ;
and they find themselves out in the street, with
their business done certainly but with their
oration undelivered. And they never forgive
Crick.
Many men turn Jingo in their old nge
because then they can’t be expected to fight for
their Jingoism. How many Australian politicians
turn bloodthirsty when they are on their last legs!
In one way, anyhow, the administration
of Far-out N.S.W. by the Western Lands Com-
mission will cost more than it has under the
Lands Department. Officers who join the Com-
missioners’ service practically sever themselves
from the Lands Department, and though there is
statutory provision for rejoining, a man has no-
guarantee as to the billet he will get back to..
Consequently, the Commissioners are finding it
necessary to offer much higher salaries than the
Department has paid for equal services, and even
then they are not being carried off their feet by the
rush of applicants.
The Senate wisely threw the cumbersome
Hare system of voting out of the Electoral Bill,
and decided just to have a plain vote. Tasmania
has tried the Hare system, and found it futile,
cumbersome, and undermining to the intellect.
In every case, the same men got in who would
have got in if only the first vote had been counted
in the ordinary way. At the Tas. Senatorial elec-
tion, the vote had to be counted 14 times under
the Hare system, and when the whole complicated
muddle was exhausted, and the returning-offieer
was exhausted also, the 15 candidates stood in
exactly the same order as at the beginning.
The Philp Government ran 75 candidates
at the recent Q. elections ; at least one-half of
these declared themselves opposed to the Ministry
on vital points of policy. Only 25 straight-out
supporters of Philp were returned, and these
included seven Ministers, the colleagues of two
of them, the Speaker, Chairman of Committees,
two of Philp’s brothers-in-law (the brother-in-law
industry is a great one in Q.), and a brother of one
of the Ministers. The balance were mainly
returned for seats in and around the metropolis,
where the electors seem to be hypnotised by a
servile press.
Of the present members of the Vic-
torian House of 48 Rich Landlords only four
had to fight for their seats at last election, and
that was merely because the departure of some of
the old gang for the Federal Legislature had some-
what stirred the scum on the surface of the
political Dead Sea. Four, or five, or even ten
thousand pounds are commonly spent on a con-
tested election —when there is one—for the dreary
House of Boodle. When a legislative body has
so stagnated and petrified that a rich man buys
his seat at a great price and then holds it for life
without a contest, it badly needs some kind of an
upheaval.
A recent London cable :
“ The South African share market has not been affected
by the reverse.”
Twenty thousand sleeping low,
Thousands more to fall in fight;
“ What of chat? ” cries Broker Joe,
“ What of that ?—the shares are right."
Honor may have, long ago,
Caused our countrymen to fight;
But to-day, say Rhodes and co.,
Money makes the army go
Forth to —keep the shares all right !
The Commonwealth duty on imported
brandies has inspired a co. to start a distillery in
the Hunter Valley. The Freetrade Sydney D.T.
of March 15 pointed out: “The company
will afford a profitable outlet for the local grape
crop, and to vignerons especially it should prove
serviceable.” That is the whole theory and prac-
tice of Protection in a nutshell, and if the 3s. a
gallon Protection afforded by the Federal tariff
has produced this good result, other Protective
duties should produce similar good results, and 1
the Telegraph’s anti-Australian policy of favor-
ing the foreign importer at the expense of the
local British subject goes by the board.
Tasmania’s revenue deficit doesn’t trouble
the dreams of the islanders. It is proposed to-
postpone provisions for payment thereof for five
years or so, in the sure and certain hope that
population will have increased in that period, and
things will somehow come right by themselves.
As the Lewis Govt., like all its weary predeces-
sors, does absolutely nothing to induce outsiders
to settle in the island, the faith of Tassy is a
solemn thing. If the V.D.L. Co. were induced by
a vigorous, graduated land-tax and a heavy
absentee-tax to get out of bed in haste and sell its
western wildernesses, there might be more settle-
ment and more people to pay taxes, and more
revenue. In this hot, dry Commonwealth almost
the only cool spot where it rains every week
regularly, lies nearly idle, all because a land-gra!
syndicate is partly asleep and partly dead.

Again the Wesfcralian goldfields unionists
are kicking against the inflow of Austrian and
Italian laborers. The local Trades Hall writes to
the Federal Govt, that
On many m'nes they are now employed as bracemen,
and through their inability to speak or understand
English, the lives of the men engaged underground are
endangered.
A Melb. intending suicide put a bullet
-into his brain the other night, and then fired off
the remaining chambers of his revolver to attract
the police. Asked why he had not finished the
job, he replied, “I fired one bullet into my head.
Then I had enough.”

Herr Rassmussen’s Australian Career:
It is • about Twenty Years since Herr
Rassmussen introduced his Herbal Remedies
to the Australian Public. At that time
he was only known to the public as a Botanist
pure and simple, having devoted almost his
whole life to the study of that most useful
and interesting branch of science. It how-
ever, soon dawned on Herr Rassmussen that
immense virtues existed in ma-y plants which
were entirely unknown to medical men, and
that it was his duty towards his fellow man
to bring these new discovered Herbal Remedies
within the means of suffering humanity.
Herr Rassmussen at once set to work,
and his entire energy was directed towards
extracting the medical properties from all
kinds of plants, and putting them up in the
most suitable and practical form, according
to the nature of the complaints which they
had been found a cure for.
Pills and Powders were found to be the
most practical form to administer these new
Herbal Remedies.
It is unnecessary to particularise here the
immense success Herr Rassmussen met with
in his venture twenty years ago ; suffice it to
say that in less than twelve months the
great and wonderful cures brought about by
his Alfaline Herbal Remedies were known
throughout Australia, New Zealand, and many
other parts of the world. Testimonials and
Honors arrived from all parts. Gold Medals
from Paris and other plac s were awarded, and
Herr Rassmussen’s name has been a house'
hold word ever since. (Sydney Press.)

The great reference work of the Commonwealth,
“ The Year Book of Australia,” is now to hand for 1902.
This is a work that never changes, except to bring its
colossal array of facts and figures up-to-date. There is
hardly a subject, from horse-racing records to divorce
statistics, from the best place to visit from Saturday till
Monday to an account of the various denominations in
detail which the “ Year Book ” is not replete with in-
formation on.*
The Hansard fountain pen compared with the
old-fashioned pen, is what the modern hook is compared
with the ancient papyrus roll. The Hansard makes
writing a pleasure. It never clogs or gets out of order.
It is perfectly clean and reliable. It is one of the most
comfortable things that ever came to a busy man, and
even the poet or the nice young girl who wants to write
love letters will compose best when armed with a
Hansard. One pen will write a thousand words without
re-filling and its price, 7s. 6d., puts it within easy reach
of everybody.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Maclarenites went Englandwards by the Omrah
Wednesday last week, together with the eleventh Aus-
tralian XI. English cricketing opinion concerning
Maclaren’s combination was quite borne out by results.
Australia hasn’t been so weak in cricket for a decade or
more past, and still the visiting side went under in four
tests out of five, and two first class (eleven aside) matches
saw them beaten in six. Visitors had all the luck
excepting in the loss of Barnes’ services, which was
serious, but Maclaren was most to blame for this, as he
bowled the very soul out of his crack in the early portion
of the tour and saw him quit the field each time limp and
completely done. Englishmen proved away above any-
thing seen here for many a day at fielding. Jessop on the
off was a revelation. Braund, Maclaren and Jones in the
slips were well nigh impregnable. Quaife, at cover point
and third man, and Tyldesley, out in “ the country,"
earned high praise always. Biggest failures of the person-
nel were Jessop and Jones whom the fast wickets beat
badly ; ditto Quaife to an extent. The Englishmen’s 769
against N.S.W. was a remarkable feat, and is in front
of anything previously done by old country reps, opposing
Australia.
* * * *
Rounds in all grades of Sydney cricket ended
Saturday. Recent rains “ queered ” several wickets. As
anticipated, Sydney and Leichhardt-Balmain are left to
decide absolute supremacy. Waverley went under to
North Sydney for 45, and, following on, lost 8 for 105.
Usually brilliant Charlie Gregory only notched one in his
two innings, clean bowled by aboriginal Marsh each time,
the first for a “duck.” Paddington men were got rid of
by Leichhardt-Balmain for 132, and latter subsequently
only scored 68, but, owing to big lead of previous
week, secured the palm. Sides that batted on the
previous Saturday had a distinct advantage. Leich-
hardt-Balmain (259) were particularly lucky in this
respect. Winning (58 not out), for Paddington,
saw nine wickets down, and F. Gow accumulated
48 before being run out. Winning only got his
place in first eleven when two-thirds of the season
had passed, and in three efforts has compiled 80, 23
(not out) and 58 (not out), or 161 for once out. Marsh
shaped capitally for Sydney, getting 4 for 24, and 3 for 26,
bringing his season’s bag to 54 for 532—average 9.84, a
remarkable achievement. Glebe hit up 374 in opposition
to Redfern, and won with 250 to the good. Grounds (for
Glebe) made the only century—l42—of the day. Duggan
(also in Glebe’s interests) was not out with a total of 86.
North Sydney’s 239 was too much for Burwood, who
finished 34 behind. Bowlers Kelk, Kermode and Hogue
(Trustees’ team) made a sad mess of Cumberland’s
wickets, which totalled 106. The Trustees eleven scored
297 for 5 wickets in first attempt (innings closed), and at
second try had 5 down for 39. A feature of Saturday’s
second-grade competition was Allen’s (for Waverley) feat
of securing 9 Sydney wickets for 30 ; the tenth man was
run out.
• • • •
Fred Lane didn’t line up at N.S.W. 100yds.
swimming supremacy, though a contributor to another
portion of the show. Apparently “ Freddv,” below his
best perhaps, didn’t care to bump Balmain crack Bob
Craig, who, a month or so since, showed him the way
over the greater part of the furlong State championship.
Explanation advanced was that Lane wanted to give his
club-chum, Healy, a chance. Transparent bunkum !
Only a week before Craig beat Healy over the same dis-
tance. Saturday’s struggle went to Craig, who won in
lmin. 2 l-ssec. Healy led more than half the journey,
but failed to sustain the effort long enough ; besides,
Craig turned better. Margin between them at the end
was a couple of yards more than Craig’s clear length.
Healy will make it hot for the absolute best next season.
Unlike Australia’s other champs., he works all day, and
doesn’t get a chance to properly train himself. Lane
Cavill, Read and co. do little but swim and lie round the
bath platform week in and week out.
* • * *
At Brisbane carnival Saturday, Australasian
champion Dick Cavill won the “ hundred ” supremacy of
Bananaland in 1.3 with local crack Gailey only .(sec.
behind. Cavill'put a gap of 30yds. between himself and
Gailey for the “ Quarter ” premiership, done in 5.55 2-5.
» * * *
Final of Victorian bowling pennant went to
M.C.C. team after the hottest struggle recorded. West
Melbourne was left to fight the final. Third end
totals showed “ 10 all.” Fifteenth head gave M.C.C. an
advantage of 5, but five ends further West Melbourne was
just 1 best. Then things waxed torrid, and competitors
brought all they knew to bear. Second last end (24th)
saw them “ 80 all.” When three rinks of each side had
finished playing, two fours were still going, having a
couple of heads to decide. Scores were equal. Interest
was intensely keen, and crowds rushed the green to get a
better view, but the umpire stopped play till all cleared
off. M.C.C. got a couple in, and, tackling the last head,
West Melbourne wanted 3 to win, but had hard luck in
the effort to get them, and M.C.C. succeeded the second
time in succession and third in all.
* • * *
Only the enthusiasm of Mr. C. 8. Guest and a
few other grafters keeps once famous “Oxboro” meet
still on the list. Local interest is dead, and Sydney only
is looked to for the bulk of support. On Saturday, finishes,
on the whole, were close. Not a single first choice got
home. Hardness of the track caused several eliminations,
and probably broke up some triers, The crowd wasn’t
extra good, though possibly a paying one. The lunch
was literally chucked at one in a regular rough-up-picnic
fashion, and had to be scrambled for. Hawkesbury
Autumn Handicap, once among the biggest N.S.W.
racing fixtures, only induced eight mediocre starters.
Mo., as usual, cramped the odds, so that often there
wasn’t above a point difference between three or four of
the fancied, but comparative and rank outsiders succeed-
ing mostly caused much Hebrew lamentation for not
having stood the public “ picks ” a bit more.
* * » *
Westralian Enfilade-Melodise 4-year-old nag
Warrior 11. made a one-horse thing of Hawkesbury Han-
dicap (ljm. 100yds.), Saturday. Waster Ace of Diamonds
humped a barrel of money at “ evens,” but could get no
nearer than third, eight or nine lengths behind the
winner, who finished as he liked. Warrior 11. had many
backers for the plums of Randwick, Caulfield and Flem-
ington Spring meetings, but—something went wrong.
He’ll be worth watching now, by the way he ran and won,
Saturday. He made the rest look hacks, His 7.7 in Don-
caster mile, this Saturday, and 7.4 in Sydney Cup—with
no penalties to tack on—shouldn’t trouble him much.
Stable was evidently as far astray as the public,’for they
didn’t come at him at all heavily ; the nag started at
S’s. Warrior’s last previous win was W.A. Derby, Decem-
ber, two years ago.
* • * »
Two absolutely unbacked nags (Clangour and
Skene Dhu) ran first and second in Hawkesbury Welter,
Saturday. Favorite Wollogorang (6 to 4 against) filled
third place, though he should have won well, but for
jock L. Want’s tactics in twice trying to get through a
compact mass of prads instead of going round them.
Skene Dhu made the running, and was only just pipped
on the post; he had a novice rider up—young Stanley,
son of old-time jock Stanley of The Barb fame. Stanley
got away well and deserved better luck. The Wollogorang
people thought their nag couldn’t lose, and their judg-
ment (bar the rider’s mistakes) was good.
Finish of Hawkesbury Park Stakes was in the
highest degree exciting. Until the judge showed the
numbers, not even men near a line with the winning-post
could say which of three had won. Trencherman snapped
the money with Olivia second and Beesting third.
Trencherman is a racehorse through and through, appar-
ently. He is by Trenchant from Kearro, by Grandmaster
from Cameo by Kingston (imp.) and was bred by present
owner, Mr. W. A. Gardiner. Kearro was among Ducken
field Park stud.
* ft * *
Randwick trainer-owner Wootton’s Acetine
looks threatening for this Randwick meeting. His win
of Hawkesbury Clarendon H’cap was full of merit. Stable
put a heap of money on him commencing with tens,
eights and sevens. Craze for Ferryman provided the
market as he went out at odds on in most places.
Acetine’s running created the impression that he’d see
another furlong (mile) out strongly, and with only 7.8 to
carry must have a chance in the Doncaster. Ferryman
wasn’t placed.
♦ * * •
No big “ commish ” had been worked on Sydney
Cup up to settling Monday last when Blue Metal was
nominal favorite ; both he and Caledonia have come on
a heap lately and the recent big Melbourne double —New-
market and Australian Cup—may be repeated at Rand-
wick this Saturday and Easter Monday. Sir Foote is an
unknown quantity at 2m. but some interested have two
admittedly high classed ones to depend upon in Beanba
and Industrious, a 4yr. old sister to Gaulus, The Grafter,
etc. Beanba has developed into a splendid mare. Punters
had better wait for the Doncaster stable lead before dis-
cussing the Cup prospectsof any one of thetrio. Patronage
is much fancied 1 • c the public, and is shaping well at work,
but 8.5 brings h :n closer to the top-mark than when San
Fran gave him 251 b. in the Metrop. and just thrust his
neck in front at the right end Patronage, however, will
he going strong when a lot of them are stopping.
Hawkesbury running of Warrior 11. gives him an unques-
tionable chance if he can repeat it, while Caleconia’s
showing at Moorefield, a fortnight ago, which could have
been improved considerably, brings him well within
reckoning. Earnshaw’s choice (Sir Foote, Industrious, or
Beanba) Blue Metal, Caledonia, Patronage, and Reviver
may provide the placed horses, with Patronage or Blue
Metal first, though writer must confess a strong leaning
towards Caledonia, but for a doubt about him seeing the
two miles out.
♦»* * •
Form and work point indubitably to Sir Foote
being the best of goods for the Doncaster, and this scribe
wouldn’t be a bit surprised to see him start, almost as hot
a pot as he did for the Newmarket. Purser’s prospects,
in view of his recent convincing defeat of a good lot at
Moorefield, are not to be ignored, provided he is one for
the money ; and then there’s Essington—said to be doing
capital work, and whom cute people have been nibbling
at for weeks past. One of those three should win, bar
the possibility of Warrior 11. chipping in—he was con-
sidered a champion in W.A., and Lamond evidently has
him very well just now.
* * * •
At the moment Sir Foote is hot favorite for
Doncaster Handicap—fives is the best available price ;
fours only in most places. On Sir F.’s Newmarket
running, the race looks good for him, but there’s not a
cent of stable coin in sight so far, though the public have
been biting at the choice thing for some days. Anything
over current odds is always snapped. The ’Ome nag has
a job on that has been successfully tackled but three
times in 22 years. Only prads to hump over 9st. first past
the post were Ben Bolt (9.5), Marvel (10.4), and Courallie
(9.3). Ben Bolt was a six-year, and Marvel and Courallie
five-year-olds. Courallie had just previously given
Maluma a stone and run up to her in the Newmarket.
Russley, as a six-year-old, appropriated the Doncaster
with 9st. The biggest burden ever carried by a three-
year-old was Sir William’s 8.6.
• * • *
Inter-State Invitation Sculls (about l.fm.), at
Sydney St. Ignatius College regatta, Saturday, saw Tas-
manian J. Bourke (10.12) 10sec., and Australian J. Daley
(lOst.) scratch, row a special event. Bourke commenced
very slowly, but, half-mile from the start, was still 7sec.
to the good (despite Daley having anticipated “ go”), and
much further on had the same advantage, when two or
three professional scullers hampered his progress badly,
thus allowing Daley to get up within 1£ lengths. Pace
now was equal, but Bourke’s erratic steering lost much
for him. Knowing the water well, Daley made the most
of a little tide here and went to the front by two lengths,
which Bourke brought down to barely one at the finish.
Time (10.42) shows 6.4 for the mile. Daley had it all-on
the whole way, and would probably have been licked but
for Bourke’s ill luck. Beaten sculler is only a youngster
yet (years younger than Daley), and with maturity and
good coaching will improve. ,
* » » •
At S. A. champion fours on Port River Satur-
day, Mercantile, Port, and Adelaide crews made things
sultry over the full course. Adelaide scored—lJ lengths
better than Port, with Mercantile I£ lengths further
away third. Victorian 8-oar supremacy same day was a
row-over for Albert Park through incapacitation of
Mercantile’s stroke and No. 2.
* « * *
Melbourne and Sydney “ feddera”—O’Neill and
James—bruised violently at Sydney Golden Gate A.C.,
Saturday night, to a huge house. At first, James, less
clever but more forceful, promised best; later, O'Neill
apparently owned the situation, till the aspect changed
and it was anybody’s fight. Betting fluctuated wildly
with fortune. James kept as cool as an iced cucumber,
and thumped away methodically. O’Neill was not at
ease, though his left worked cleverly and he took a
thumping that would have made an iron man wince, and
still came up gamely for more, which James supplied
liberally when the rather scarce openings allowed. James
attacked the ribs, till a big red splurge spread on both
sides. O’Neill was winning sure in the 13th round when
James’ hard head crashed against the Melbourne man’s
eyebrow and gashed it. Then the job proved softer, and
a right on the point settled things. James fouled once
by throwing his opponent, but the referee didn’t indicate
that he had seen the flagrant breach. Winner is a real
instinctive fighter who’ll slug-out a few when he knows
the game better.
* * • *
Lightweight bruisers Barr and Turner supplied
a skin shifting struggle at Sydney Gaiety last week. For
20 rounds, it was the bitterest and most determined thing
in fist-fighting seen for years. Barr’s elbow blinded
Turner’s right eye in the second round, and had him par-
tially dazed for the next four or five, during which he
stood a stiff gruelling. Thenceforth the battle was
fairly evenly fought, though Turner steadily wiped
out Pother’s early advantage, and had him anyhow at
times, near the end. Referee “Bill” Jennings called it a
draw, but Turner really won.
* * * *
Sydney Forest Lodge pony races developed
something out of the ordinary last Monday. Kitty B. (9.7)
and Manalea (8.5) finished a dead-heat in 14-hands handi-
cap. Running off, Manalea went out 7to 4 on, and the
same result occurred. After a spell the two got toge-
ther a third time, with Manalea 2 to 1 on now, and they
still couldn’t be separated. Owners wouldn’t agree to a
division, consequently the little ’uns will have another
cut at the club’s next meeting. Honors are undoubtedly
with Kitty 8., for she gave away not only 161b5., but ljin.
in height.
* * * «
Retired champion boxer “Larry” Foley and
Federal Prime Minister “Toby” Barton were the features
of the St. Patrick’s Day gathering on Sydney Agricul-
tural Society’s ground. “Toby” (at dinner) attributed
the success of the function to the “ tact and good fellow-
ship of an old friend and a formidable Irish champion,
Mr. Laurence Foley.” “Toby” and “Larry” strolled
arm-in-arm about the grounds, and when they were
driving away, cheers for “ Toby ” were absolutely stifled
by the roar that rent the-air for “ Larry.” This scribe is
tipping Foley as a “ sure thing ” for Parliament whenever
he likes to put up in the Conservative and bruising
interests.
* * * *
On consideration, George Adams’ plan of saving
his sweeps by getting customers to send their letters
addressed to themselves, care of Hobart Stock Exchange,
doesn’t look as if it would work. It would leave Adams
at the mercy of any police agent or reverend foe ; Jabez
Smith, for instance, might send a registered letter to
“ Jabez Smith, c/o Sec., Hobart Stock Exchange,” and if
it were opened (as it would be) prosecute for theft of the
letter.
» * * *
“Via”: “Tattersall’s” latest scheme for cir-
cumventing the Federal embargo contains a fatal weak-
ness. “Tatt.” asks that an order to collect amounts
and transact biz. generally be sent down to Tasmania
with the remittance. Doesn’t he know that that order
will be impounded by the P.O. officials the first time
“Tatt’s” representative hands it in at Hobart Post-
office ? After that he will be unable to collect money-
orders. This is according to P.O. regulations, and Drake
and Co. will see that the rule isn't dodged. I see that he
is said to have given the originator of his plan £lOOO.
Would to heaven I had known of this. I could put him
on to something simpler for half the price, but it’s pro-
bably too late now.
* • • *
The attitude of Postmaster-General Drake in
refusing to even consider the question of stopping letters
addressed to advertising bookmakers, turf-tipsters and
other sporting parasites, whilst relentlessly crushing out
“Tattersall’s” fair gamble, suggests the idea that Mo.
must have some very good friends in the Federal Cabinet.
The sense of fair play in the community should now sug-
gest the appointment of a vigilance committee to watch
the “ cigar”-shops of the big bookmakers —who are at the
bottom of the agitation against “Tatts.” —and see that
they have dealt put to them some of the suppression
sauce. Let their correspondence be confiscated by the
post office “in the interests of morality.” The present
position is that the fair gamble has been knocked out in
the interests of the unfair gamble, and the Federal
Government is acting as if it were in league with the
“ crooks ” of sport to prevent the gambling public getting
a fair deal for its money.
• * • •
The racing libel action, Castles v. Brisbane
Courier and Observer (decided in favor of defendants)
caused much foul linen to be aired. The cause of action
was the newspaper’s comments on certain doings at a
little soup-plate 500yds. course at Woolloongabba, Bris-
bane ; the “ racing” 'was such that even the dull Courier
lashed out. Half-a-dozen witnesses swore that they “ flew
the pigeon,” fixed the starter, weighed in and out 2st.
light, etc., etc. It was all a game of points. Altogether
there was such a picturesque exposition on oath of the
sport of things as should convince the most foolish of
mugs to give up punting.

The totalisator again. At Port Adelaide
meeting the St. Pat.’s Handicap paid a div. of £96 13s. ;
and this although the two-div. system was being tried,
and backers of the second got £4 7s. for their pound as
well. (Cuba, the winner, would have paid nearly £l3O
on the single div. plan). Also the club had secured in
tote commission about £88 —more than the value of the
the stake ! Likewise there was a trifle left over in the
shape of “ fractions ” for local charities.

was born 27th March, 1861. His greatest bowling feat ot
the present season was the capture of 13 wickets for 9!
runs in the first match of Maclaren’s team against S.A.
His batting average in first-class matches for this year is
24T6, and his bowling average 19'23. Giffen is not yet
convinced that his da vs of cricket are over.

Arnold Collins, while playing cricket recently
for the Imperial United Club (what a name 1) in South
Australia, took six wickets in one over.
* • * m
Oswald T. Caust, who scored 99 not out against
the Englishmen at Broken Hill, is 29 years of age, but
has been playing cricket since early boyhood. He hails
from South Australia, where he was for years a local
champion ; he on one occasion was first emergency in an
inter-State match, but never had a chance. His record
this season, in senior matches alone, is : —l2 innings, 6
not outs, 1056 runs. He this season twice scored 200,
each time not out, and seven times went over the century.
It is not always the lodger who disturbs
matrimonial bliss in Australia. In the Mertel v.
Mertel case (Sydney) the wife alleged cruelty and
in reply respondent alleged :
He gave her a ’possum as a pet, and she would never
leave it alone. She used to take it to bed. He objected
and the quarrel took place about the ’possum, lie
admitted pinching his wife’s nose once during a quarrel
about the ’possum, but he did not do it in a brutal way
He admitted he was unkind to the ’possum. But he did
not do away with it, because it was the only thing his
wife was fond of. Quarrels did take place between him-
self and his wife, but they were owing to her having
neglected the baby and her domestic duties.
The court held that if the husband hadn’t been •
unkind to the ’possum he had been more unkind
to his wife than jealousy of the ’possum warranted,
and granted a divorce.
“The organised day laborers” of the
State of N.S. W. held a demonstration the other
day. The O’Sullivan was, of course, there, and in
response to a toast:
He had been asked whether, if he built a house, he
would do it by day labor. He would—cheers—if ever he
had the means and they would start him with the
necessary land.
Seeing that Mr. O’Sullivan has already had one
handsome testimonial from a class whose wages he
increased, and that the organised day laborers,
whose wages he has also increased, might take bis
hint about giving him a valuable present seriously,
is The O’S. quite judicious in making jokes of this
sort — The Bulletin presumes it is a joke?
Sydney D.T.’s religious reporter on the
farewell church-service to the last N.S.W. batch
of five-bob-a-day’s :
Whether from the effect of the long walk down or the
cramped position in the pews, the men behaved some-
what badly during the service. They left their places
in two and threes during hymns, prayers or lessons—it
did not seem to matter, and no one attempted to stop
the wholesale exit. Some came back during the sermon,
some during prayers, and some not at all. Surely this
sort of thing ought not to be allowed.
But, after all, didn’t the uneasy, impatient Cohen-
tingenters have a truer idea of the situation than
the censorious reporter ? They were hired to
burn down farms and hunt children into concen-
tration camps ; and what has the Christian God’s
blessing to do with that work ?

“ O, Australia’s not too stinkin’,

Before we pack and sail
’Way back to dear ’Old Heaven,

That’s hung on us like lead :
Tho’ we bravely faced your bowling
Your Chyack killed us dead 1

And Raphael was as bad ;
St. Michael’s been prostrated
Since that first match, poor lad 1
Er —yes, we love Australia
Except for your Ohyack. . . .
What ? No, I fear we’ll never
Come back ! Come back !! Come back Ml”

Sentence of death passed by Westralian
Chief Justice on Donald M‘Kay, William Cullen,
and John Campbell, for being concerned in the
death of a man named Grey, was a clear contempt
of jury. The jury’s verdict was—
We find Donald M‘Kay guilty of murder, and William
Oullen and John Campbell guilty of aiding and abetting,
with a strong recommendation to mercy. ... In the
interests of justice we have agreed to a verdict of murder,
but we hope that the full extent of the law will not be
carried out, because we do not believe that the prisoners
intended to murder John Gray.
Yet the Judge sentenced all to death, and 1 ‘ held out
no hope of mercy” to M‘Kay. The three ruffians all
deserve exemplary punishment, but, if the Jedge’s
verdict is followed by an execution, the inevitable
result will be that in the next half-dozen or so
fatal push outrages the jury will (sensibly enough)
resolutely refuse to convict at all, and a score of
scoundrels be redeemed by the hanging of one.
The Bullltin doesn’t believe in vicarious punish-
ment of that sort. No evil results seem to have
followed the present N.S.W. Government’s policy
of never hanging a man who has been recom-
mended to mercy. Certainty, not ferocious
severity of punishment—that is society’s goal.
Melb. Age cable-faking takes the palm
among Australian fact-twisters. Speaking of some
angry politics in the English House of Commons,
the Age put up the heading, “Insult to Mr.
Chamberlain,” and went on to state that the
Joss of the Cohen-jingoes had called Irishman
Dillon “a good Judge of traitors,” —after which
Dillon’s declaration that Judas was “a ———
liar,” was surely not the most unexpected thing in
the world ! You can hang a man for being a
traitor, but he can’t be strung - up for
plain lying, so Chamberlain’s provocative
remark was by far the more insulting of the two.
Einally, the Age states that Nationalist leader
Redmond “ had much difficulty in restraining his
48 followers from language and conduct that would
have provoked their general suspension.” How
on earth could anyone tell what sort of language
and conduct they were going to employ ?

A Sydney street scene : Sunday evening,
about the hour when people maunder off to chapel
—and other places. A policeman came down and
snared a disreputable dog. He then asked the
bystanders for a piece of string, but without
success. So he proceeded to drag the mangy
animal away on its hind legs. At this stage a
woman with compunctions produced from among
her clothes about four feet of red tape ! She in-
quired where “the poor creature” was to be
dragged, and the law explained that it was only a
stone’s throw to a dark alley, where the animal
would be bashed to death with the baton, and its
remains would be called for later by the dust
gentleman.

Sydney Telegraph :—
With regard to the licensing laws, the most urgent
requirement is to have the law administered more closely
in accordance with the public desire than it appears to be
at present, and this important phase seems to have been
touched by the Rev. W. G. Taylor in his “ protest against
a Minister of the Crown going into Licensing Courts to
plead for licenses.” Without suggesting that magis-
trates are influenced in their decisions by the Ministerial
advocate, etc.
But some magistrates certainly are influenced.
A.nd if the Telegraph doesn’t suggest that they
are influenced then what does it suggest ? And if
it doesn’t suggest anything then the public wants
its penny back.
The loud curse of “M.B.” :—
Your women’s column scribe writes (referring to sur-
geon Fitzgerald) : “And as he is very fond of dogs he
must be a good sort.” I presume the hygienic, well-
groomed, flealess brute is meant. Not the ill-fed, mangy,
ring-wormed, unfortunate middle-class and working-man
dog, the whelp that father buys to be a companion to
the children, the our with the sore eyes and warty hide,
the neglected pup that never gets washed, the leprous-
looking animal that disseminates ringworm, and is for
ever nosing after a flea that never gets caught, the
member of the utterly useless battalion of dogs that live
on garbage, kill fowls, chase goats, infest shop-fronts,
snap and bite the butcher and baker at the back door,
bark all night long and fight most of the day. Can’t a
man be a “good sort” if he isn’t fond of dogs?
More trouble about the baby that is paid
for before delivery. A pushing girl at Ballarat
(Vic ) secured an order for £10 pre-maternity ex’s
from a Creswick miner, and then set cheerfully
to work to hire, beg, or borrow a baby. The baby
having been secured by means of an advt., the
young woman wrote to the miner telling him to
call round and admire his offspring, and to bring
along the £10. The police have worked the
matter out to the above effect. The girl admits
that the baby was borrowed for the occasion,
yet a local doctor is ready to swear that when
the maternity order was secured, a few weeks
ago, it was evident that she —if she was the same
girl—would not be reduced to going in debt for a
baby. This, the dailies say, is a most singular
feature of the case, and it certainly looks as
singular as anybody could expect.
A couple of big farm-burners—one much
the worse of liquor—were sprawling on the grass
with a couple of girls at a recent military shivoo,
and the more sober of the two was thrilling the
damsels—apparently sisters of the comatose one—
with descriptions of their experiences at the front.
“They never bury ’em properly out there,” he
remarked. “ One day we come into Whitlefon-
tein, an’ I sees a feller’s leg stickin’ up through
the veldt an’ Alf. sez ter me, ‘ Why, blime, if that
ain’t big Thomson’s foot. I’d swear ter it any-
wheres’—an’, strike me stiff, so it was. He’s
mighty smart at recognisin’ things is Alf. Poor
old Thommy was topped off in the scrap a couple
er weeks afore. Then over near Bruikspit’s farm
we seen a heap er them with arms an’ heads outer
the ground. Alf. knew two of the heads at once.
I tell yer, Alf.’s got brains. There ain’t no £4 10s.
funerals over there. Yer got ter take what they
give yer, an’ mighty lucky if yer git that. But
Alf.,” he added admiringly, glancing at the
snoring form of the other, “ t hey ain’t no better
feller than Alf ever potted a Boer. I never want
no better mate than what Alf. is. Hey ! you
there ! ” this to a harmless-looking youth who had
approached the prostrate form;_ “yOu leave him
alone! Anyone as touches him touches me!
See!” The youth edged off. “ Yer don’t stand
no nonsense when yer’ve been on the veldt for a
bit. It’s shut up, or make ’em shut up.” He
entered into a long story descriptive of the
heroism of himself and Alf. “‘And take that,’
says Alf, an’ he ups with his rifle and drops the
Boer. Then another of ’em comes at me, an’ Alf.
fetches him down with the butt of his rifle. I tell
yer I’d do anything for Alf. If he was ter git
topped off d’ye know what I’d do. I’d just grab my
rifle an’ put a bullet in my head, I would s’welp me.
That’s what I’d do.” One of the girls coughed.
An old man with a grey beard was placidly puffing
his pipe in the line of the breeze three yards in
front. “Hi, there!” cried Alf.’s eulogist, “put
out that pipe. Ye’re blowin’ the smoke on Alf.”
There was no answer. “ D’ye hear? Put out that
pipe an’ put it out quickanlively, an’ get away
from Alf.” The smoker turned—he was sixty if
he was a day : “ You come here an’ put it out,” he
ordered. “ It’s all right ! it’s all right!” cried the
cohentingenter, “I wasn’t speaking ter you, ole
man. I was talkin’ ter Alf. ”

whilst in bed, but on standing up the swelling

believe the climate has been the cause of my ill-

write you for your Powder to see if it will relieve
me.—Yours truly,
“W. H. WILTON

swift removal of insoluble materials from the body. Without such actions insoluble sub-
stances will precipitate in the kidneys and bladder. The intense suffering produced by stone, together with
consecutive pyelitis and cystitis, are avoided by prompt elimination. Unquestionably, although the speedy
removal of uric acid and other products of faulty tissue change is of conspicuous benefit, yet to pre-
a service still more important. This service is performed

sponsible for the production of - deleterious materials.—Fide Medical Record, Sept laol

prehensible mischief to those who suffer from derangement of the digestive or
excretory organs. Success in business or the professions, or in any walk in life,

All Acquainted with this Case.
The Case of
Mr. W. O. SCOTT.
(an independent report.)
To those whose business brings them into
contact with numerous individuals of neces-
sarily various temperaments it iSj generally
considered an inherent quality to be able to
create favourable impressions on probable
customers when negotiations are in their
rudimental stage. Where one man may
succeed another may fail, according to the
tact and discretion displayed in approach-
ing the prospective client. Mysterious in-
stances of failure occur where success seemed
to be assured, and the serenity of our pass-
ing days are sometimes disturbed by conse-
quent unhappy reflections. Mr. William
Owen Scott, residing in May Street, St.
Peters, informed our reporter that he “very
frequently felt incapable of extending ordin-
ary politeness to his customers at one period
of his existence.” “I was often too queru-
lous to be spoken to,” continued Mr. Scott.
“ What was the reason ? ”
“I was driven neai ly mad with neuralgia.
I had three attacks during twelve months,
and the pain multiplied in severity each
time it came on. You can ask nearly every-
body in Marrickville, Enmore, St. Peters,
and Newtown about the way I suffered, and
as lam well-known in all those places, a
little enquiry will satisfy you that my state-
ments are quite correct. When I used to
drive round the people could not understand
what on earth was the matter with me.
Instead of having a few moments’ agreeable
chat with themas I usually had, they found
Vie eccentric in my behaviour and unpardon-
#bly bad-tempered. I took the orders and
drove off without even saying * Good-day,’
leaving them standing with a look of amaze-
ment. The torture which I experienced
robbed me of all my patience. As I had al-
ways enjoyed the most robust health I
could not account for my attacks.”
“ Perhaps you have some decayed teeth ?”
“ I thought that too, as I went to a den-
tist and had them examined. He told me
.hat my teeth were perfectly sound and tin t
my affliction had probably arisen through
my open-air work involving exposure to
cold winds and rain. I was somewhat dis-
appointed, as I was in hopes of being re-
lieved from my torture by the extraction of
two or three teeth. I would willingly have
had them all out if such an operation would
have ended my suffering.”
“ Where did the pains first appear ? ”
“ They first started on the top of my
head, making my temples throb unmerci-
fully ; then my gums, which were very much
inflamed and tender, became affected. Be-
fore long I had acute flashes of pain darting
all round my head and face like lightning.
From my gums the nagging torture travelled
to my ears, making them ache intensely.
My head was so bad that I could not bear to
turn around. My gums were swollen, and
a sensation like pins and needles sticking in
them was always irritating me. A few
moments’ relief was occasionally obtainable
through the application of hot water, a
mouthful of which, as hot as I could bear
it, inducing a little ease ; but when the
water commenced to cool the agony reas-
serted itself with redoubled vigour. It was
impossible to sleep either day or night, and
during the dark hours, when everybody but
myself was asleep, I felt more distressed and
still more melancholy. Just as the day
would be dawning I have often got up in a
fit of desperation and wrapped my head
with tow'els and flannels and then laid down
on the floor with a pillow. The hours slowly
ebbed away, but no sleep came to me. Dur-
ing the day I tried cloths steeped in boiling
water and then applied to the face, but to
no purpose.”
“ Could you attend to business at all ? ”
“It was compulsory, although I was not
in a fit condition to work, as my system was
in a state of collapse. Being unable to eat
for weeks I naturally fell off in weight, and
T suffered greatly from general weakness
and an intolerable feeling of exnaustion.
On going out into the light of day I im-
mediately felt giddy, and had to remain
stationary until the sensation wore ott. My
nerves were quite unstrung, and I _ was
always apprehensive of danger when driving
through the streets. On another occasion,
when I was in a state of frenzy through my
tortuous afflictions, I drove like a madman,
caring little about the risk I ran. If ever
any person has had such a bad experience
of neuralgia as mine was I would like to
make his acquaintance.”
“ For what purpose ? ” .
“In order to make a comparison of its
effects. From a physical standpoint I was
ruined. The many remedies I had tried
did not prove beneficial, and their adminis-
tration did not give me any encouragement.
I kep!) trying first one physic and then
another, besides experimenting with several
old-fashioned prescriptions, with the result
that I thought myself incurable. Then I was
advised to try Clements Tonic. At first I
refused to do so, in view of so many other
medicines having failed ; but finally I con-
sented to give it a trial.” v
“ And what opinion did you form ?”
“ I cannot find words to express my high
appreciation of Clements Tonic, from which
I derived great benefit and quickly regained
my health. After taking it a while I
noticed my neuralgic pains gradually dying
out. When I had taken the contents of the
first bottle the improvement manifested was
sufficient to warrant the continuance of the
same treatment, and I therefore procured
another. My hopes had quite revived, the
pains vanished. It may seem incredible,
but it is nevertheless a fact, that such mad-
dening agonies were so promptly brought to
a standstill. But Clements Tonic did more.
It brought sound, refreshing sleep to my
distressed mind. Instead of parading the
bed-room like a maniac I obtained comfort-
able rest. A new life was opened to me.
My appetite returned and my former vigour
was established. My eyes became clear and
bright, and the heavy laden, black marks
disappeared from bene th them.*" The
drawn, worried look of anguish in my face
departed with their cause, and my cheeks
soon filled out with a healthy plumpness.
'1 he second bottle of Clements Tonic effected
a complete cure. There was not a vestige
of pain left, and in every sense I was a
healthy man. A nervous irritability came
on through fear of my old enemy returning.
My mind was soon set at rest on that score,
and I am thankful to say that I have had
neither ache nor pain since Clements Tonic
cured me, and I am now three years older
since that happy event. I have frequently
recommended it to my many friends, and I
am thoroughly convinced that Clements
Tonic is an iufallible curative for all nervous
disorders, neuralgia in particular.”
“ Then I may report your cure ?”
“ Publish it broadcast, if you will, for
others’ benefit.”

I, William Owkn Scott, of May-street, St. Peters
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales;
do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I have carefully read the annexed document
consisting of three folios and consecutively
numbered from one to three, and that it contains an?,

permission to publish in any way my statements—
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any pay-
ment ; and I make this solemn declaration conscien-
tiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue
of the provisions of an Act made and passed in the
ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled “ An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales,
and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and
for the suppression of voluntary and Extra-judicial
Oaths and Affidavits.”

minister, arguing against divorce for any cause but
adultery said he knew of the following jumble of
relationships haying occurred in an Australian
.at 0 A married woman got a divorce ; her
niece got a divorce ; the aunt married the niece’s
divorced husband ; the niece married again • the
aunt got a divorce (a second time) from the niece’s
divorced husband ; the aunt’s first husband came
back to her in the capacity of a hired servant.

THE
“ It seems too good to be true ”
is what one woman writes about the performances of an
HSolian she has recently purchased.
She could not believe it possible that she could have in her
own drawing-room an agent through which she could produce
any piece of music ever written, at any time, in a truly
artistic manner with discriminate emphasis and the most
refined expression—thousands of pieces which she could never
otherwise hear; music of all masters, ancient or modern,
of every style and grade of excellence, from a
Palestrina Motet to the latest popular song.
The Quartets, Symphonies, and Sonatas of Haydn, Mozart,
Beethoven, Brahms ; the Chorales, the Fugues,
Suites, Sonatas of Bach.
The entire orchestral scores of the music-dramas of Wagner.
The marvellous musical poetry of Schumann and Chopin.
The exquisite songs of Schubert, Franz, and Brahms.
The Overtures of Weber, Rossini, Verdi, Meyerbeer, Donizetti,
Auber, Suppd, Balfe, Thomas,
Gounod, Bizet, Goldmark, Humperdinck.
The fascinating Gypsy music of Hungary—
The Rhapsodies of Liszt.
In short, the whole world of music.
An impression prevails that ALolians are very expensive instruments, and
can only be afforded by the very rich. This is a mistake; you can buy a good
.dSolian for less than you would pay for a piano, and on easy payments. The
cheapest .dEolian plays more than five thousand compositions—and plays them
well, too.

ON OUR SLECTJON,” by Steele Rudd.

Explorations in Civilisation.
Being the letter of an Australian in exile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
October 19. —Passed Point de Galle and now
running along the Ceylon coast. I begin to
understand :
What tho’ the spicy breezes
Blow soft from Ceylon’s Isle.
You get the same thing, or something like it, in
North Queensland. I shall find , the East smelling
like Chinatown at Cairns—the smell of incense
and dead fish, and fine old Gruy&re and ground
coffee. The mystery of the East, I believe, is
really the mystery of dirt. Colombo and all the
East have much snake-life. The Bible was in-
spired from the East, and so they chose a cobra to
typify Satan. If the Bible were to be written in
England to-day the Evil One would be represented
as an American or a pro-Boer. ,
A steamer panted, along the coast on the smooth
surface of a wonderfully blue sea, as blue as the
indigo tub on washing-day, and many catamarans
bobbed into our ken. The catamaran is a herring-
gutted boat that yells its own antiquity—its
square sail depends from two masts stepped at
right angles. The boat is just wide enough to
allow thin-shanked Cingalese to sit in it. The
men pull with long sweeps which were probably
in use a thousand years before Belshazzar 's mother
put him into long trousers. On the spars that
connect the boat proper with the outrigger that
prevents capsize, sits a clouted coffee-colored
sailor, worming himself further outward as his
drawing sail tilts the catamaran to leeward —he
is the Labor Party of Indian Ocean-sailing, and
holds the balance of power.
As we came up to the breakwater and the squat
sea-front and red barrack-liketown, the catamarans
passed us oceanwards. I cannot forget the rapture
of that first knowledge of this new world ; in the
back of my brain there is a photograph of a
Cingalese throwing water on his square sail to
make it draw ; another of the crew sitting further
out on the outrigger as the wind filled the sail —
a splash of glossy brown on dead grey blue.
Going in we passed a husked cocoanut making
back to the shore on the inflowing tide. All the
allegories of Scripture have a literal and direct
application hidden somewhere in them. I wonder
if that cocoanut has any bearing on the injunction
to cast bread upon the waters ? The cocoanut is
bread in the East, anyway.
At four o’clock we rounded the breakwater and
found the country as it should be. The surprise is
that after seeing Kipling’s material, after seeing
the chances of him
“ Who held the gorgeous East in fee,*
that he did so little—not that he did so much. A
truer man with a more seeing eye and with less
than Kipling’s literary ability would have done so
much more. The atmosphere and the color should
make any newcomer imaginative enough to see
truly—only a born-tired man or a Chinaman could
escape it. The color is more the color of the
litterateur than of the artist.
If an artist were to paint these scenes honestly
he could produce nothing but apparently ex-
aggerated daubs—flaming as an advertisement for
varieties of aniline dyes. Nowhere is the color
touched on—it is plastered on by the pot-full.
There is a white steamer in a washing-day-blue
sea surrounded by copper-colored boys paddling
their plank craft—ready for threepence to dive to
the cave where the Kraken lives, and therefore
proportionally prepared to suicide for one-and-
ninepence. little language have they, but it is
various.
; ‘ Dibe - dibe - dibe - di be - dibe - alri - alri - tripens-
tripens-alri-la mer-la mer-la mer.”
And then they sing with all the pride of a great
singer in new music—“ Tara-ra-froom-dyah-
yah—tarara-6oom-dya.h-yah,” keeping tune with
their elbows on their ribs.
And then the dhoby-man arrives in his boat, and
the money-changer in his boat, and the seller of
oranges in his catamaran, and the glaring colors
of a transformation-scene come. And all of the
actors are thieves, from the jeweller who will sell
you a ring at an enormous sacrifice for 150 francs,
and two minutes later will offer you the twin of
it cheap for 30 francs; and the newspaper-seller,
who will try to sell you to-day’s paper, which is
really last year’s paper of a similar month and
date, at 50 per cent, over the published price;
and the money-changer, who gives the confiding
stranger 14 rupees 50 cents for a sovereign, thus
swindling that stranger of 1 rupee 50 cents. And,
Lord ! how hungrily he grabs the red gold. And
there is the dhoby-man, who is driven to the
depths of despair because he fails to get the
honorable washing of your dirty linen ; and the
oily nigger, who boards the ship with samples and
a tape-measure, and wants to measure you on the
spot for linen clothes ; and all the other thieves
who try to sell cocoanut curios and wooden ele-
phants, and model catamarans. Then the brown
ant-hills of the coal barges crawl up—the lumpers
naked but for the breech-clout—half-a-dozen of
them pulling on great sweeps with the blade of
the oar shaped like a short, dumpy shovel.
These sweeps are 20ft. long, and their shape must
mean a great loss of energy, but the nigger is
cheap and as plentiful as waratahs on Sydney
side, and so he is allowed to struggle out with his
loaded coal-barges because steamtugs cost money.
This is a picture some of our Tories would like to
see repeated in Australia.
The clothing evidently varies with the caste;
but you will often see a proud nigger, 6ft. high,
half-starved, and of cavernous belly, striding
along with a small afternoon-tea-table-cloth looped
from his left shoulder to his right thigh, leaving
his southerly elevation bare; and in the same
street a meek Uriah Heap sort of trader, as light
in color almost as a Chinese, clad in all the glories
of a surprise-packet.
Ashore the variety of color—the heaping of
pigment on pigment—is almost brutally sudden.
A red road, whose covering surface and impalpable
dust can only be made by the millionous tramping
of naked feet. I thought I had seen and eaten at
Broken Hill and in the West the finest dust
possible, but compared to this impalpable powder
Australian dust is merely a fine gravel. The
buildings are three or four storeyed, of stucco
or brick. The stucco is red or yellow —its hideous-
ness tempered by long weathering; all the win-
dows are masked with sepia’d wooden jalousies.
Cocoanut palms are everywhere—invading the
building spaces, closing in the distances, and
growing to the beach sands; the sea from here
is grey-blue, the sky ultramarine with a haze
under the skin ; there are palms of sage-green, of
lettuce-green, of spinach-green, and of greens that
are blues.
There are belts of cocoanut like those in the
convent garden at Townsville; jacarandas as in
the Supreme Court garden in Brisbane, but not
bo fine; and poncianas in bloom, like the pon-
ianaa at Harry Wade’s, on the Chillagoe-road ;
but here they are on a bigger, or rather an older
scale. And down the road to Cinnamon Gardens
the frangipanni blooms—volutes of scented ivory
on spades of magnolia green.
I hate to see a man wearing livery or doing the
work of an animal, but after some converse with
a rickshaw man I felt that ordinary standards did
not apply to him. So I packed my party in four
rickshaws and got myself into a fifth, and the
expedition started. Past the fort we went and
the Golden Gardens and the lighthouse, which is
built in the middle of a street, and out on to the
Galle Face Road, going by the sea fo>’ most of the
way. Then, turning at the Galle Face bungalows,
the rickshaw men trotted past the Cinnamon
Gardens —the night filled with frangipanni scent.
Further on, there were jacarandas and poncianas
and flame-trees—all scent and color; the night
air was cool, but almost as dense as water ; then
through groves of banyans threaded by hundreds
of fire-flies.
And, where the road was lighted, you saw the
sweat-glistering copper bodies of the rickshaw
men ; the red on the uniforms of Sikh soldiers ;
the flaring caps of Parsee merchants, and the bril-
liant turbans of Mohammedans.
We crossed the electric tram, which my nck-
shaw-man (my human horse) contemptuously
said, throwing the words over his shoulder as he
ran, was only for coolies—great sahibs like my-
self rode always in horse-carriages and rickshaws.
How much of his contempt for the electric tram
was real, and how much of it the self-interest of
the rickshaw-man I knew not—but it is quite
understandable that the tired white man, long
habituated to service by humans and the ex-
clusiveness of a rickshaw, should despise the
democratic tram. Over these perfect roads the
rickshaw ran silently—l could not hear even the
footfalls of my human horse. It was all Eastern,
quiet, suggestive and mysterious.
We passed long strings of two-wheeled waggons,
thatched over, as in the days Of Moses, and each
drawn by two small buffalo bulls, very little
bigger than Newfoundland dogs. These carry
enormous loads—tea and coffee from Kandy and
beyond to Colombo —and travel their twenty miles
a day. They are driven by green-hide bridles
passed through the nostrils —the driver adds him-
self to the load and crouches like a monkey on a
pole, his toes gripping the timber as a ’possum
grips a bough. There is not a pair of Australian
bullocks of three times the weight up to the pace,
even if they could endure the direct weight on
their necks.
Then came a great decorated litter on wheels —
it was almost the size of the coffee-wagons, and
had a thatched tilt of flat sedge. In it lay a
monstrously fat nigger of 20 stone, or thereabouts,
scarcely able to see for the bulbous fat around
his eyes ; his forehead hung over his brows like a
dew-lap. The enormous vehicle and its obese
lord were drawn at a gallop by a very tall and
horribly thin coolie —naked but for a breech-cloth,
a brown ghost, a half-carbonised skeleton, the
exaggeration of a photo, of an Indian famine.
The rickshaw-men trotted into the narrow
streets of the native quarter, and there we saw
many things. Bazaars, where the merchants sat
where the shop-windows ought to be, surrounded
by their wares. It is from the East that the
churches in tke white men’s countries brought the
idea of the bazaar—a place where all passers-by
were to be customers, the willing because of their
wants, the unwilling by the goading of whining
persuasiveness. The merchants sat, indifferent to
all hygienic considerations, in the midst of cocoa-
nuts and palm sweetmeats and areca ; the cross-
legged, half-naked clothier with his stock making
a glaring frame for his picture ; the private houses
where the family gathered round the communistic
pot, eating together, amiable even when groping
hand touched groping hand in the hot moisture of
the curry. And in the shops and in the roadways
were many girls—each of them clothed in a single
garment, very narrow and very long—folded to
display under one thickness of vesture the firm,
plump breasts, aggressive enough to strain the
texture of the thin material.
We had German lager in an old caravanserai
where the manager, a Cingalese in English clothes,
who spoke excellent baboo English, dissuaded me
from giving lager to the rickshaw-men, saying—
“Draught beer is exceedingly adequate for Hindu
blacks. ”
The narrow alley before the place where we sat
drinking was crowded until the street seemed as
confused as a mile of tangled string—by Mahom-
medans, Cingalese, Hindus, Eurasians, and all
the skin-colors earth has produced—rickshaws,
bullock-gharrys, tea - waggons, an infinity of
beggars, and very small landaus, almost toys,
drawn by two toy buffalo bulls. A dozen small
naked beggars—the color of newly polished tan-
boots —and of from two to five years of age, whined
for two annas, and sprawled under my honorable
feet as I returned to the rickshaw —and then came
a wondrous religious procession with its small and
ridiculous god under a thatched awning where
rain might not hurt his Omnipotence. He brought
with him a din of wooden drums, and cymbals,
and a queer three-stringed violin of wood and
metal, making a pleasant tumult, such as the
worshippers’ ancestors had made many a thousand
years agone. But the superior Cingalese keeper
of the shypoo shop said to me in apologetic pity :
“Such a silly procession to high degrees. These
poor black people are not Christians like us.”
He looked so pious that I wanted to kick him,
but I compromised by saying, “ I am not a
Christian,” and by paying him two rupees less
than he asked for the beer. I don’t think his
Christianity was the same after the deduction.
We rode or were drawn—can’t say “driven,”
because the human horse is his own reins—past
the Roman Catholic cathedral of the same diseased
kind of stucco, and plunged into another portion
of the native quarter, stopping before a Buddhist
temple. The swarms of loafers on the Roman
Catholic cathedral steps were like ants in a col-
lapsed ant-hill, except that they had no ant
activity and appeared to be dead. On the ap-
proaches to the Buddhist temple there were not so
many, either because they respected the Buddhist
temple or because the attendants had no respect
for them. The temple was filled with garish
representations of gods ; at the portals were great
pots of water, in which the worshippers pre-
tended to immerse their feet before entering.
“Forty miles a day,” say the rickshaw-men, is
the average of travel—they say they can do forty
miles in six hours. They spoiled us of an extra
rupee or so, and the parti-colored boatmen rowed
us back to the ship just before midnight.
But there was no sleep for me—the horde of
coolies were still coaling—the electric lights of the
ship and the tropical moonlight found new tints
and shades for the glaring palette called Colombo.
The number of catamarans and row-boats round
the ship had increased to legion, and the air was
filled with the brown man’s equivalent for curses
at the momentarily-recurring collisions. And
while standing on the accommodation-ladder of
the ship I had the greatest disappointment I have
ever felt since the grief of seeing the Australian
shore dip over the eastern horizon.

Glad tidings indeed they have proved to
thousands. Bile Beans have come like a ray

ousting Sickness and Misery and introducing

Farm, Brisbane, Q., when he was
recommended by a friend to try
Bile Beans for Biliousness, as will be
admitted on perusal of his statement,
which reads: —“I had been troubled by
severe attacks of indigestion for two years.
After each meal I suffered exceedingly.
This inconvenienced me in my work to
a great extent, causing me in many in-
stances to put aside work I should have
done. I had tried many medicines with-
out obtaining any relief. After two years
of misery I was advised by a friend to try
Bile Beans, as he could speak from ex-
perience as to their efficacy in such cases

thoroughly cured. Since that time I have
felt none of the old symptoms return, and

festion accompanied by severe headaches,
ut since taking the beans she fully
endorses my approval of their great worth
in such complaints. Mrs. Foster is now
free from the excruciating headaches which
caused her so much misery and inconveni-
ence in her household duties and I may
safely say that Bile Beans have brought
happiness to a home where sickness had
formerly brought misery. I am an
engineer by profession, and have been
employed during the. past seven years at
Dixon’s tobacco factory.”
