The bowmen of the crews of two colliding boats
caught each other by the throats, after a short
argument, not in any words of any language, but
simply in dog-snarls. They do not fight with
fists—each man fastens to his opponent’s windpipe
and tries to roll overboard and drown him. I
should have liked to see them fighting in the
water, and the electric light would have shown it
with beautiful distinctness, but a serang picked
up a boat-hook and beat the combatants’ heads
with a wonderfully accurate impartiality. But
they were game men and even with their heads
bloody had to be torn off each other.
A few scenes like this explain that paragraph
in. the Governor’s last speech to the Ceylon Legis-
lative Council in which he “ regrets that assaults
with the knife are so common.” These assaults
the Baboo reporters of the Ceylon press call
“causing hurt.”
The third-class of this mail-boat is not cheering
to regard, but all my pity was given to four of my
country-men—to four Australian bullocks, so
tamed with heat and despair that they allowed me
to pat them—poor wretches. In the next pen
were six full-woolled sheep, gasping in the thick
air. Why don’t they carry shorn sheep for kill-
ing, and why haven’t they a big modern refrige-
rator, so that the meat could be shipped dead—
instead of dooming it to a hell of pain—which,
like most hells, is unnecessary ?
In the coops, there were hundreds of ducks and
geese—and, worst of all, hundreds of pigeons,
crowded one on the other, and doomed so to stay
through all the Indian Ocean and Red Sea heat;
that is, all unlucky enough to escape the knife of
the butcher in these first days.
Now pigeons were made to fly about, and pair,
and fight for the hen, and flash iris in the sun-
rays, and so justify their existence. And the
killing of pigeons and quail and larks, and such
things, for food should be unnecessary in a world
of strong men, and is only another proof that the
men of old civilisation are becoming ladylike.
Bread, beef and beer for a man, and whisky and
pipes—and, for a change, garlic and chablis.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Our Bards.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Since then critics seem to have tired of this simple
verse, and newcomers have been received with more or
less sarcasm.— A. W. Jose in Literature (London).
So the critics tire of the simple verse that comes
from our poets’ pens,
And scoff, with a tall, bell-toppered scoff, when
they glance at the specimens.
Well, our bards write of the life they know —pray
what should they write beside ?
We want to hear of the swagman’s tramp and the
reckless bushman’s ride;
And how should they write of the deeds of Bill
and the doings of Jim and Jack ?
Should the words of Shakespeare run through the
yarns and the tales that are told Out-Back ?
Should brumbies be chased with a Browning poem
or caught with some Cowper verse ?
And how would the language of Byron look sur-
rounding a Mulga curse ?
Or picture Bob on a hairy nag with about eleven
stone up,
And with Greek hexameters in his mouth, a-win-
ning the Bullhide Cup !
Should Greenhide Billy and Queensland Joe have
a spar to a Campbell ode ?
And think of a poem by Tennyson describing a
feed-bare road !
Just fancy the swagmen gathered round a travel-
ler’s fire at eve,
When Bill starts out to recite the tale of “ Par-
don, the Son of Reprieve ”
But is blocked by Jim: “Enough of that; we
want something tone.y,” says he,
And he gives them “Eugene Aram’s Dream,”
which is classical, don’t you see !
But they only grunt at the polished lines—they
haven’t the same old ring ;
And the high-class songs we can’t enjoy like those
that the drovers sing.
Our poets write of the lives they know, and what
should they write beside ?
And wasn’t it grand how Ben Duggan rode, and
how bravely poor Gilbert died ?
Just think of the “ Banjo,” our rider-bard, whose
verses we love to read,
Till we almost fancy our worn old crocks belong
to a fancy breed ;
And when we’re reading his ringing lines and his
hero is galloping,
We seem to battle it out with him—we can hear
the hoof-beats ring.
We have followed our Lawson on lonely tramps
o’er plains that are hot and wide,
And through his verses as though in life we have
tramped with him side by side.
We’ll stick to him now as in days gone by, he has
friends still in Bill and Jim,
And I’m proud to consider myself as one of the
chaps who have “ barracked ” for him :
Though critics tire of the simple verse and scoff on
the British shore,
They’re tired of the lines our bush-bards write,
and they want these bards no more.
They want a Bill with bright tan boots, and a Jim
with a tall top hat,
And his missus in jewels and evening dress in the
humpy on Mulga Flat.
We’re to put green grass on the howling plains
and flowers on the Mount Browne track,
And Bob will henceforth be the Earl of Bob, and
Jack will be Baron Fitzjack !
And they’ll casually meet the Duke de Jim at the
opera at Broken Hill,
And there will be peace with the critics who say
they are tired of our plain old Bill.
N.S.W.
RIVERNA.
Anglesey, the b.ig island off the coast of
Wales, has just decided, by its County Council,
that all public notices and bye-laws must be
published in Welsh, as the farmers do not know
enough English to understand them otherwise.
The suppression of the insurgent Welshman who
refuses to be Anglicised may yet furnish a light
job for Ketchener.
As Told by the Dog.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was me that spoke (said a dog standing
close by). Yes, it is funny to hear a dog
talk. I never talk to anyone, only swag-
men. I’ll tell you a yarn if you like.
He’s a swell sort of chap that you spoke
to, isn’t he ? He’s my master. He used to
be pretty conceited one time —before he got
married, I mean. His wife don’t live with him.
How’s that ? you ask. I’ll tell you.
When my boss came here there were two girls
on this station —house and parlormaid, Florrie
and Kitty Lawley : they were twins. They
dressed so alike you could not tell one from the
other, especially at night, and wearing the
uniform dress servants wear.
One night, about a month after my boss came
here, he was in the room before the looking-glass.
He took some stuff from a bottle, rubbed it on his
top lip and tried to catch hold of the hair, but
there wasn’t any there; then he muttered to
himself, “Hanged if I know which of them girls
I like best; they’re both gone on me, I know.
Blest if I can tell one from the other sometimes.
However, I’ll wait a bit; one of them is bound to
ask me to marry her before long.”
I didn’t say anything, I only smiled and walked
away. I met Lassie outside. Lassie’s Harry
Holmes’s dog. He is head stockman. “ I say,”
said Lassie to me, “Harry’s funny lately—gone
on one of the girls, I think—always talking about
Florrie.”
Shortly after this my boss made up his mind
about these girls. Everyone had got a ticket in
Tattersall’s sweep, and one day there was great
excitement, for Kitty had won £2OOO. Fred made
up his mind that he liked her best, and that he
would propose to her first chance. Kitty liked
Fred all right, but she liked Harry Holmes better.
But Harry was a coward when it came to love-
making, although he was frightened of nothing
otherwise. Florrie liked Fred because he was a
swell from ’Ome, and she wanted to be a lady.
A fortnight after, one night while outside in the
kitchen, I heard voices at one end of the building.
I knew the voices. It was Harry Holmes and
Florrie. “ Now look here, Flo,” said Harry, “I
know you are in love with Fred, and you know
that lam fond of your sister. He’s such a stupid,
silly ass, that —No, I beg pardon, but you know
he doesn’t know which of you he does flirt with.
Would you marry him if he asked you, Flo ?”
“What right have you to —I wish you would
mind your own business, Harry Holmes,” stam-
mered Florrie ; “ you are”
“Yes, but darn it, Florrie ; you don’t think—
Fred is sure to propose to Kitty now she has got
plenty of money. Be sensible, and listen ; I have
a plan that will injure no one, and put everything
right. When we go fishing next Sunday we will
walk instead of riding. You just watcli me, and
every time I speak to you I will call you Kitty in
Fred’s hearing, and, so that your sister won’t
notice it when she is in hearing, I will call you
Miss Lawley, and the same to her. When we are
coming home—Fred is sure to want to come home
with you, thinking you’re Kitty—you can do the
rest, Florrie. You won’t want to tell any lies.
You know what I mean.”
Florrie did know. “ Well, Harry, I think it
is a shame that I did not win the money, and
then—mind you, I’m not going to deceive him,
Harry, but—but I do think people should marry
the persons they want to.”
Sunday afternoon passed away pleasantly. It
got time to come home.
Harry Holmes and Kitty—the proper Kitty—
were together. Florrie and Fred were standing a
little distance away. “Would you mind going
along for the fish-basket, Kitty, while I fix my
rod,” said Harry. Kitty went away.
Harry then turned to the other who was real!} 7
Florrie, and said, “ Now, then, Kitty, which of
us is going to see you home ? ”
“Oh, that’s all right, Harry,” said Fred, “I
will take care of Kitty, won’t I?” turning to
Florrie, whom he thought was Kitty.
“Oh, well, just my luck,” said Harry, sulky-
like. My word !he was cunning.
Kitty and Fred went —no, I’m wrong. Fred
and Florrie, that was supposed to be Kitty,
walked on in silence for some time. Florrie spoke
in a sad sort of way, “ I don’t think we will have
many more Sundays together, Fred.”
“ No,” said he. “ How is that ? Have I
offended you ? I hope ”
“ Oh, no; but I will be going away soon, I
think.”
“Going away? Why, where—what has hap-
pened ? Oh, Miss Lawley, don’t go away; stay
here and be my wife ; you know I have always
loved you. I will try to make you happy. Do
not ”
“ But, Fred, you are making a mistake, I am
not ”
“ Oh, yes, you are worthy to be my wife, my
darling. Say yes.”
“Please let me go, Fred. I’ll tell” (kiss).
“Oh, Fred, do”—— (another kiss).
Florrie tried to explain, but she hadn’t room to
speak. I never saw two people so silly. So I
came away and left them.
Lassie told me after about Harry Holmes and
the proper Kitty. They were just as silly as the
other two.
Well, there was a double wedding a fortnight
later. Everything passed off pleasantly. Harry
got married to his girl, then Fred to his ; then
there were some papers to sign.
Suddenly I noticed my boss get flurried-like,
look hard at the paper, then at his newly-made
wife. “My dear,” he said, “you have—have
made a mistake. ”
“Have I, Fred ; how?”
“ You have signed yourself Florrie, darling.”
“Yes, Fred, I am Florrie, I tried to tell you,
you—.”
“You Florrie,” almost yelled my boss, “and
you haven’t got the money ? ”
“ No, Fred, I—l —.”
A glassy sort of stare came into my boss’s eyes.
“We are married,” he muttered. “We are
married. ”
And she led him gently from the church.
They didn’t live long together. Florrie’s a bar-
maid in Sydney now.
They say my master’s a woman-hater. I believe
he is.
I never see him putting stuff on his top lip now.
N.S.W.
WANDERER.
The British Government makes a slight
increase in the pay of its Tommy Atkins and
issues an order that soldiers may wear spectacles
so that men with defect ve sight will not necess-
arily be barred in future. But still recruits are
scarce, and conscription mu3t come sooner or
later.
